The power of thence departing took from all.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-58">A painted people there below we found,
Who went about with footsteps very slow,
Weeping and in their semblance tired and vanquished.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-61">They had on mantles with the hoods low down
Before their eyes, and fashioned of the cut
That in Cologne they for the monks are made.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-64">Without, they gilded are so that it dazzles;
But inwardly all leaden and so heavy
That Frederick used to put them on of straw.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-67">O everlastingly fatiguing mantle!
Again we turned us, still to the left hand
Along with them, intent on their sad plaint;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-70">But owing to the weight, that weary folk
Came on so tardily, that we were new
In company at each motion of the haunch.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-73">Whence I unto my Leader: "See thou find
Some one who may by deed or name be known,
And thus in going move thine eye about."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-76">And one, who understood the Tuscan speech,
Cried to us from behind: "Stay ye your feet,
Ye, who so run athwart the dusky air!
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-79">Perhaps thou'lt have from me what thou demandest."
Whereat the Leader turned him, and said: "Wait,
And then according to his pace proceed."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-82">I stopped, and two beheld I show great haste
Of spirit, in their faces, to be with me;
But the burden and the narrow way delayed them.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-85">When they came up, long with an eye askance
They scanned me without uttering a word.
Then to each other turned, and said together:
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-88">"He by the action of his throat seems living;
And if they dead are, by what privilege
Go they uncovered by the heavy stole?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-91">Then said to me: "Tuscan, who to the college
Of miserable hypocrites art come,
Do not disdain to tell us who thou art."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-94">And I to them: "Born was I, and grew up
In the great town on the fair river of Arno,
And with the body am I've always had.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-97">But who are ye, in whom there trickles down
Along your cheeks such grief as I behold?
And what pain is upon you, that so sparkles?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-100">And one replied to me: "These orange cloaks
Are made of lead so heavy, that the weights
Cause in this way their balances to creak.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-103">Frati Gaudenti were we, and Bolognese;
I Catalano, and he Loderingo
Named, and together taken by thy city,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-106">As the wont is to take one man alone,
For maintenance of its peace; and we were such
That still it is apparent round Gardingo."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-109">"O Friars," began I, "your iniquitous. . ."
But said no more; for to mine eyes there rushed
One crucified with three stakes on the ground.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-112">When me he saw, he writhed himself all over,
Blowing into his beard with suspirations;
And the Friar Catalan, who noticed this,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-115">Said to me: "This transfixed one, whom thou seest,
Counselled the Pharisees that it was meet
To put one man to torture for the people.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-118">Crosswise and naked is he on the path,
As thou perceivest; and he needs must feel,
Whoever passes, first how much he weighs;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-121">And in like mode his father-in-law is punished
Within this moat, and the others of the council,
Which for the Jews was a malignant seed."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-124">And thereupon I saw Virgilius marvel
O'er him who was extended on the cross
So vilely in eternal banishment.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-127">Then he directed to the Friar this voice:
"Be not displeased, if granted thee, to tell us
If to the right hand any pass slope down
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-130">By which we two may issue forth from here,
Without constraining some of the black angels
To come and extricate us from this deep."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-133">Then he made answer: "Nearer than thou hopest
There is a rock, that forth from the great circle
Proceeds, and crosses all the cruel valleys,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-136">Save that at this 'tis broken, and does not bridge it;
You will be able to mount up the ruin,
That sidelong slopes and at the bottom rises."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-139">The Leader stood awhile with head bowed down;
Then said: "The business badly he recounted
Who grapples with his hook the sinners yonder."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-142">And the Friar: "Many of the Devil's vices
Once heard I at Bologna, and among them,
That he's a liar and the father of lies."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-145">Thereat my Leader with great strides went on,
Somewhat disturbed with anger in his looks;
Whence from the heavy-laden I departed
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiii-148">After the prints of his beloved feet.
</p>

## Inferno - Canto XXIV

<p id="inferno-xxiv-1">In that part of the youthful year wherein
The Sun his locks beneath Aquarius tempers,
And now the nights draw near to half the day,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-4">What time the hoar-frost copies on the ground
The outward semblance of her sister white,
But little lasts the temper of her pen,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-7">The husbandman, whose forage faileth him,
Rises, and looks, and seeth the champaign
All gleaming white, whereat he beats his flank,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-10">Returns in doors, and up and down laments,
Like a poor wretch, who knows not what to do;
Then he returns and hope revives again,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-13">Seeing the world has changed its countenance
In little time, and takes his shepherd's crook,
And forth the little lambs to pasture drives.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-16">Thus did the Master fill me with alarm,
When I beheld his forehead so disturbed,
And to the ailment came as soon the plaster.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-19">For as we came unto the ruined bridge,
The Leader turned to me with that sweet look
Which at the mountain's foot I first beheld.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-22">His arms he opened, after some advisement
Within himself elected, looking first
Well at the ruin, and laid hold of me.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-25">And even as he who acts and meditates,
For aye it seems that he provides beforehand,
So upward lifting me towards the summit
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-28">Of a huge rock, he scanned another crag,
Saying: "To that one grapple afterwards,
But try first if 'tis such that it will hold thee."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-31">This was no way for one clothed with a cloak;
For hardly we, he light, and I pushed upward,
Were able to ascend from jag to jag.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-34">And had it not been, that upon that precinct
Shorter was the ascent than on the other,
He I know not, but I had been dead beat.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-37">But because Malebolge tow'rds the mouth
Of the profoundest well is all inclining,
The structure of each valley doth import
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-40">That one bank rises and the other sinks.
Still we arrived at length upon the point
Wherefrom the last stone breaks itself asunder.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-43">The breath was from my lungs so milked away,
When I was up, that I could go no farther,
Nay, I sat down upon my first arrival.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-46">"Now it behoves thee thus to put off sloth,"
My Master said; "for sitting upon down,
Or under quilt, one cometh not to fame,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-49">Withouten which whoso his life consumes
Such vestige leaveth of himself on earth,
As smoke in air or in the water foam.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-52">And therefore raise thee up, o'ercome the anguish
With spirit that o'ercometh every battle,
If with its heavy body it sink not.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-55">A longer stairway it behoves thee mount;
'Tis not enough from these to have departed;
Let it avail thee, if thou understand me."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-58">Then I uprose, showing myself provided
Better with breath than I did feel myself,
And said: "Go on, for I am strong and bold."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-61">Upward we took our way along the crag,
Which jagged was, and narrow, and difficult,
And more precipitous far than that before.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-64">Speaking I went, not to appear exhausted;
Whereat a voice from the next moat came forth,
Not well adapted to articulate words.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-67">I know not what it said, though o'er the back
I now was of the arch that passes there;
But he seemed moved to anger who was speaking.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-70">I was bent downward, but my living eyes
Could not attain the bottom, for the dark;
Wherefore I: "Master, see that thou arrive
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-73">At the next round, and let us descend the wall;
For as from hence I hear and understand not,
So I look down and nothing I distinguish."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-76">"Other response," he said, "I make thee not,
Except the doing; for the modest asking
Ought to be followed by the deed in silence."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-79">We from the bridge descended at its head,
Where it connects itself with the eighth bank,
And then was manifest to me the Bolgia;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-82">And I beheld therein a terrible throng
Of serpents, and of such a monstrous kind,
That the remembrance still congeals my blood
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-85">Let Libya boast no longer with her sand;
For if Chelydri, Jaculi, and Phareae
She breeds, with Cenchri and with Amphisbaena,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-88">Neither so many plagues nor so malignant
E'er showed she with all Ethiopia,
Nor with whatever on the Red Sea is!
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-91">Among this cruel and most dismal throng
People were running naked and affrighted.
Without the hope of hole or heliotrope.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-94">They had their hands with serpents bound behind them;
These riveted upon their reins the tail
And head, and were in front of them entwined.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-97">And lo! at one who was upon our side
There darted forth a serpent, which transfixed him
There where the neck is knotted to the shoulders.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-100">Nor 'O' so quickly e'er, nor 'I' was written,
As he took fire, and burned; and ashes wholly
Behoved it that in falling he became.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-103">And when he on the ground was thus destroyed,
The ashes drew together, and of themselves
Into himself they instantly returned.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-106">Even thus by the great sages 'tis confessed
The phoenix dies, and then is born again,
When it approaches its five-hundredth year;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-109">On herb or grain it feeds not in its life,
But only on tears of incense and amomum,
And nard and myrrh are its last winding-sheet.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-112">And as he is who falls, and knows not how,
By force of demons who to earth down drag him,
Or other oppilation that binds man,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-115">When he arises and around him looks,
Wholly bewildered by the mighty anguish
Which he has suffered, and in looking sighs;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-118">Such was that sinner after he had risen.
Justice of God! O how severe it is,
That blows like these in vengeance poureth down!
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-121">The Guide thereafter asked him who he was;
Whence he replied: "I rained from Tuscany
A short time since into this cruel gorge.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-124">A bestial life, and not a human, pleased me,
Even as the mule I was; I'm Vanni Fucci,
Beast, and Pistoia was my worthy den."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-127">And I unto the Guide: "Tell him to stir not,
And ask what crime has thrust him here below,
For once a man of blood and wrath I saw him."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-130">And the sinner, who had heard, dissembled not,
But unto me directed mind and face,
And with a melancholy shame was painted.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-133">Then said: "It pains me more that thou hast caught me
Amid this misery where thou seest me,
Than when I from the other life was taken.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-136">What thou demandest I cannot deny;
So low am I put down because I robbed
The sacristy of the fair ornaments,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-139">And falsely once 'twas laid upon another;
But that thou mayst not such a sight enjoy,
If thou shalt e'er be out of the dark places,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-142">Thine ears to my announcement ope and hear:
Pistoia first of Neri groweth meagre;
Then Florence doth renew her men and manners;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-145">Mars draws a vapour up from Val di Magra,
Which is with turbid clouds enveloped round,
And with impetuous and bitter tempest
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-148">Over Campo Picen shall be the battle;
When it shall suddenly rend the mist asunder,
So that each Bianco shall thereby be smitten.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxiv-151">And this I've said that it may give thee pain."
</p>

## Inferno - Canto XXV

<p id="inferno-xxv-1">At the conclusion of his words, the thief
Lifted his hands aloft with both the figs,
Crying: "Take that, God, for at thee I aim them."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-4">From that time forth the serpents were my friends;
For one entwined itself about his neck
As if it said: "I will not thou speak more;"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-7">And round his arms another, and rebound him,
Clinching itself together so in front,
That with them he could not a motion make.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-10">Pistoia, ah, Pistoia! why resolve not
To burn thyself to ashes and so perish,
Since in ill-doing thou thy seed excellest?
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-13">Through all the sombre circles of this Hell,
Spirit I saw not against God so proud,
Not he who fell at Thebes down from the walls!
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-16">He fled away, and spake no further word;
And I beheld a Centaur full of rage
Come crying out: "Where is, where is the scoffer?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-19">I do not think Maremma has so many
Serpents as he had all along his back,
As far as where our countenance begins.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-22">Upon the shoulders, just behind the nape,
With wings wide open was a dragon lying,
And he sets fire to all that he encounters.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-25">My Master said: "That one is Cacus, who
Beneath the rock upon Mount Aventine
Created oftentimes a lake of blood.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-28">He goes not on the same road with his brothers,
By reason of the fraudulent theft he made
Of the great herd, which he had near to him;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-31">Whereat his tortuous actions ceased beneath
The mace of Hercules, who peradventure
Gave him a hundred, and he felt not ten."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-34">While he was speaking thus, he had passed by,
And spirits three had underneath us come,
Of which nor I aware was, nor my Leader,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-37">Until what time they shouted: "Who are you?"
On which account our story made a halt,
And then we were intent on them alone.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-40">I did not know them; but it came to pass,
As it is wont to happen by some chance,
That one to name the other was compelled,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-43">Exclaiming: "Where can Cianfa have remained?"
Whence I, so that the Leader might attend,
Upward from chin to nose my finger laid.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-46">If thou art, Reader, slow now to believe
What I shall say, it will no marvel be,
For I who saw it hardly can admit it.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-49">As I was holding raised on them my brows,
Behold! a serpent with six feet darts forth
In front of one, and fastens wholly on him.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-52">With middle feet it bound him round the paunch,
And with the forward ones his arms it seized;
Then thrust its teeth through one cheek and the other;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-55">The hindermost it stretched upon his thighs,
And put its tail through in between the two,
And up behind along the reins outspread it.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-58">Ivy was never fastened by its barbs
Unto a tree so, as this horrible reptile
Upon the other's limbs entwined its own.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-61">Then they stuck close, as if of heated wax
They had been made, and intermixed their colour;
Nor one nor other seemed now what he was;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-64">E'en as proceedeth on before the flame
Upward along the paper a brown colour,
Which is not black as yet, and the white dies.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-67">The other two looked on, and each of them
Cried out: "O me, Agnello, how thou changest!
Behold, thou now art neither two nor one."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-70">Already the two heads had one become,
When there appeared to us two figures mingled
Into one face, wherein the two were lost.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-73">Of the four lists were fashioned the two arms,
The thighs and legs, the belly and the chest
Members became that never yet were seen.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-76">Every original aspect there was cancelled;
Two and yet none did the perverted image
Appear, and such departed with slow pace.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-79">Even as a lizard, under the great scourge
Of days canicular, exchanging hedge,
Lightning appeareth if the road it cross;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-82">Thus did appear, coming towards the bellies
Of the two others, a small fiery serpent,
Livid and black as is a peppercorn.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-85">And in that part whereat is first received
Our aliment, it one of them transfixed;
Then downward fell in front of him extended.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-88">The one transfixed looked at it, but said naught;
Nay, rather with feet motionless he yawned,
Just as if sleep or fever had assailed him.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-91">He at the serpent gazed, and it at him;
One through the wound, the other through the mouth
Smoked violently, and the smoke commingled.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-94">Henceforth be silent Lucan, where he mentions
Wretched Sabellus and Nassidius,
And wait to hear what now shall be shot forth.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-97">Be silent Ovid, of Cadmus and Arethusa;
For if him to a snake, her to fountain,
Converts he fabling, that I grudge him not;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-100">Because two natures never front to front
Has he transmuted, so that both the forms
To interchange their matter ready were.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-103">Together they responded in such wise,
That to a fork the serpent cleft his tail,
And eke the wounded drew his feet together.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-106">The legs together with the thighs themselves
Adhered so, that in little time the juncture
No sign whatever made that was apparent.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-109">He with the cloven tail assumed the figure
The other one was losing, and his skin
Became elastic, and the other's hard.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-112">I saw the arms draw inward at the armpits,
And both feet of the reptile, that were short,
Lengthen as much as those contracted were.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-115">Thereafter the hind feet, together twisted,
Became the member that a man conceals,
And of his own the wretch had two created.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-118">While both of them the exhalation veils
With a new colour, and engenders hair
On one of them and depilates the other,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-121">The one uprose and down the other fell,
Though turning not away their impious lamps,
Underneath which each one his muzzle changed.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-124">He who was standing drew it tow'rds the temples,
And from excess of matter, which came thither,
Issued the ears from out the hollow cheeks;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-127">What did not backward run and was retained
Of that excess made to the face a nose,
And the lips thickened far as was befitting.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-130">He who lay prostrate thrusts his muzzle forward,
And backward draws the ears into his head,
In the same manner as the snail its horns;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-133">And so the tongue, which was entire and apt
For speech before, is cleft, and the bi-forked
In the other closes up, and the smoke ceases.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-136">The soul, which to a reptile had been changed,
Along the valley hissing takes to flight,
And after him the other speaking sputters.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-139">Then did he turn upon him his new shoulders,
And said to the other: "I'll have Buoso run,
Crawling as I have done, along this road."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-142">In this way I beheld the seventh ballast
Shift and reshift, and here be my excuse
The novelty, if aught my pen transgress.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-145">And notwithstanding that mine eyes might be
Somewhat bewildered, and my mind dismayed,
They could not flee away so secretly
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-148">But that I plainly saw Puccio Sciancato;
And he it was who sole of three companions,
Which came in the beginning, was not changed;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxv-151">The other was he whom thou, Gaville, weepest.
</p>

## Inferno - Canto XXVI

<p id="inferno-xxvi-1">Rejoice, O Florence, since thou art so great,
That over sea and land thou beatest thy wings,
And throughout Hell thy name is spread abroad!
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-4">Among the thieves five citizens of thine
Like these I found, whence shame comes unto me,
And thou thereby to no great honour risest.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-7">But if when morn is near our dreams are true,
Feel shalt thou in a little time from now
What Prato, if none other, craves for thee.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-10">And if it now were, it were not too soon;
Would that it were, seeing it needs must be,
For 'twill aggrieve me more the more I age.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-13">We went our way, and up along the stairs
The bourns had made us to descend before,
Remounted my Conductor and drew me.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-16">And following the solitary path
Among the rocks and ridges of the crag,
The foot without the hand sped not at all.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-19">Then sorrowed I, and sorrow now again,
When I direct my mind to what I saw,
And more my genius curb than I am wont,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-22">That it may run not unless virtue guide it;
So that if some good star, or better thing,
Have given me good, I may myself not grudge it.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-25">As many as the hind (who on the hill
Rests at the time when he who lights the world
His countenance keeps least concealed from us,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-28">While as the fly gives place unto the gnat)
Seeth the glow-worms down along the valley,
Perchance there where he ploughs and makes his vintage;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-31">With flames as manifold resplendent all
Was the eighth Bolgia, as I grew aware
As soon as I was where the depth appeared.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-34">And such as he who with the bears avenged him
Beheld Elijah's chariot at departing,
What time the steeds to heaven erect uprose,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-37">For with his eye he could not follow it
So as to see aught else than flame alone,
Even as a little cloud ascending upward,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-40">Thus each along the gorge of the intrenchment
Was moving; for not one reveals the theft,
And every flame a sinner steals away.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-43">I stood upon the bridge uprisen to see,
So that, if I had seized not on a rock,
Down had I fallen without being pushed.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-46">And the Leader, who beheld me so attent,
Exclaimed: "Within the fires the spirits are;
Each swathes himself with that wherewith he burns."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-49">"My Master," I replied, "by hearing thee
I am more sure; but I surmised already
It might be so, and already wished to ask thee
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-52">Who is within that fire, which comes so cleft
At top, it seems uprising from the pyre
Where was Eteocles with his brother placed."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-55">He answered me: "Within there are tormented
Ulysses and Diomed, and thus together
They unto vengeance run as unto wrath.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-58">And there within their flame do they lament
The ambush of the horse, which made the door
Whence issued forth the Romans' gentle seed;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-61">Therein is wept the craft, for which being dead
Deidamia still deplores Achilles,
And pain for the Palladium there is borne."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-64">"If they within those sparks possess the power
To speak," I said, "thee, Master, much I pray,
And re-pray, that the prayer be worth a thousand,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-67">That thou make no denial of awaiting
Until the horned flame shall hither come;
Thou seest that with desire I lean towards it."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-70">And he to me: "Worthy is thy entreaty
Of much applause, and therefore I accept it;
But take heed that thy tongue restrain itself.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-73">Leave me to speak, because I have conceived
That which thou wishest; for they might disdain
Perchance, since they were Greeks, discourse of thine."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-76">When now the flame had come unto that point,
Where to my Leader it seemed time and place,
After this fashion did I hear him speak:
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-79">"O ye, who are twofold within one fire,
If I deserved of you, while I was living,
If I deserved of you or much or little
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-82">When in the world I wrote the lofty verses,
Do not move on, but one of you declare
Whither, being lost, he went away to die."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-85">Then of the antique flame the greater horn,
Murmuring, began to wave itself about
Even as a flame doth which the wind fatigues.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-88">Thereafterward, the summit to and fro
Moving as if it were the tongue that spake,
It uttered forth a voice, and said: "When I
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-91">From Circe had departed, who concealed me
More than a year there near unto Gaeta,
Or ever yet Aeneas named it so,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-94">Nor fondness for my son, nor reverence
For my old father, nor the due affection
Which joyous should have made Penelope,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-97">Could overcome within me the desire
I had to be experienced of the world,
And of the vice and virtue of mankind;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-100">But I put forth on the high open sea
With one sole ship, and that small company
By which I never had deserted been.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-103">Both of the shores I saw as far as Spain,
Far as Morocco, and the isle of Sardes,
And the others which that sea bathes round about.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-106">I and my company were old and slow
When at that narrow passage we arrived
Where Hercules his landmarks set as signals,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-109">That man no farther onward should adventure.
On the right hand behind me left I Seville,
And on the other already had left Ceuta.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-112">'O brothers, who amid a hundred thousand
Perils,' I said, 'have come unto the West,
To this so inconsiderable vigil
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-115">Which is remaining of your senses still
Be ye unwilling to deny the knowledge,
Following the sun, of the unpeopled world.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-118">Consider ye the seed from which ye sprang;
Ye were not made to live like unto brutes,
But for pursuit of virtue and of knowledge.'
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-121">So eager did I render my companions,
With this brief exhortation, for the voyage,
That then I hardly could have held them back.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-124">And having turned our stern unto the morning,
We of the oars made wings for our mad flight,
Evermore gaining on the larboard side.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-127">Already all the stars of the other pole
The night beheld, and ours so very low
It did not rise above the ocean floor.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-130">Five times rekindled and as many quenched
Had been the splendour underneath the moon,
Since we had entered into the deep pass,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-133">When there appeared to us a mountain, dim
From distance, and it seemed to me so high
As I had never any one beheld.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-136">Joyful were we, and soon it turned to weeping;
For out of the new land a whirlwind rose,
And smote upon the fore part of the ship.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-139">Three times it made her whirl with all the waters,
At the fourth time it made the stern uplift,
And the prow downward go, as pleased Another,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvi-142">Until the sea above us closed again."
</p>

## Inferno - Canto XXVII

<p id="inferno-xxvii-1">Already was the flame erect and quiet,
To speak no more, and now departed from us
With the permission of the gentle Poet;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-4">When yet another, which behind it came,
Caused us to turn our eyes upon its top
By a confused sound that issued from it.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-7">As the Sicilian bull (that bellowed first
With the lament of him, and that was right,
Who with his file had modulated it)
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-10">Bellowed so with the voice of the afflicted,
That, notwithstanding it was made of brass,
Still it appeared with agony transfixed;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-13">Thus, by not having any way or issue
At first from out the fire, to its own language
Converted were the melancholy words.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-16">But afterwards, when they had gathered way
Up through the point, giving it that vibration
The tongue had given them in their passage out,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-19">We heard it said: "O thou, at whom I aim
My voice, and who but now wast speaking Lombard,
Saying, 'Now go thy way, no more I urge thee,'
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-22">Because I come perchance a little late,
To stay and speak with me let it not irk thee;
Thou seest it irks not me, and I am burning.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-25">If thou but lately into this blind world
Hast fallen down from that sweet Latian land,
Wherefrom I bring the whole of my transgression,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-28">Say, if the Romagnuols have peace or war,
For I was from the mountains there between
Urbino and the yoke whence Tiber bursts."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-31">I still was downward bent and listening,
When my Conductor touched me on the side,
Saying: "Speak thou: this one a Latian is."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-34">And I, who had beforehand my reply
In readiness, forthwith began to speak:
"O soul, that down below there art concealed,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-37">Romagna thine is not and never has been
Without war in the bosom of its tyrants;
But open war I none have left there now.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-40">Ravenna stands as it long years has stood;
The Eagle of Polenta there is brooding,
So that she covers Cervia with her vans.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-43">The city which once made the long resistance,
And of the French a sanguinary heap,
Beneath the Green Paws finds itself again;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-46">Verrucchio's ancient Mastiff and the new,
Who made such bad disposal of Montagna,
Where they are wont make wimbles of their teeth.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-49">The cities of Lamone and Santerno
Governs the Lioncel of the white lair,
Who changes sides 'twixt summer-time and winter;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-52">And that of which the Savio bathes the flank,
Even as it lies between the plain and mountain,
Lives between tyranny and a free state.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-55">Now I entreat thee tell us who thou art;
Be not more stubborn than the rest have been,
So may thy name hold front there in the world."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-58">After the fire a little more had roared
In its own fashion, the sharp point it moved
This way and that, and then gave forth such breath:
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-61">"If I believed that my reply were made
To one who to the world would e'er return,
This flame without more flickering would stand still;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-64">But inasmuch as never from this depth
Did any one return, if I hear true,
Without the fear of infamy I answer,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-67">I was a man of arms, then Cordelier,
Believing thus begirt to make amends;
And truly my belief had been fulfilled
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-70">But for the High Priest, whom may ill betide,
Who put me back into my former sins;
And how and wherefore I will have thee hear.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-73">While I was still the form of bone and pulp
My mother gave to me, the deeds I did
Were not those of a lion, but a fox.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-76">The machinations and the covert ways
I knew them all, and practised so their craft,
That to the ends of earth the sound went forth.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-79">When now unto that portion of mine age
I saw myself arrived, when each one ought
To lower the sails, and coil away the ropes,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-82">That which before had pleased me then displeased me;
And penitent and confessing I surrendered,
Ah woe is me! and it would have bestead me;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-85">The Leader of the modern Pharisees
Having a war near unto Lateran,
And not with Saracens nor with the Jews,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-88">For each one of his enemies was Christian,
And none of them had been to conquer Acre,
Nor merchandising in the Sultan's land,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-91">Nor the high office, nor the sacred orders,
In him regarded, nor in me that cord
Which used to make those girt with it more meagre;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-94">But even as Constantine sought out Sylvester
To cure his leprosy, within Soracte,
So this one sought me out as an adept
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-97">To cure him of the fever of his pride.
Counsel he asked of me, and I was silent,
Because his words appeared inebriate.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-100">And then he said: 'Be not thy heart afraid;
Henceforth I thee absolve; and thou instruct me
How to raze Palestrina to the ground.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-103">Heaven have I power to lock and to unlock,
As thou dost know; therefore the keys are two,
The which my predecessor held not dear.'
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-106">Then urged me on his weighty arguments
There, where my silence was the worst advice;
And said I: 'Father, since thou washest me
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-109">Of that sin into which I now must fall,
The promise long with the fulfilment short
Will make thee triumph in thy lofty seat.'
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-112">Francis came afterward, when I was dead,
For me; but one of the black Cherubim
Said to him: 'Take him not; do me no wrong;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-115">He must come down among my servitors,
Because he gave the fraudulent advice
From which time forth I have been at his hair;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-118">For who repents not cannot be absolved,
Nor can one both repent and will at once,
Because of the contradiction which consents not.'
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-121">O miserable me! how I did shudder
When he seized on me, saying: 'Peradventure
Thou didst not think that I was a logician!'
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-124">He bore me unto Minos, who entwined
Eight times his tail about his stubborn back,
And after he had bitten it in great rage,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-127">Said: 'Of the thievish fire a culprit this;'
Wherefore, here where thou seest, am I lost,
And vested thus in going I bemoan me."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-130">When it had thus completed its recital,
The flame departed uttering lamentations,
Writhing and flapping its sharp-pointed horn.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-133">Onward we passed, both I and my Conductor,
Up o'er the crag above another arch,
Which the moat covers, where is paid the fee
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxvii-136">By those who, sowing discord, win their burden.
</p>

## Inferno - Canto XXVIII

<p id="inferno-xxviii-1">Who ever could, e'en with untrammelled words,
Tell of the blood and of the wounds in full
Which now I saw, by many times narrating?
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-4">Each tongue would for a certainty fall short
By reason of our speech and memory,
That have small room to comprehend so much.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-7">If were again assembled all the people
Which formerly upon the fateful land
Of Puglia were lamenting for their blood
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-10">Shed by the Romans and the lingering war
That of the rings made such illustrious spoils,
As Livy has recorded, who errs not,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-13">With those who felt the agony of blows
By making counterstand to Robert Guiscard,
And all the rest, whose bones are gathered still
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-16">At Ceperano, where a renegade
Was each Apulian, and at Tagliacozzo,
Where without arms the old Alardo conquered,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-19">And one his limb transpierced, and one lopped off,
Should show, it would be nothing to compare
With the disgusting mode of the ninth Bolgia.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-22">A cask by losing centre-piece or cant
Was never shattered so, as I saw one
Rent from the chin to where one breaketh wind.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-25">Between his legs were hanging down his entrails;
His heart was visible, and the dismal sack
That maketh excrement of what is eaten.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-28">While I was all absorbed in seeing him,
He looked at me, and opened with his hands
His bosom, saying: "See now how I rend me;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-31">How mutilated, see, is Mahomet;
In front of me doth Ali weeping go,
Cleft in the face from forelock unto chin;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-34">And all the others whom thou here beholdest,
Disseminators of scandal and of schism
While living were, and therefore are cleft thus.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-37">A devil is behind here, who doth cleave us
Thus cruelly, unto the falchion's edge
Putting again each one of all this ream,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-40">When we have gone around the doleful road;
By reason that our wounds are closed again
Ere any one in front of him repass.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-43">But who art thou, that musest on the crag,
Perchance to postpone going to the pain
That is adjudged upon thine accusations?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-46">"Nor death hath reached him yet, nor guilt doth bring him,"
My Master made reply, "to be tormented;
But to procure him full experience,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-49">Me, who am dead, behoves it to conduct him
Down here through Hell, from circle unto circle;
And this is true as that I speak to thee."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-52">More than a hundred were there when they heard him,
Who in the moat stood still to look at me,
Through wonderment oblivious of their torture.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-55">"Now say to Fra Dolcino, then, to arm him,
Thou, who perhaps wilt shortly see the sun,
If soon he wish not here to follow me,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-58">So with provisions, that no stress of snow
May give the victory to the Novarese,
Which otherwise to gain would not be easy."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-61">After one foot to go away he lifted,
This word did Mahomet say unto me,
Then to depart upon the ground he stretched it.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-64">Another one, who had his throat pierced through,
And nose cut off close underneath the brows,
And had no longer but a single ear,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-67">Staying to look in wonder with the others,
Before the others did his gullet open,
Which outwardly was red in every part,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-70">And said: "O thou, whom guilt doth not condemn,
And whom I once saw up in Latian land,
Unless too great similitude deceive me,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-73">Call to remembrance Pier da Medicina,
If e'er thou see again the lovely plain
That from Vercelli slopes to Marcabo,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-76">And make it known to the best two of Fano,
To Messer Guido and Angiolello likewise,
That if foreseeing here be not in vain,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-79">Cast over from their vessel shall they be,
And drowned near unto the Cattolica,
By the betrayal of a tyrant fell.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-82">Between the isles of Cyprus and Majorca
Neptune ne'er yet beheld so great a crime,
Neither of pirates nor Argolic people.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-85">That traitor, who sees only with one eye,
And holds the land, which some one here with me
Would fain be fasting from the vision of,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-88">Will make them come unto a parley with him;
Then will do so, that to Focara's wind
They will not stand in need of vow or prayer."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-91">And I to him: "Show to me and declare,
If thou wouldst have me bear up news of thee,
Who is this person of the bitter vision."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-94">Then did he lay his hand upon the jaw
Of one of his companions, and his mouth
Oped, crying: "This is he, and he speaks not.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-97">This one, being banished, every doubt submerged
In Caesar by affirming the forearmed
Always with detriment allowed delay."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-100">O how bewildered unto me appeared,
With tongue asunder in his windpipe slit,
Curio, who in speaking was so bold!
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-103">And one, who both his hands dissevered had,
The stumps uplifting through the murky air,
So that the blood made horrible his face,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-106">Cried out: "Thou shalt remember Mosca also,
Who said, alas! 'A thing done has an end!'
Which was an ill seed for the Tuscan people."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-109">"And death unto thy race," thereto I added;
Whence he, accumulating woe on woe,
Departed, like a person sad and crazed.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-112">But I remained to look upon the crowd;
And saw a thing which I should be afraid,
Without some further proof, even to recount,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-115">If it were not that conscience reassures me,
That good companion which emboldens man
Beneath the hauberk of its feeling pure.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-118">I truly saw, and still I seem to see it,
A trunk without a head walk in like manner
As walked the others of the mournful herd.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-121">And by the hair it held the head dissevered,
Hung from the hand in fashion of a lantern,
And that upon us gazed and said: "O me!"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-124">It of itself made to itself a lamp,
And they were two in one, and one in two;
How that can be, He knows who so ordains it.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-127">When it was come close to the bridge's foot,
It lifted high its arm with all the head,
To bring more closely unto us its words,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-130">Which were: "Behold now the sore penalty,
Thou, who dost breathing go the dead beholding;
Behold if any be as great as this.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-133">And so that thou may carry news of me,
Know that Bertram de Born am I, the same
Who gave to the Young King the evil comfort.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-136">I made the father and the son rebellious;
Achitophel not more with Absalom
And David did with his accursed goadings.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-139">Because I parted persons so united,
Parted do I now bear my brain, alas!
From its beginning, which is in this trunk.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxviii-142">Thus is observed in me the counterpoise."
</p>

## Inferno - Canto XXIX

<p id="inferno-xxix-1">The many people and the divers wounds
These eyes of mine had so inebriated,
That they were wishful to stand still and weep;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-4">But said Virgilius: "What dost thou still gaze at?
Why is thy sight still riveted down there
Among the mournful, mutilated shades?
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-7">Thou hast not done so at the other Bolge;
Consider, if to count them thou believest,
That two-and-twenty miles the valley winds,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-10">And now the moon is underneath our feet;
Henceforth the time allotted us is brief,
And more is to be seen than what thou seest."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-13">"If thou hadst," I made answer thereupon,
"Attended to the cause for which I looked,
Perhaps a longer stay thou wouldst have pardoned."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-16">Meanwhile my Guide departed, and behind him
I went, already making my reply,
And superadding: "In that cavern where
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-19">I held mine eyes with such attention fixed,
I think a spirit of my blood laments
The sin which down below there costs so much."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-22">Then said the Master: "Be no longer broken
Thy thought from this time forward upon him;
Attend elsewhere, and there let him remain;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-25">For him I saw below the little bridge,
Pointing at thee, and threatening with his finger
Fiercely, and heard him called Geri del Bello.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-28">So wholly at that time wast thou impeded
By him who formerly held Altaforte,
Thou didst not look that way; so he departed."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-31">"O my Conductor, his own violent death,
Which is not yet avenged for him," I said,
"By any who is sharer in the shame,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-34">Made him disdainful; whence he went away,
As I imagine, without speaking to me,
And thereby made me pity him the more."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-37">Thus did we speak as far as the first place
Upon the crag, which the next valley shows
Down to the bottom, if there were more light.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-40">When we were now right over the last cloister
Of Malebolge, so that its lay-brothers
Could manifest themselves unto our sight,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-43">Divers lamentings pierced me through and through,
Which with compassion had their arrows barbed,
Whereat mine ears I covered with my hands.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-46">What pain would be, if from the hospitals
Of Valdichiana, 'twixt July and September,
And of Maremma and Sardinia
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-49">All the diseases in one moat were gathered,
Such was it here, and such a stench came from it
As from putrescent limbs is wont to issue.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-52">We had descended on the furthest bank
From the long crag, upon the left hand still,
And then more vivid was my power of sight
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-55">Down tow'rds the bottom, where the ministress
Of the high Lord, Justice infallible,
Punishes forgers, which she here records.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-58">I do not think a sadder sight to see
Was in Aegina the whole people sick,
(When was the air so full of pestilence,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-61">The animals, down to the little worm,
All fell, and afterwards the ancient people,
According as the poets have affirmed,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-64">Were from the seed of ants restored again,)
Than was it to behold through that dark valley
The spirits languishing in divers heaps.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-67">This on the belly, that upon the back
One of the other lay, and others crawling
Shifted themselves along the dismal road.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-70">We step by step went onward without speech,
Gazing upon and listening to the sick
Who had not strength enough to lift their bodies.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-73">I saw two sitting leaned against each other,
As leans in heating platter against platter,
From head to foot bespotted o'er with scabs;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-76">And never saw I plied a currycomb
By stable-boy for whom his master waits,
Or him who keeps awake unwillingly,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-79">As every one was plying fast the bite
Of nails upon himself, for the great rage
Of itching which no other succour had.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-82">And the nails downward with them dragged the scab,
In fashion as a knife the scales of bream,
Or any other fish that has them largest.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-85">"O thou, that with thy fingers dost dismail thee,"
Began my Leader unto one of them,
"And makest of them pincers now and then,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-88">Tell me if any Latian is with those
Who are herein; so may thy nails suffice thee
To all eternity unto this work."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-91">"Latians are we, whom thou so wasted seest,
Both of us here," one weeping made reply;
"But who art thou, that questionest about us?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-94">And said the Guide: "One am I who descends
Down with this living man from cliff to cliff,
And I intend to show Hell unto him."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-97">Then broken was their mutual support,
And trembling each one turned himself to me,
With others who had heard him by rebound.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-100">Wholly to me did the good Master gather,
Saying: "Say unto them whate'er thou wishest."
And I began, since he would have it so:
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-103">"So may your memory not steal away
In the first world from out the minds of men,
But so may it survive 'neath many suns,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-106">Say to me who ye are, and of what people;
Let not your foul and loathsome punishment
Make you afraid to show yourselves to me."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-109">"I of Arezzo was," one made reply,
"And Albert of Siena had me burned;
But what I died for does not bring me here.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-112">'Tis true I said to him, speaking in jest,
That I could rise by flight into the air,
And he who had conceit, but little wit,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-115">Would have me show to him the art; and only
Because no Daedalus I made him, made me
Be burned by one who held him as his son.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-118">But unto the last Bolgia of the ten,
For alchemy, which in the world I practised,
Minos, who cannot err, has me condemned."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-121">And to the Poet said I: "Now was ever
So vain a people as the Sienese?
Not for a certainty the French by far."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-124">Whereat the other leper, who had heard me,
Replied unto my speech: "Taking out Stricca,
Who knew the art of moderate expenses,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-127">And Niccolo, who the luxurious use
Of cloves discovered earliest of all
Within that garden where such seed takes root;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-130">And taking out the band, among whom squandered
Caccia d'Ascian his vineyards and vast woods,
And where his wit the Abbagliato proffered!
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-133">But, that thou know who thus doth second thee
Against the Sienese, make sharp thine eye
Tow'rds me, so that my face well answer thee,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-136">And thou shalt see I am Capocchio's shade,
Who metals falsified by alchemy;
Thou must remember, if I well descry thee,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxix-139">How I a skilful ape of nature was."
</p>

## Inferno - Canto XXX

<p id="inferno-xxx-1">'Twas at the time when Juno was enraged,
For Semele, against the Theban blood,
As she already more than once had shown,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-4">So reft of reason Athamas became,
That, seeing his own wife with children twain
Walking encumbered upon either hand,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-7">He cried: "Spread out the nets, that I may take
The lioness and her whelps upon the passage;"
And then extended his unpitying claws,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-10">Seizing the first, who had the name Learchus,
And whirled him round, and dashed him on a rock;
And she, with the other burthen, drowned herself;--
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-13">And at the time when fortune downward hurled
The Trojan's arrogance, that all things dared,
So that the king was with his kingdom crushed,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-16">Hecuba sad, disconsolate, and captive,
When lifeless she beheld Polyxena,
And of her Polydorus on the shore
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-19">Of ocean was the dolorous one aware,
Out of her senses like a dog she barked,
So much the anguish had her mind distorted;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-22">But not of Thebes the furies nor the Trojan
Were ever seen in any one so cruel
In goading beasts, and much more human members,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-25">As I beheld two shadows pale and naked,
Who, biting, in the manner ran along
That a boar does, when from the sty turned loose.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-28">One to Capocchio came, and by the nape
Seized with its teeth his neck, so that in dragging
It made his belly grate the solid bottom.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-31">And the Aretine, who trembling had remained,
Said to me: "That mad sprite is Gianni Schicchi,
And raving goes thus harrying other people."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-34">"O," said I to him, "so may not the other
Set teeth on thee, let it not weary thee
To tell us who it is, ere it dart hence."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-37">And he to me: "That is the ancient ghost
Of the nefarious Myrrha, who became
Beyond all rightful love her father's lover.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-40">She came to sin with him after this manner,
By counterfeiting of another's form;
As he who goeth yonder undertook,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-43">That he might gain the lady of the herd,
To counterfeit in himself Buoso Donati,
Making a will and giving it due form."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-46">And after the two maniacs had passed
On whom I held mine eye, I turned it back
To look upon the other evil-born.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-49">I saw one made in fashion of a lute,
If he had only had the groin cut off
Just at the point at which a man is forked.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-52">The heavy dropsy, that so disproportions
The limbs with humours, which it ill concocts,
That the face corresponds not to the belly,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-55">Compelled him so to hold his lips apart
As does the hectic, who because of thirst
One tow'rds the chin, the other upward turns.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-58">"O ye, who without any torment are,
And why I know not, in the world of woe,"
He said to us, "behold, and be attentive
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-61">Unto the misery of Master Adam;
I had while living much of what I wished,
And now, alas! a drop of water crave.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-64">The rivulets, that from the verdant hills
Of Cassentin descend down into Arno,
Making their channels to be cold and moist,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-67">Ever before me stand, and not in vain;
For far more doth their image dry me up
Than the disease which strips my face of flesh.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-70">The rigid justice that chastises me
Draweth occasion from the place in which
I sinned, to put the more my sighs in flight.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-73">There is Romena, where I counterfeited
The currency imprinted with the Baptist,
For which I left my body burned above.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-76">But if I here could see the tristful soul
Of Guido, or Alessandro, or their brother,
For Branda's fount I would not give the sight.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-79">One is within already, if the raving
Shades that are going round about speak truth;
But what avails it me, whose limbs are tied?
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-82">If I were only still so light, that in
A hundred years I could advance one inch,
I had already started on the way,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-85">Seeking him out among this squalid folk,
Although the circuit be eleven miles,
And be not less than half a mile across.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-88">For them am I in such a family;
They did induce me into coining florins,
Which had three carats of impurity."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-91">And I to him: "Who are the two poor wretches
That smoke like unto a wet hand in winter,
Lying there close upon thy right-hand confines?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-94">"I found them here," replied he, "when I rained
Into this chasm, and since they have not turned,
Nor do I think they will for evermore.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-97">One the false woman is who accused Joseph,
The other the false Sinon, Greek of Troy;
From acute fever they send forth such reek."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-100">And one of them, who felt himself annoyed
At being, peradventure, named so darkly,
Smote with the fist upon his hardened paunch.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-103">It gave a sound, as if it were a drum;
And Master Adam smote him in the face,
With arm that did not seem to be less hard,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-106">Saying to him: "Although be taken from me
All motion, for my limbs that heavy are,
I have an arm unfettered for such need."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-109">Whereat he answer made: "When thou didst go
Unto the fire, thou hadst it not so ready:
But hadst it so and more when thou wast coining."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-112">The dropsical: "Thou sayest true in that;
But thou wast not so true a witness there,
Where thou wast questioned of the truth at Troy."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-115">"If I spake false, thou falsifiedst the coin,"
Said Sinon; "and for one fault I am here,
And thou for more than any other demon."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-118">"Remember, perjurer, about the horse,"
He made reply who had the swollen belly,
"And rueful be it thee the whole world knows it."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-121">"Rueful to thee the thirst be wherewith cracks
Thy tongue," the Greek said, "and the putrid water
That hedges so thy paunch before thine eyes."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-124">Then the false-coiner: "So is gaping wide
Thy mouth for speaking evil, as 'tis wont;
Because if I have thirst, and humour stuff me
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-127">Thou hast the burning and the head that aches,
And to lick up the mirror of Narcissus
Thou wouldst not want words many to invite thee."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-130">In listening to them was I wholly fixed,
When said the Master to me: "Now just look,
For little wants it that I quarrel with thee."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-133">When him I heard in anger speak to me,
I turned me round towards him with such shame
That still it eddies through my memory.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-136">And as he is who dreams of his own harm,
Who dreaming wishes it may be a dream,
So that he craves what is, as if it were not;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-139">Such I became, not having power to speak,
For to excuse myself I wished, and still
Excused myself, and did not think I did it.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-142">"Less shame doth wash away a greater fault,"
The Master said, "than this of thine has been;
Therefore thyself disburden of all sadness,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-145">And make account that I am aye beside thee,
If e'er it come to pass that fortune bring thee
Where there are people in a like dispute;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxx-148">For a base wish it is to wish to hear it."
</p>

## Inferno - Canto XXXI

<p id="inferno-xxxi-1">One and the selfsame tongue first wounded me,
So that it tinged the one cheek and the other,
And then held out to me the medicine;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-4">Thus do I hear that once Achilles' spear,
His and his father's, used to be the cause
First of a sad and then a gracious boon.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-7">We turned our backs upon the wretched valley,
Upon the bank that girds it round about,
Going across it without any speech.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-10">There it was less than night, and less than day,
So that my sight went little in advance;
But I could hear the blare of a loud horn,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-13">So loud it would have made each thunder faint,
Which, counter to it following its way,
Mine eyes directed wholly to one place.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-16">After the dolorous discomfiture
When Charlemagne the holy emprise lost,
So terribly Orlando sounded not.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-19">Short while my head turned thitherward I held
When many lofty towers I seemed to see,
Whereat I: "Master, say, what town is this?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-22">And he to me: "Because thou peerest forth
Athwart the darkness at too great a distance,
It happens that thou errest in thy fancy.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-25">Well shalt thou see, if thou arrivest there,
How much the sense deceives itself by distance;
Therefore a little faster spur thee on."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-28">Then tenderly he took me by the hand,
And said: "Before we farther have advanced,
That the reality may seem to thee
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-31">Less strange, know that these are not towers, but giants,
And they are in the well, around the bank,
From navel downward, one and all of them."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-34">As, when the fog is vanishing away,
Little by little doth the sight refigure
Whate'er the mist that crowds the air conceals,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-37">So, piercing through the dense and darksome air,
More and more near approaching tow'rd the verge,
My error fled, and fear came over me;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-40">Because as on its circular parapets
Montereggione crowns itself with towers,
E'en thus the margin which surrounds the well
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-43">With one half of their bodies turreted
The horrible giants, whom Jove menaces
E'en now from out the heavens when he thunders.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-46">And I of one already saw the face,
Shoulders, and breast, and great part of the belly,
And down along his sides both of the arms.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-49">Certainly Nature, when she left the making
Of animals like these, did well indeed,
By taking such executors from Mars;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-52">And if of elephants and whales she doth not
Repent her, whosoever looketh subtly
More just and more discreet will hold her for it;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-55">For where the argument of intellect
Is added unto evil will and power,
No rampart can the people make against it.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-58">His face appeared to me as long and large
As is at Rome the pine-cone of Saint Peter's,
And in proportion were the other bones;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-61">So that the margin, which an apron was
Down from the middle, showed so much of him
Above it, that to reach up to his hair
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-64">Three Frieslanders in vain had vaunted them;
For I beheld thirty great palms of him
Down from the place where man his mantle buckles.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-67">"Raphael mai amech izabi almi,"
Began to clamour the ferocious mouth,
To which were not befitting sweeter psalms.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-70">And unto him my Guide: "Soul idiotic,
Keep to thy horn, and vent thyself with that,
When wrath or other passion touches thee.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-73">Search round thy neck, and thou wilt find the belt
Which keeps it fastened, O bewildered soul,
And see it, where it bars thy mighty breast."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-76">Then said to me: "He doth himself accuse;
This one is Nimrod, by whose evil thought
One language in the world is not still used.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-79">Here let us leave him and not speak in vain;
For even such to him is every language
As his to others, which to none is known."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-82">Therefore a longer journey did we make,
Turned to the left, and a crossbow-shot oft
We found another far more fierce and large.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-85">In binding him, who might the master be
I cannot say; but he had pinioned close
Behind the right arm, and in front the other,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-88">With chains, that held him so begirt about
From the neck down, that on the part uncovered
It wound itself as far as the fifth gyre.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-91">"This proud one wished to make experiment
Of his own power against the Supreme Jove,"
My Leader said, "whence he has such a guerdon.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-94">Ephialtes is his name; he showed great prowess.
What time the giants terrified the gods;
The arms he wielded never more he moves."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-97">And I to him: "If possible, I should wish
That of the measureless Briareus
These eyes of mine might have experience."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-100">Whence he replied: "Thou shalt behold Antaeus
Close by here, who can speak and is unbound,
Who at the bottom of all crime shall place us.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-103">Much farther yon is he whom thou wouldst see,
And he is bound, and fashioned like to this one,
Save that he seems in aspect more ferocious."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-106">There never was an earthquake of such might
That it could shake a tower so violently,
As Ephialtes suddenly shook himself.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-109">Then was I more afraid of death than ever,
For nothing more was needful than the fear,
If I had not beheld the manacles.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-112">Then we proceeded farther in advance,
And to Antaeus came, who, full five ells
Without the head, forth issued from the cavern.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-115">"O thou, who in the valley fortunate,
Which Scipio the heir of glory made,
When Hannibal turned back with all his hosts,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-118">Once brought'st a thousand lions for thy prey,
And who, hadst thou been at the mighty war
Among thy brothers, some it seems still think
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-121">The sons of Earth the victory would have gained:
Place us below, nor be disdainful of it,
There where the cold doth lock Cocytus up.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-124">Make us not go to Tityus nor Typhoeus;
This one can give of that which here is longed for;
Therefore stoop down, and do not curl thy lip.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-127">Still in the world can he restore thy fame;
Because he lives, and still expects long life,
If to itself Grace call him not untimely."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-130">So said the Master; and in haste the other
His hands extended and took up my Guide,--
Hands whose great pressure Hercules once felt.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-133">Virgilius, when he felt himself embraced,
Said unto me: "Draw nigh, that I may take thee;"
Then of himself and me one bundle made.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-136">As seems the Carisenda, to behold
Beneath the leaning side, when goes a cloud
Above it so that opposite it hangs;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-139">Such did Antaeus seem to me, who stood
Watching to see him stoop, and then it was
I could have wished to go some other way.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-142">But lightly in the abyss, which swallows up
Judas with Lucifer, he put us down;
Nor thus bowed downward made he there delay,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxi-145">But, as a mast does in a ship, uprose.
</p>

## Inferno - Canto XXXII

<p id="inferno-xxxii-1">If I had rhymes both rough and stridulous,
As were appropriate to the dismal hole
Down upon which thrust all the other rocks,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-4">I would press out the juice of my conception
More fully; but because I have them not,
Not without fear I bring myself to speak;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-7">For 'tis no enterprise to take in jest,
To sketch the bottom of all the universe,
Nor for a tongue that cries Mamma and Babbo.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-10">But may those Ladies help this verse of mine,
Who helped Amphion in enclosing Thebes,
That from the fact the word be not diverse.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-13">O rabble ill-begotten above all,
Who're in the place to speak of which is hard,
'Twere better ye had here been sheep or goats!
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-16">When we were down within the darksome well,
Beneath the giant's feet, but lower far,
And I was scanning still the lofty wall,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-19">I heard it said to me: "Look how thou steppest!
Take heed thou do not trample with thy feet
The heads of the tired, miserable brothers!"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-22">Whereat I turned me round, and saw before me
And underfoot a lake, that from the frost
The semblance had of glass, and not of water.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-25">So thick a veil ne'er made upon its current
In winter-time Danube in Austria,
Nor there beneath the frigid sky the Don,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-28">As there was here; so that if Tambernich
Had fallen upon it, or Pietrapana,
E'en at the edge 'twould not have given a creak.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-31">And as to croak the frog doth place himself
With muzzle out of water,--when is dreaming
Of gleaning oftentimes the peasant-girl,--
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-34">Livid, as far down as where shame appears,
Were the disconsolate shades within the ice,
Setting their teeth unto the note of storks.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-37">Each one his countenance held downward bent;
From mouth the cold, from eyes the doleful heart
Among them witness of itself procures.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-40">When round about me somewhat I had looked,
I downward turned me, and saw two so close,
The hair upon their heads together mingled.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-43">"Ye who so strain your breasts together, tell me,"
I said, "who are you;" and they bent their necks,
And when to me their faces they had lifted,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-46">Their eyes, which first were only moist within,
Gushed o'er the eyelids, and the frost congealed
The tears between, and locked them up again.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-49">Clamp never bound together wood with wood
So strongly; whereat they, like two he-goats,
Butted together, so much wrath o'ercame them.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-52">And one, who had by reason of the cold
Lost both his ears, still with his visage downward,
Said: "Why dost thou so mirror thyself in us?
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-55">If thou desire to know who these two are,
The valley whence Bisenzio descends
Belonged to them and to their father Albert.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-58">They from one body came, and all Caina
Thou shalt search through, and shalt not find a shade
More worthy to be fixed in gelatine;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-61">Not he in whom were broken breast and shadow
At one and the same blow by Arthur's hand;
Focaccia not; not he who me encumbers
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-64">So with his head I see no farther forward,
And bore the name of Sassol Mascheroni;
Well knowest thou who he was, if thou art Tuscan.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-67">And that thou put me not to further speech,
Know that I Camicion de' Pazzi was,
And wait Carlino to exonerate me."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-70">Then I beheld a thousand faces, made
Purple with cold; whence o'er me comes a shudder,
And evermore will come, at frozen ponds.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-73">And while we were advancing tow'rds the middle,
Where everything of weight unites together,
And I was shivering in the eternal shade,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-76">Whether 'twere will, or destiny, or chance,
I know not; but in walking 'mong the heads
I struck my foot hard in the face of one.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-79">Weeping he growled: "Why dost thou trample me?
Unless thou comest to increase the vengeance
of Montaperti, why dost thou molest me?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-82">And I: "My Master, now wait here for me,
That I through him may issue from a doubt;
Then thou mayst hurry me, as thou shalt wish."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-85">The Leader stopped; and to that one I said
Who was blaspheming vehemently still:
"Who art thou, that thus reprehendest others?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-88">"Now who art thou, that goest through Antenora
Smiting," replied he, "other people's cheeks,
So that, if thou wert living, 'twere too much?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-91">"Living I am, and dear to thee it may be,"
Was my response, "if thou demandest fame,
That 'mid the other notes thy name I place."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-94">And he to me: "For the reverse I long;
Take thyself hence, and give me no more trouble;
For ill thou knowest to flatter in this hollow."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-97">Then by the scalp behind I seized upon him,
And said: "It must needs be thou name thyself,
Or not a hair remain upon thee here."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-100">Whence he to me: "Though thou strip off my hair,
I will not tell thee who I am, nor show thee,
If on my head a thousand times thou fall."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-103">I had his hair in hand already twisted,
And more than one shock of it had pulled out,
He barking, with his eyes held firmly down,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-106">When cried another: "What doth ail thee, Bocca?
Is't not enough to clatter with thy jaws,
But thou must bark? what devil touches thee?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-109">"Now," said I, "I care not to have thee speak,
Accursed traitor; for unto thy shame
I will report of thee veracious news."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-112">"Begone," replied he, "and tell what thou wilt,
But be not silent, if thou issue hence,
Of him who had just now his tongue so prompt;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-115">He weepeth here the silver of the French;
'I saw,' thus canst thou phrase it, 'him of Duera
There where the sinners stand out in the cold.'
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-118">If thou shouldst questioned be who else was there,
Thou hast beside thee him of Beccaria,
Of whom the gorget Florence slit asunder;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-121">Gianni del Soldanier, I think, may be
Yonder with Ganellon, and Tebaldello
Who oped Faenza when the people slep."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-124">Already we had gone away from him,
When I beheld two frozen in one hole,
So that one head a hood was to the other;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-127">And even as bread through hunger is devoured,
The uppermost on the other set his teeth,
There where the brain is to the nape united.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-130">Not in another fashion Tydeus gnawed
The temples of Menalippus in disdain,
Than that one did the skull and the other things.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-133">"O thou, who showest by such bestial sign
Thy hatred against him whom thou art eating,
Tell me the wherefore," said I, "with this compact,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-136">That if thou rightfully of him complain,
In knowing who ye are, and his transgression,
I in the world above repay thee for it,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxii-139">If that wherewith I speak be not dried up."
</p>

## Inferno - Canto XXXIII

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-1">His mouth uplifted from his grim repast,
That sinner, wiping it upon the hair
Of the same head that he behind had wasted.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-4">Then he began: "Thou wilt that I renew
The desperate grief, which wrings my heart already
To think of only, ere I speak of it;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-7">But if my words be seed that may bear fruit
Of infamy to the traitor whom I gnaw,
Speaking and weeping shalt thou see together.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-10">I know not who thou art, nor by what mode
Thou hast come down here; but a Florentine
Thou seemest to me truly, when I hear thee.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-13">Thou hast to know I was Count Ugolino,
And this one was Ruggieri the Archbishop;
Now I will tell thee why I am such a neighbour.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-16">That, by effect of his malicious thoughts,
Trusting in him I was made prisoner,
And after put to death, I need not say;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-19">But ne'ertheless what thou canst not have heard,
That is to say, how cruel was my death,
Hear shalt thou, and shalt know if he has wronged me.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-22">A narrow perforation in the mew,
Which bears because of me the title of Famine,
And in which others still must be locked up,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-25">Had shown me through its opening many moons
Already, when I dreamed the evil dream
Which of the future rent for me the veil.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-28">This one appeared to me as lord and master,
Hunting the wolf and whelps upon the mountain
For which the Pisans cannot Lucca see.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-31">With sleuth-hounds gaunt, and eager, and well trained,
Gualandi with Sismondi and Lanfianchi
He had sent out before him to the front.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-34">After brief course seemed unto me forespent
The father and the sons, and with sharp tushes
It seemed to me I saw their flanks ripped open.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-37">When I before the morrow was awake,
Moaning amid their sleep I heard my sons
Who with me were, and asking after bread.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-40">Cruel indeed art thou, if yet thou grieve not,
Thinking of what my heart foreboded me,
And weep'st thou not, what art thou wont to weep at?
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-43">They were awake now, and the hour drew nigh
At which our food used to be brought to us,
And through his dream was each one apprehensive;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-46">And I heard locking up the under door
Of the horrible tower; whereat without a word
I gazed into the faces of my sons.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-49">I wept not, I within so turned to stone;
They wept; and darling little Anselm mine
Said: 'Thou dost gaze so, father, what doth ail thee?'
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-52">Still not a tear I shed, nor answer made
All of that day, nor yet the night thereafter,
Until another sun rose on the world.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-55">As now a little glimmer made its way
Into the dolorous prison, and I saw
Upon four faces my own very aspect,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-58">Both of my hands in agony I bit;
And, thinking that I did it from desire
Of eating, on a sudden they uprose,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-61">And said they: 'Father, much less pain 'twill give us
If thou do eat of us; thyself didst clothe us
With this poor flesh, and do thou strip it off.'
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-64">I calmed me then, not to make them more sad.
That day we all were silent, and the next.
Ah! obdurate earth, wherefore didst thou not open?
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-67">When we had come unto the fourth day, Gaddo
Threw himself down outstretched before my feet,
Saying, 'My father, why dost thou not help me?'
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-70">And there he died; and, as thou seest me,
I saw the three fall, one by one, between
The fifth day and the sixth; whence I betook me,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-73">Already blind, to groping over each,
And three days called them after they were dead;
Then hunger did what sorrow could not do."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-76">When he had said this, with his eyes distorted,
The wretched skull resumed he with his teeth,
Which, as a dog's, upon the bone were strong.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-79">Ah! Pisa, thou opprobrium of the people
Of the fair land there where the 'Si' doth sound,
Since slow to punish thee thy neighbours are,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-82">Let the Capraia and Gorgona move,
And make a hedge across the mouth of Arno
That every person in thee it may drown!
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-85">For if Count Ugolino had the fame
Of having in thy castles thee betrayed,
Thou shouldst not on such cross have put his sons.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-88">Guiltless of any crime, thou modern Thebes!
Their youth made Uguccione and Brigata,
And the other two my song doth name above!
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-91">We passed still farther onward, where the ice
Another people ruggedly enswathes,
Not downward turned, but all of them reversed.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-94">Weeping itself there does not let them weep,
And grief that finds a barrier in the eyes
Turns itself inward to increase the anguish;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-97">Because the earliest tears a cluster form,
And, in the manner of a crystal visor,
Fill all the cup beneath the eyebrow full.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-100">And notwithstanding that, as in a callus,
Because of cold all sensibility
Its station had abandoned in my face,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-103">Still it appeared to me I felt some wind;
Whence I: "My Master, who sets this in motion?
Is not below here every vapour quenched?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-106">Whence he to me: "Full soon shalt thou be where
Thine eye shall answer make to thee of this,
Seeing the cause which raineth down the blast."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-109">And one of the wretches of the frozen crust
Cried out to us: "O souls so merciless
That the last post is given unto you,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-112">Lift from mine eyes the rigid veils, that I
May vent the sorrow which impregns my heart
A little, e'er the weeping recongeal."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-115">Whence I to him: "If thou wouldst have me help thee
Say who thou wast; and if I free thee not,
May I go to the bottom of the ice."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-118">Then he replied: "I am Friar Alberigo;
He am I of the fruit of the bad garden,
Who here a date am getting for my fig."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-121">"O," said I to him, "now art thou, too, dead?"
And he to me: "How may my body fare
Up in the world, no knowledge I possess.
</p>
