<p id="inferno-xxxiii-124">Such an advantage has this Ptolomaea,
That oftentimes the soul descendeth here
Sooner than Atropos in motion sets it.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-127">And, that thou mayest more willingly remove
From off my countenance these glassy tears,
Know that as soon as any soul betrays
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-130">As I have done, his body by a demon
Is taken from him, who thereafter rules it,
Until his time has wholly been revolved.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-133">Itself down rushes into such a cistern;
And still perchance above appears the body
Of yonder shade, that winters here behind me.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-136">This thou shouldst know, if thou hast just come down;
It is Ser Branca d' Oria, and many years
Have passed away since he was thus locked up."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-139">"I think," said I to him, "thou dost deceive me;
For Branca d' Oria is not dead as yet,
And eats, and drinks, and sleeps, and puts on clothes."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-142">"In moat above," said he, "of Malebranche,
There where is boiling the tenacious pitch,
As yet had Michel Zanche not arrived,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-145">When this one left a devil in his stead
In his own body and one near of kin,
Who made together with him the betrayal.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-148">But hitherward stretch out thy hand forthwith,
Open mine eyes;"--and open them I did not,
And to be rude to him was courtesy.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-151">Ah, Genoese! ye men at variance
With every virtue, full of every vice
Wherefore are ye not scattered from the world?
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-154">For with the vilest spirit of Romagna
I found of you one such, who for his deeds
In soul already in Cocytus bathes,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiii-157">And still above in body seems alive!
</p>

## Inferno - Canto XXXIV

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-1">"'Vexilla Regis prodeunt Inferni'
Towards us; therefore look in front of thee,"
My Master said, "if thou discernest him."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-4">As, when there breathes a heavy fog, or when
Our hemisphere is darkening into night,
Appears far off a mill the wind is turning,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-7">Methought that such a building then I saw;
And, for the wind, I drew myself behind
My Guide, because there was no other shelter.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-10">Now was I, and with fear in verse I put it,
There where the shades were wholly covered up,
And glimmered through like unto straws in glass.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-13">Some prone are lying, others stand erect,
This with the head, and that one with the soles;
Another, bow-like, face to feet inverts.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-16">When in advance so far we had proceeded,
That it my Master pleased to show to me
The creature who once had the beauteous semblance,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-19">He from before me moved and made me stop,
Saying: "Behold Dis, and behold the place
Where thou with fortitude must arm thyself."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-22">How frozen I became and powerless then,
Ask it not, Reader, for I write it not,
Because all language would be insufficient.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-25">I did not die, and I alive remained not;
Think for thyself now, hast thou aught of wit,
What I became, being of both deprived.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-28">The Emperor of the kingdom dolorous
From his mid-breast forth issued from the ice;
And better with a giant I compare
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-31">Than do the giants with those arms of his;
Consider now how great must be that whole,
Which unto such a part conforms itself.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-34">Were he as fair once, as he now is foul,
And lifted up his brow against his Maker,
Well may proceed from him all tribulation.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-37">O, what a marvel it appeared to me,
When I beheld three faces on his head!
The one in front, and that vermilion was;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-40">Two were the others, that were joined with this
Above the middle part of either shoulder,
And they were joined together at the crest;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-43">And the right-hand one seemed 'twixt white and yellow;
The left was such to look upon as those
Who come from where the Nile falls valley-ward.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-46">Underneath each came forth two mighty wings,
Such as befitting were so great a bird;
Sails of the sea I never saw so large.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-49">No feathers had they, but as of a bat
Their fashion was; and he was waving them,
So that three winds proceeded forth therefrom.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-52">Thereby Cocytus wholly was congealed.
With six eyes did he weep, and down three chins
Trickled the tear-drops and the bloody drivel.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-55">At every mouth he with his teeth was crunching
A sinner, in the manner of a brake,
So that he three of them tormented thus.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-58">To him in front the biting was as naught
Unto the clawing, for sometimes the spine
Utterly stripped of all the skin remained.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-61">"That soul up there which has the greatest pain,"
The Master said, "is Judas Iscariot;
With head inside, he plies his legs without.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-64">Of the two others, who head downward are,
The one who hangs from the black jowl is Brutus;
See how he writhes himself, and speaks no word.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-67">And the other, who so stalwart seems, is Cassius.
But night is reascending, and 'tis time
That we depart, for we have seen the whole."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-70">As seemed him good, I clasped him round the neck,
And he the vantage seized of time and place,
And when the wings were opened wide apart,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-73">He laid fast hold upon the shaggy sides;
From fell to fell descended downward then
Between the thick hair and the frozen crust.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-76">When we were come to where the thigh revolves
Exactly on the thickness of the haunch,
The Guide, with labour and with hard-drawn breath,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-79">Turned round his head where he had had his legs,
And grappled to the hair, as one who mounts,
So that to Hell I thought we were returning.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-82">"Keep fast thy hold, for by such stairs as these,"
The Master said, panting as one fatigued,
"Must we perforce depart from so much evil."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-85">Then through the opening of a rock he issued,
And down upon the margin seated me;
Then tow'rds me he outstretched his wary step.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-88">I lifted up mine eyes and thought to see
Lucifer in the same way I had left him;
And I beheld him upward hold his legs.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-91">And if I then became disquieted,
Let stolid people think who do not see
What the point is beyond which I had passed.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-94">"Rise up," the Master said, "upon thy feet;
The way is long, and difficult the road,
And now the sun to middle-tierce returns."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-97">It was not any palace corridor
There where we were, but dungeon natural,
With floor uneven and unease of light.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-100">"Ere from the abyss I tear myself away,
My Master," said I when I had arisen,
"To draw me from an error speak a little;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-103">Where is the ice? and how is this one fixed
Thus upside down? and how in such short time
From eve to morn has the sun made his transit?"
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-106">And he to me: "Thou still imaginest
Thou art beyond the centre, where I grasped
The hair of the fell worm, who mines the world.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-109">That side thou wast, so long as I descended;
When round I turned me, thou didst pass the point
To which things heavy draw from every side,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-112">And now beneath the hemisphere art come
Opposite that which overhangs the vast
Dry-land, and 'neath whose cope was put to death
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-115">The Man who without sin was born and lived.
Thou hast thy feet upon the little sphere
Which makes the other face of the Judecca.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-118">Here it is morn when it is evening there;
And he who with his hair a stairway made us
Still fixed remaineth as he was before.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-121">Upon this side he fell down out of heaven;
And all the land, that whilom here emerged,
For fear of him made of the sea a veil,
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-124">And came to our hemisphere; and peradventure
To flee from him, what on this side appears
Left the place vacant here, and back recoiled."
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-127">A place there is below, from Beelzebub
As far receding as the tomb extends,
Which not by sight is known, but by the sound
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-130">Of a small rivulet, that there descendeth
Through chasm within the stone, which it has gnawed
With course that winds about and slightly falls.
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-133">The Guide and I into that hidden road
Now entered, to return to the bright world;
And without care of having any rest
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-136">We mounted up, he first and I the second,
Till I beheld through a round aperture
Some of the beauteous things that Heaven doth bear;
</p>

<p id="inferno-xxxiv-139">Thence we came forth to rebehold the stars.
</p>

# PURGATORIO

## Purgatorio - Canto I

<p id="purgatorio-i-1">To run o'er better waters hoists its sail
The little vessel of my genius now,
That leaves behind itself a sea so cruel;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-4">And of that second kingdom will I sing
Wherein the human spirit doth purge itself,
And to ascend to heaven becometh worthy.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-7">But let dead Poesy here rise again,
O holy Muses, since that I am yours,
And here Calliope somewhat ascend,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-10">My song accompanying with that sound,
Of which the miserable magpies felt
The blow so great, that they despaired of pardon.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-13">Sweet colour of the oriental sapphire,
That was upgathered in the cloudless aspect
Of the pure air, as far as the first circle,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-16">Unto mine eyes did recommence delight
Soon as I issued forth from the dead air,
Which had with sadness filled mine eyes and breast.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-19">The beauteous planet, that to love incites,
Was making all the orient to laugh,
Veiling the Fishes that were in her escort.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-22">To the right hand I turned, and fixed my mind
Upon the other pole, and saw four stars
Ne'er seen before save by the primal people.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-25">Rejoicing in their flamelets seemed the heaven.
O thou septentrional and widowed site,
Because thou art deprived of seeing these!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-28">When from regarding them I had withdrawn,
Turning a little to the other pole,
There where the Wain had disappeared already,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-31">I saw beside me an old man alone,
Worthy of so much reverence in his look,
That more owes not to father any son.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-34">A long beard and with white hair intermingled
He wore, in semblance like unto the tresses,
Of which a double list fell on his breast.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-37">The rays of the four consecrated stars
Did so adorn his countenance with light,
That him I saw as were the sun before him.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-40">"Who are you? ye who, counter the blind river,
Have fled away from the eternal prison?"
Moving those venerable plumes, he said:
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-43">"Who guided you? or who has been your lamp
In issuing forth out of the night profound,
That ever black makes the infernal valley?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-46">The laws of the abyss, are they thus broken?
Or is there changed in heaven some council new,
That being damned ye come unto my crags?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-49">Then did my Leader lay his grasp upon me,
And with his words, and with his hands and signs,
Reverent he made in me my knees and brow;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-52">Then answered him: "I came not of myself;
A Lady from Heaven descended, at whose prayers
I aided this one with my company.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-55">But since it is thy will more be unfolded
Of our condition, how it truly is,
Mine cannot be that this should be denied thee.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-58">This one has never his last evening seen,
But by his folly was so near to it
That very little time was there to turn.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-61">As I have said, I unto him was sent
To rescue him, and other way was none
Than this to which I have myself betaken.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-64">I've shown him all the people of perdition,
And now those spirits I intend to show
Who purge themselves beneath thy guardianship.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-67">How I have brought him would be long to tell thee.
Virtue descendeth from on high that aids me
To lead him to behold thee and to hear thee.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-70">Now may it please thee to vouchsafe his coming;
He seeketh Liberty, which is so dear,
As knoweth he who life for her refuses.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-73">Thou know'st it; since, for her, to thee not bitter
Was death in Utica, where thou didst leave
The vesture, that will shine so, the great day.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-76">By us the eternal edicts are not broken;
Since this one lives, and Minos binds not me;
But of that circle I, where are the chaste
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-79">Eyes of thy Marcia, who in looks still prays thee,
O holy breast, to hold her as thine own;
For her love, then, incline thyself to us.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-82">Permit us through thy sevenfold realm to go;
I will take back this grace from thee to her,
If to be mentioned there below thou deignest."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-85">"Marcia so pleasing was unto mine eyes
While I was on the other side," then said he,
"That every grace she wished of me I granted;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-88">Now that she dwells beyond the evil river,
She can no longer move me, by that law
Which, when I issued forth from there, was made.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-91">But if a Lady of Heaven do move and rule thee,
As thou dost say, no flattery is needful;
Let it suffice thee that for her thou ask me.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-94">Go, then, and see thou gird this one about
With a smooth rush, and that thou wash his face,
So that thou cleanse away all stain therefrom,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-97">For 'twere not fitting that the eye o'ercast
By any mist should go before the first
Angel, who is of those of Paradise.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-100">This little island round about its base
Below there, yonder, where the billow beats it,
Doth rushes bear upon its washy ooze;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-103">No other plant that putteth forth the leaf,
Or that doth indurate, can there have life,
Because it yieldeth not unto the shocks.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-106">Thereafter be not this way your return;
The sun, which now is rising, will direct you
To take the mount by easier ascent."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-109">With this he vanished; and I raised me up
Without a word, and wholly drew myself
Unto my Guide, and turned mine eyes to him.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-112">And he began: "Son, follow thou my steps;
Let us turn back, for on this side declines
The plain unto its lower boundaries."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-115">The dawn was vanquishing the matin hour
Which fled before it, so that from afar
I recognised the trembling of the sea.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-118">Along the solitary plain we went
As one who unto the lost road returns,
And till he finds it seems to go in vain.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-121">As soon as we were come to where the dew
Fights with the sun, and, being in a part
Where shadow falls, little evaporates,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-124">Both of his hands upon the grass outspread
In gentle manner did my Master place;
Whence I, who of his action was aware,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-127">Extended unto him my tearful cheeks;
There did he make in me uncovered wholly
That hue which Hell had covered up in me.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-130">Then came we down upon the desert shore
Which never yet saw navigate its waters
Any that afterward had known return.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-133">There he begirt me as the other pleased;
O marvellous! for even as he culled
The humble plant, such it sprang up again
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-i-136">Suddenly there where he uprooted it.
</p>

## Purgatorio - Canto II

<p id="purgatorio-ii-1">Already had the sun the horizon reached
Whose circle of meridian covers o'er
Jerusalem with its most lofty point,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-4">And night that opposite to him revolves
Was issuing forth from Ganges with the Scales
That fall from out her hand when she exceedeth;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-7">So that the white and the vermilion cheeks
Of beautiful Aurora, where I was,
By too great age were changing into orange.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-10">We still were on the border of the sea,
Like people who are thinking of their road,
Who go in heart and with the body stay;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-13">And lo! as when, upon the approach of morning,
Through the gross vapours Mars grows fiery red
Down in the West upon the ocean floor,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-16">Appeared to me--may I again behold it!--
A light along the sea so swiftly coming,
Its motion by no flight of wing is equalled;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-19">From which when I a little had withdrawn
Mine eyes, that I might question my Conductor,
Again I saw it brighter grown and larger.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-22">Then on each side of it appeared to me
I knew not what of white, and underneath it
Little by little there came forth another.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-25">My Master yet had uttered not a word
While the first whiteness into wings unfolded;
But when he clearly recognised the pilot,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-28">He cried: "Make haste, make haste to bow the knee!
Behold the Angel of God! fold thou thy hands!
Henceforward shalt thou see such officers!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-31">See how he scorneth human arguments,
So that nor oar he wants, nor other sail
Than his own wings, between so distant shores.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-34">See how he holds them pointed up to heaven,
Fanning the air with the eternal pinions,
That do not moult themselves like mortal hair!"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-37">Then as still nearer and more near us came
The Bird Divine, more radiant he appeared,
So that near by the eye could not endure him,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-40">But down I cast it; and he came to shore
With a small vessel, very swift and light,
So that the water swallowed naught thereof.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-43">Upon the stern stood the Celestial Pilot;
Beatitude seemed written in his face,
And more than a hundred spirits sat within.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-46">"In exitu Israel de Aegypto!"
They chanted all together in one voice,
With whatso in that psalm is after written.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-49">Then made he sign of holy rood upon them,
Whereat all cast themselves upon the shore,
And he departed swiftly as he came.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-52">The throng which still remained there unfamiliar
Seemed with the place, all round about them gazing,
As one who in new matters makes essay.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-55">On every side was darting forth the day.
The sun, who had with his resplendent shafts
From the mid-heaven chased forth the Capricorn,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-58">When the new people lifted up their faces
Towards us, saying to us: "If ye know,
Show us the way to go unto the mountain."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-61">And answer made Virgilius: "Ye believe
Perchance that we have knowledge of this place,
But we are strangers even as yourselves.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-64">Just now we came, a little while before you,
Another way, which was so rough and steep,
That mounting will henceforth seem sport to us."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-67">The souls who had, from seeing me draw breath,
Become aware that I was still alive,
Pallid in their astonishment became;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-70">And as to messenger who bears the olive
The people throng to listen to the news,
And no one shows himself afraid of crowding,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-73">So at the sight of me stood motionless
Those fortunate spirits, all of them, as if
Oblivious to go and make them fair.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-76">One from among them saw I coming forward,
As to embrace me, with such great affection,
That it incited me to do the like.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-79">O empty shadows, save in aspect only!
Three times behind it did I clasp my hands,
As oft returned with them to my own breast!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-82">I think with wonder I depicted me;
Whereat the shadow smiled and backward drew;
And I, pursuing it, pressed farther forward.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-85">Gently it said that I should stay my steps;
Then knew I who it was, and I entreated
That it would stop awhile to speak with me.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-88">It made reply to me: "Even as I loved thee
In mortal body, so I love thee free;
Therefore I stop; but wherefore goest thou?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-91">"My own Casella! to return once more
There where I am, I make this journey," said I;
"But how from thee has so much time be taken?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-94">And he to me: "No outrage has been done me,
If he who takes both when and whom he pleases
Has many times denied to me this passage,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-97">For of a righteous will his own is made.
He, sooth to say, for three months past has taken
Whoever wished to enter with all peace;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-100">Whence I, who now had turned unto that shore
Where salt the waters of the Tiber grow,
Benignantly by him have been received.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-103">Unto that outlet now his wing is pointed,
Because for evermore assemble there
Those who tow'rds Acheron do not descend."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-106">And I: "If some new law take not from thee
Memory or practice of the song of love,
Which used to quiet in me all my longings,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-109">Thee may it please to comfort therewithal
Somewhat this soul of mine, that with its body
Hitherward coming is so much distressed."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-112">"Love, that within my mind discourses with me,"
Forthwith began he so melodiously,
The melody within me still is sounding.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-115">My Master, and myself, and all that people
Which with him were, appeared as satisfied
As if naught else might touch the mind of any.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-118">We all of us were moveless and attentive
Unto his notes; and lo! the grave old man,
Exclaiming: "What is this, ye laggard spirits?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-121">What negligence, what standing still is this?
Run to the mountain to strip off the slough,
That lets not God be manifest to you."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-124">Even as when, collecting grain or tares,
The doves, together at their pasture met,
Quiet, nor showing their accustomed pride,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-127">If aught appear of which they are afraid,
Upon a sudden leave their food alone,
Because they are assailed by greater care;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-130">So that fresh company did I behold
The song relinquish, and go tow'rds the hill,
As one who goes, and knows not whitherward;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ii-133">Nor was our own departure less in haste.
</p>

## Purgatorio - Canto III

<p id="purgatorio-iii-1">Inasmuch as the instantaneous flight
Had scattered them asunder o'er the plain,
Turned to the mountain whither reason spurs us,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-4">I pressed me close unto my faithful comrade,
And how without him had I kept my course?
Who would have led me up along the mountain?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-7">He seemed to me within himself remorseful;
O noble conscience, and without a stain,
How sharp a sting is trivial fault to thee!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-10">After his feet had laid aside the haste
Which mars the dignity of every act,
My mind, that hitherto had been restrained,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-13">Let loose its faculties as if delighted,
And I my sight directed to the hill
That highest tow'rds the heaven uplifts itself.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-16">The sun, that in our rear was flaming red,
Was broken in front of me into the figure
Which had in me the stoppage of its rays;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-19">Unto one side I turned me, with the fear
Of being left alone, when I beheld
Only in front of me the ground obscured.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-22">"Why dost thou still mistrust?" my Comforter
Began to say to me turned wholly round;
"Dost thou not think me with thee, and that I guide thee?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-25">'Tis evening there already where is buried
The body within which I cast a shadow;
'Tis from Brundusium ta'en, and Naples has it.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-28">Now if in front of me no shadow fall,
Marvel not at it more than at the heavens,
Because one ray impedeth not another
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-31">To suffer torments, both of cold and heat,
Bodies like this that Power provides, which wills
That how it works be not unveiled to us.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-34">Insane is he who hopeth that our reason
Can traverse the illimitable way,
Which the one Substance in three Persons follows!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-37">Mortals, remain contented at the 'Quia;'
For if ye had been able to see all,
No need there were for Mary to give birth;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-40">And ye have seen desiring without fruit,
Those whose desire would have been quieted,
Which evermore is given them for a grief.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-43">I speak of Aristotle and of Plato,
And many others;"--and here bowed his head,
And more he said not, and remained disturbed.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-46">We came meanwhile unto the mountain's foot;
There so precipitate we found the rock,
That nimble legs would there have been in vain.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-49">'Twixt Lerici and Turbia, the most desert,
The most secluded pathway is a stair
Easy and open, if compared with that.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-52">"Who knoweth now upon which hand the hill
Slopes down," my Master said, his footsteps staying,
"So that who goeth without wings may mount?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-55">And while he held his eyes upon the ground
Examining the nature of the path,
And I was looking up around the rock,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-58">On the left hand appeared to me a throng
Of souls, that moved their feet in our direction,
And did not seem to move, they came so slowly.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-61">"Lift up thine eyes," I to the Master said;
"Behold, on this side, who will give us counsel,
If thou of thine own self can have it not."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-64">Then he looked at me, and with frank expression
Replied: "Let us go there, for they come slowly,
And thou be steadfast in thy hope, sweet son."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-67">Still was that people as far off from us,
After a thousand steps of ours I say,
As a good thrower with his hand would reach,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-70">When they all crowded unto the hard masses
Of the high bank, and motionless stood and close,
As he stands still to look who goes in doubt.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-73">"O happy dead!  O spirits elect already!"
Virgilius made beginning, "by that peace
Which I believe is waiting for you all,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-76">Tell us upon what side the mountain slopes,
So that the going up be possible,
For to lose time irks him most who most knows."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-79">As sheep come issuing forth from out the fold
By ones and twos and threes, and the others stand
Timidly, holding down their eyes and nostrils,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-82">And what the foremost does the others do,
Huddling themselves against her, if she stop,
Simple and quiet and the wherefore know not;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-85">So moving to approach us thereupon
I saw the leader of that fortunate flock,
Modest in face and dignified in gait.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-88">As soon as those in the advance saw broken
The light upon the ground at my right side,
So that from me the shadow reached the rock,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-91">They stopped, and backward drew themselves somewhat;
And all the others, who came after them,
Not knowing why nor wherefore, did the same.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-94">"Without your asking, I confess to you
This is a human body which you see,
Whereby the sunshine on the ground is cleft.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-97">Marvel ye not thereat, but be persuaded
That not without a power which comes from Heaven
Doth he endeavour to surmount this wall."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-100">The Master thus; and said those worthy people:
"Return ye then, and enter in before us,"
Making a signal with the back o' the hand
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-103">And one of them began: "Whoe'er thou art,
Thus going turn thine eyes, consider well
If e'er thou saw me in the other world."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-106">I turned me tow'rds him, and looked at him closely;
Blond was he, beautiful, and of noble aspect,
But one of his eyebrows had a blow divided.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-109">When with humility I had disclaimed
E'er having seen him, "Now behold!" he said,
And showed me high upon his breast a wound.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-112">Then said he with a smile: "I am Manfredi,
The grandson of the Empress Costanza;
Therefore, when thou returnest, I beseech thee
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-115">Go to my daughter beautiful, the mother
Of Sicily's honour and of Aragon's,
And the truth tell her, if aught else be told.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-118">After I had my body lacerated
By these two mortal stabs, I gave myself
Weeping to Him, who willingly doth pardon.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-121">Horrible my iniquities had been;
But Infinite Goodness hath such ample arms,
That it receives whatever turns to it.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-124">Had but Cosenza's pastor, who in chase
Of me was sent by Clement at that time,
In God read understandingly this page,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-127">The bones of my dead body still would be
At the bridge-head, near unto Benevento,
Under the safeguard of the heavy cairn.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-130">Now the rain bathes and moveth them the wind,
Beyond the realm, almost beside the Verde,
Where he transported them with tapers quenched.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-133">By malison of theirs is not so lost
Eternal Love, that it cannot return,
So long as hope has anything of green.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-136">True is it, who in contumacy dies
Of Holy Church, though penitent at last,
Must wait upon the outside this bank
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-139">Thirty times told the time that he has been
In his presumption, unless such decree
Shorter by means of righteous prayers become.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-142">See now if thou hast power to make me happy,
By making known unto my good Costanza
How thou hast seen me, and this ban beside,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iii-145">For those on earth can much advance us here."
</p>

## Purgatorio - Canto IV

<p id="purgatorio-iv-1">Whenever by delight or else by pain,
That seizes any faculty of ours,
Wholly to that the soul collects itself,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-4">It seemeth that no other power it heeds;
And this against that error is which thinks
One soul above another kindles in us.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-7">And hence, whenever aught is heard or seen
Which keeps the soul intently bent upon it,
Time passes on, and we perceive it not,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-10">Because one faculty is that which listens,
And other that which the soul keeps entire;
This is as if in bonds, and that is free.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-13">Of this I had experience positive
In hearing and in gazing at that spirit;
For fifty full degrees uprisen was
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-16">The sun, and I had not perceived it, when
We came to where those souls with one accord
Cried out unto us: "Here is what you ask."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-19">A greater opening ofttimes hedges up
With but a little forkful of his thorns
The villager, what time the grape imbrowns,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-22">Than was the passage-way through which ascended
Only my Leader and myself behind him,
After that company departed from us.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-25">One climbs Sanleo and descends in Noli,
And mounts the summit of Bismantova,
With feet alone; but here one needs must fly;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-28">With the swift pinions and the plumes I say
Of great desire, conducted after him
Who gave me hope, and made a light for me.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-31">We mounted upward through the rifted rock,
And on each side the border pressed upon us,
And feet and hands the ground beneath required.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-34">When we were come upon the upper rim
Of the high bank, out on the open slope,
"My Master," said I, "what way shall we take?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-37">And he to me: "No step of thine descend;
Still up the mount behind me win thy way,
Till some sage escort shall appear to us."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-40">The summit was so high it vanquished sight,
And the hillside precipitous far more
Than line from middle quadrant to the centre.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-43">Spent with fatigue was I, when I began:
"O my sweet Father! turn thee and behold
How I remain alone, unless thou stay!"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-46">"O son," he said, "up yonder drag thyself,"
Pointing me to a terrace somewhat higher,
Which on that side encircles all the hill.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-49">These words of his so spurred me on, that I
Strained every nerve, behind him scrambling up,
Until the circle was beneath my feet.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-52">Thereon ourselves we seated both of us
Turned to the East, from which we had ascended,
For all men are delighted to look back.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-55">To the low shores mine eyes I first directed,
Then to the sun uplifted them, and wondered
That on the left hand we were smitten by it.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-58">The Poet well perceived that I was wholly
Bewildered at the chariot of the light,
Where 'twixt us and the Aquilon it entered.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-61">Whereon he said to me: "If Castor and Pollux
Were in the company of yonder mirror,
That up and down conducteth with its light,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-64">Thou wouldst behold the zodiac's jagged wheel
Revolving still more near unto the Bears,
Unless it swerved aside from its old track.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-67">How that may be wouldst thou have power to think,
Collected in thyself, imagine Zion
Together with this mount on earth to stand,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-70">So that they both one sole horizon have,
And hemispheres diverse; whereby the road
Which Phaeton, alas! knew not to drive,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-73">Thou'lt see how of necessity must pass
This on one side, when that upon the other,
If thine intelligence right clearly heed."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-76">"Truly, my Master," said I, "never yet
Saw I so clearly as I now discern,
There where my wit appeared incompetent,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-79">That the mid-circle of supernal motion,
Which in some art is the Equator called,
And aye remains between the Sun and Winter,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-82">For reason which thou sayest, departeth hence
Tow'rds the Septentrion, what time the Hebrews
Beheld it tow'rds the region of the heat.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-85">But, if it pleaseth thee, I fain would learn
How far we have to go; for the hill rises
Higher than eyes of mine have power to rise."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-88">And he to me: "This mount is such, that ever
At the beginning down below 'tis tiresome,
And aye the more one climbs, the less it hurts.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-91">Therefore, when it shall seem so pleasant to thee,
That going up shall be to thee as easy
As going down the current in a boat,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-94">Then at this pathway's ending thou wilt be;
There to repose thy panting breath expect;
No more I answer; and this I know for true."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-97">And as he finished uttering these words,
A voice close by us sounded: "Peradventure
Thou wilt have need of sitting down ere that."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-100">At sound thereof each one of us turned round,
And saw upon the left hand a great rock,
Which neither I nor he before had noticed.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-103">Thither we drew; and there were persons there
Who in the shadow stood behind the rock,
As one through indolence is wont to stand.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-106">And one of them, who seemed to me fatigued,
Was sitting down, and both his knees embraced,
Holding his face low down between them bowed.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-109">"O my sweet Lord," I said, "do turn thine eye
On him who shows himself more negligent
Then even Sloth herself his sister were."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-112">Then he turned round to us, and he gave heed,
Just lifting up his eyes above his thigh,
And said: "Now go thou up, for thou art valiant."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-115">Then knew I who he was; and the distress,
That still a little did my breathing quicken,
My going to him hindered not; and after
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-118">I came to him he hardly raised his head,
Saying: "Hast thou seen clearly how the sun
O'er thy left shoulder drives his chariot?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-121">His sluggish attitude and his curt words
A little unto laughter moved my lips;
Then I began: "Belacqua, I grieve not
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-124">For thee henceforth; but tell me, wherefore seated
In this place art thou?  Waitest thou an escort?
Or has thy usual habit seized upon thee?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-127">And he: "O brother, what's the use of climbing?
Since to my torment would not let me go
The Angel of God, who sitteth at the gate.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-130">First heaven must needs so long revolve me round
Outside thereof, as in my life it did,
Since the good sighs I to the end postponed,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-133">Unless, e'er that, some prayer may bring me aid
Which rises from a heart that lives in grace;
What profit others that in heaven are heard not?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-136">Meanwhile the Poet was before me mounting,
And saying: "Come now; see the sun has touched
Meridian, and from the shore the night
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-iv-139">Covers already with her foot Morocco."
</p>

## Purgatorio - Canto V

<p id="purgatorio-v-1">I had already from those shades departed,
And followed in the footsteps of my Guide,
When from behind, pointing his finger at me,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-4">One shouted: "See, it seems as if shone not
The sunshine on the left of him below,
And like one living seems he to conduct him."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-7">Mine eyes I turned at utterance of these words,
And saw them watching with astonishment
But me, but me, and the light which was broken!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-10">"Why doth thy mind so occupy itself,"
The Master said, "that thou thy pace dost slacken?
What matters it to thee what here is whispered?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-13">Come after me, and let the people talk;
Stand like a steadfast tower, that never wags
Its top for all the blowing of the winds;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-16">For evermore the man in whom is springing
Thought upon thought, removes from him the mark,
Because the force of one the other weakens."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-19">What could I say in answer but "I come"?
I said it somewhat with that colour tinged
Which makes a man of pardon sometimes worthy.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-22">Meanwhile along the mountain-side across
Came people in advance of us a little,
Singing the Miserere verse by verse.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-25">When they became aware I gave no place
For passage of the sunshine through my body,
They changed their song into a long, hoarse "Oh!"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-28">And two of them, in form of messengers,
Ran forth to meet us, and demanded of us,
"Of your condition make us cognisant."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-31">And said my Master: "Ye can go your way
And carry back again to those who sent you,
That this one's body is of very flesh.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-34">If they stood still because they saw his shadow,
As I suppose, enough is answered them;
Him let them honour, it may profit them."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-37">Vapours enkindled saw I ne'er so swiftly
At early nightfall cleave the air serene,
Nor, at the set of sun, the clouds of August,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-40">But upward they returned in briefer time,
And, on arriving, with the others wheeled
Tow'rds us, like troops that run without a rein.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-43">"This folk that presses unto us is great,
And cometh to implore thee," said the Poet;
"So still go onward, and in going listen."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-46">"O soul that goest to beatitude
With the same members wherewith thou wast born,"
Shouting they came, "a little stay thy steps,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-49">Look, if thou e'er hast any of us seen,
So that o'er yonder thou bear news of him;
Ah, why dost thou go on?  Ah, why not stay?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-52">Long since we all were slain by violence,
And sinners even to the latest hour;
Then did a light from heaven admonish us,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-55">So that, both penitent and pardoning, forth
From life we issued reconciled to God,
Who with desire to see Him stirs our hearts."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-58">And I: "Although I gaze into your faces,
No one I recognize; but if may please you
Aught I have power to do, ye well-born spirits,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-61">Speak ye, and I will do it, by that peace
Which, following the feet of such a Guide,
From world to world makes itself sought by me."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-64">And one began: "Each one has confidence
In thy good offices without an oath,
Unless the I cannot cut off the I will;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-67">Whence I, who speak alone before the others,
Pray thee, if ever thou dost see the land
That 'twixt Romagna lies and that of Charles,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-70">Thou be so courteous to me of thy prayers
In Fano, that they pray for me devoutly,
That I may purge away my grave offences.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-73">From thence was I; but the deep wounds, through which
Issued the blood wherein I had my seat,
Were dealt me in bosom of the Antenori,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-76">There where I thought to be the most secure;
'Twas he of Este had it done, who held me
In hatred far beyond what justice willed.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-79">But if towards the Mira I had fled,
When I was overtaken at Oriaco,
I still should be o'er yonder where men breathe.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-82">I ran to the lagoon, and reeds and mire
Did so entangle me I fell, and saw there
A lake made from my veins upon the ground."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-85">Then said another: "Ah, be that desire
Fulfilled that draws thee to the lofty mountain,
As thou with pious pity aidest mine.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-88">I was of Montefeltro, and am Buonconte;
Giovanna, nor none other cares for me;
Hence among these I go with downcast front."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-91">And I to him: "What violence or what chance
Led thee astray so far from Campaldino,
That never has thy sepulture been known?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-94">"Oh," he replied, "at Casentino's foot
A river crosses named Archiano, born
Above the Hermitage in Apennine.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-97">There where the name thereof becometh void
Did I arrive, pierced through and through the throat,
Fleeing on foot, and bloodying the plain;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-100">There my sight lost I, and my utterance
Ceased in the name of Mary, and thereat
I fell, and tenantless my flesh remained.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-103">Truth will I speak, repeat it to the living;
God's Angel took me up, and he of hell
Shouted: 'O thou from heaven, why dost thou rob me?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-106">Thou bearest away the eternal part of him,
For one poor little tear, that takes him from me;
But with the rest I'll deal in other fashion!'
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-109">Well knowest thou how in the air is gathered
That humid vapour which to water turns,
Soon as it rises where the cold doth grasp it.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-112">He joined that evil will, which aye seeks evil,
To intellect, and moved the mist and wind
By means of power, which his own nature gave;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-115">Thereafter, when the day was spent, the valley
From Pratomagno to the great yoke covered
With fog, and made the heaven above intent,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-118">So that the pregnant air to water changed;
Down fell the rain, and to the gullies came
Whate'er of it earth tolerated not;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-121">And as it mingled with the mighty torrents,
Towards the royal river with such speed
It headlong rushed, that nothing held it back.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-124">My frozen body near unto its outlet
The robust Archian found, and into Arno
Thrust it, and loosened from my breast the cross
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-127">I made of me, when agony o'ercame me;
It rolled me on the banks and on the bottom,
Then with its booty covered and begirt me."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-130">"Ah, when thou hast returned unto the world,
And rested thee from thy long journeying,"
After the second followed the third spirit,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-133">"Do thou remember me who am the Pia;
Siena made me, unmade me Maremma;
He knoweth it, who had encircled first,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-v-136">Espousing me, my finger with his gem."
</p>

## Purgatorio - Canto VI

<p id="purgatorio-vi-1">Whene'er is broken up the game of Zara,
He who has lost remains behind despondent,
The throws repeating, and in sadness learns;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-4">The people with the other all depart;
One goes in front, and one behind doth pluck him,
And at his side one brings himself to mind;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-7">He pauses not, and this and that one hears;
They crowd no more to whom his hand he stretches,
And from the throng he thus defends himself.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-10">Even such was I in that dense multitude,
Turning to them this way and that my face,
And, promising, I freed myself therefrom.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-13">There was the Aretine, who from the arms
Untamed of Ghin di Tacco had his death,
And he who fleeing from pursuit was drowned.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-16">There was imploring with his hands outstretched
Frederick Novello, and that one of Pisa
Who made the good Marzucco seem so strong.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-19">I saw Count Orso; and the soul divided
By hatred and by envy from its body,
As it declared, and not for crime committed,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-22">Pierre de la Brosse I say; and here provide
While still on earth the Lady of Brabant,
So that for this she be of no worse flock!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-25">As soon as I was free from all those shades
Who only prayed that some one else may pray,
So as to hasten their becoming holy,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-28">Began I: "It appears that thou deniest,
O light of mine, expressly in some text,
That orison can bend decree of Heaven;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-31">And ne'ertheless these people pray for this.
Might then their expectation bootless be?
Or is to me thy saying not quite clear?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-34">And he to me: "My writing is explicit,
And not fallacious is the hope of these,
If with sane intellect 'tis well regarded;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-37">For top of judgment doth not vail itself,
Because the fire of love fulfils at once
What he must satisfy who here installs him.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-40">And there, where I affirmed that proposition,
Defect was not amended by a prayer,
Because the prayer from God was separate.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-43">Verily, in so deep a questioning
Do not decide, unless she tell it thee,
Who light 'twixt truth and intellect shall be.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-46">I know not if thou understand; I speak
Of Beatrice; her shalt thou see above,
Smiling and happy, on this mountain's top."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-49">And I: "Good Leader, let us make more haste,
For I no longer tire me as before;
And see, e'en now the hill a shadow casts."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-52">"We will go forward with this day" he answered,
"As far as now is possible for us;
But otherwise the fact is than thou thinkest.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-55">Ere thou art up there, thou shalt see return
Him, who now hides himself behind the hill,
So that thou dost not interrupt his rays.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-58">But yonder there behold! a soul that stationed
All, all alone is looking hitherward;
It will point out to us the quickest way."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-61">We came up unto it; O Lombard soul,
How lofty and disdainful thou didst bear thee,
And grand and slow in moving of thine eyes!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-64">Nothing whatever did it say to us,
But let us go our way, eying us only
After the manner of a couchant lion;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-67">Still near to it Virgilius drew, entreating
That it would point us out the best ascent;
And it replied not unto his demand,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-70">But of our native land and of our life
It questioned us; and the sweet Guide began:
"Mantua,"--and the shade, all in itself recluse,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-73">Rose tow'rds him from the place where first it was,
Saying: "O Mantuan, I am Sordello
Of thine own land!" and one embraced the other.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-76">Ah! servile Italy, grief's hostelry!
A ship without a pilot in great tempest!
No Lady thou of Provinces, but brothel!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-79">That noble soul was so impatient, only
At the sweet sound of his own native land,
To make its citizen glad welcome there;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-82">And now within thee are not without war
Thy living ones, and one doth gnaw the other
Of those whom one wall and one fosse shut in!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-85">Search, wretched one, all round about the shores
Thy seaboard, and then look within thy bosom,
If any part of thee enjoyeth peace!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-88">What boots it, that for thee Justinian
The bridle mend, if empty be the saddle?
Withouten this the shame would be the less.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-91">Ah! people, thou that oughtest to be devout,
And to let Caesar sit upon the saddle,
If well thou hearest what God teacheth thee,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-94">Behold how fell this wild beast has become,
Being no longer by the spur corrected,
Since thou hast laid thy hand upon the bridle.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-97">O German Albert! who abandonest
Her that has grown recalcitrant and savage,
And oughtest to bestride her saddle-bow,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-100">May a just judgment from the stars down fall
Upon thy blood, and be it new and open,
That thy successor may have fear thereof;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-103">Because thy father and thyself have suffered,
By greed of those transalpine lands distrained,
The garden of the empire to be waste.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-106">Come and behold Montecchi and Cappelletti,
Monaldi and Fillippeschi, careless man!
Those sad already, and these doubt-depressed!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-109">Come, cruel one! come and behold the oppression
Of thy nobility, and cure their wounds,
And thou shalt see how safe is Santafiore!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-112">Come and behold thy Rome, that is lamenting,
Widowed, alone, and day and night exclaims,
"My Caesar, why hast thou forsaken me?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-115">Come and behold how loving are the people;
And if for us no pity moveth thee,
Come and be made ashamed of thy renown!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-118">And if it lawful be, O Jove Supreme!
Who upon earth for us wast crucified,
Are thy just eyes averted otherwhere?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-121">Or preparation is 't, that, in the abyss
Of thine own counsel, for some good thou makest
From our perception utterly cut off?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-124">For all the towns of Italy are full
Of tyrants, and becometh a Marcellus
Each peasant churl who plays the partisan!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-127">My Florence! well mayst thou contented be
With this digression, which concerns thee not,
Thanks to thy people who such forethought take!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-130">Many at heart have justice, but shoot slowly,
That unadvised they come not to the bow,
But on their very lips thy people have it!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-133">Many refuse to bear the common burden;
But thy solicitous people answereth
Without being asked, and crieth: "I submit."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-136">Now be thou joyful, for thou hast good reason;
Thou affluent, thou in peace, thou full of wisdom!
If I speak true, the event conceals it not.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-139">Athens and Lacedaemon, they who made
The ancient laws, and were so civilized,
Made towards living well a little sign
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-142">Compared with thee, who makest such fine-spun
Provisions, that to middle of November
Reaches not what thou in October spinnest.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-145">How oft, within the time of thy remembrance,
Laws, money, offices, and usages
Hast thou remodelled, and renewed thy members?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-148">And if thou mind thee well, and see the light,
Thou shalt behold thyself like a sick woman,
Who cannot find repose upon her down,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vi-151">But by her tossing wardeth off her pain.
</p>

## Purgatorio - Canto VII

<p id="purgatorio-vii-1">After the gracious and glad salutations
Had three and four times been reiterated,
Sordello backward drew and said, "Who are you?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-4">"Or ever to this mountain were directed
The souls deserving to ascend to God,
My bones were buried by Octavian.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-7">I am Virgilius; and for no crime else
Did I lose heaven, than for not having faith;"
In this wise then my Leader made reply.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-10">As one who suddenly before him sees
Something whereat he marvels, who believes
And yet does not, saying, "It is! it is not!"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-13">So he appeared; and then bowed down his brow,
And with humility returned towards him,
And, where inferiors embrace, embraced him.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-16">"O glory of the Latians, thou," he said,
"Through whom our language showed what it could do
O pride eternal of the place I came from,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-19">What merit or what grace to me reveals thee?
If I to hear thy words be worthy, tell me
If thou dost come from Hell, and from what cloister."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-22">"Through all the circles of the doleful realm,"
Responded he, "have I come hitherward;
Heaven's power impelled me, and with that I come.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-25">I by not doing, not by doing, lost
The sight of that high sun which thou desirest,
And which too late by me was recognized.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-28">A place there is below not sad with torments,
But darkness only, where the lamentations
Have not the sound of wailing, but are sighs.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-31">There dwell I with the little innocents
Snatched by the teeth of Death, or ever they
Were from our human sinfulness exempt.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-34">There dwell I among those who the three saintly
Virtues did not put on, and without vice
The others knew and followed all of them.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-37">But if thou know and can, some indication
Give us by which we may the sooner come
Where Purgatory has its right beginning."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-40">He answered: "No fixed place has been assigned us;
'Tis lawful for me to go up and round;
So far as I can go, as guide I join thee.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-43">But see already how the day declines,
And to go up by night we are not able;
Therefore 'tis well to think of some fair sojourn.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-46">Souls are there on the right hand here withdrawn;
If thou permit me I will lead thee to them,
And thou shalt know them not without delight."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-49">"How is this?" was the answer; "should one wish
To mount by night would he prevented be
By others? or mayhap would not have power?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-52">And on the ground the good Sordello drew
His finger, saying, "See, this line alone
Thou couldst not pass after the sun is gone;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-55">Not that aught else would hindrance give, however,
To going up, save the nocturnal darkness;
This with the want of power the will perplexes.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-58">We might indeed therewith return below,
And, wandering, walk the hill-side round about,
While the horizon holds the day imprisoned."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-61">Thereon my Lord, as if in wonder, said:
"Do thou conduct us thither, where thou sayest
That we can take delight in tarrying."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-64">Little had we withdrawn us from that place,
When I perceived the mount was hollowed out
In fashion as the valleys here are hollowed.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-67">"Thitherward," said that shade, "will we repair,
Where of itself the hill-side makes a lap,
And there for the new day will we await."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-70">'Twixt hill and plain there was a winding path
Which led us to the margin of that dell,
Where dies the border more than half away.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-73">Gold and fine silver, and scarlet and pearl-white,
The Indian wood resplendent and serene,
Fresh emerald the moment it is broken,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-76">By herbage and by flowers within that hollow
Planted, each one in colour would be vanquished,
As by its greater vanquished is the less.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-79">Nor in that place had nature painted only,
But of the sweetness of a thousand odours
Made there a mingled fragrance and unknown.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-82">"Salve Regina," on the green and flowers
There seated, singing, spirits I beheld,
Which were not visible outside the valley.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-85">"Before the scanty sun now seeks his nest,"
Began the Mantuan who had led us thither,
"Among them do not wish me to conduct you.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-88">Better from off this ledge the acts and faces
Of all of them will you discriminate,
Than in the plain below received among them.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-91">He who sits highest, and the semblance bears
Of having what he should have done neglected,
And to the others' song moves not his lips,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-94">Rudolph the Emperor was, who had the power
To heal the wounds that Italy have slain,
So that through others slowly she revives.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-97">The other, who in look doth comfort him,
Governed the region where the water springs,
The Moldau bears the Elbe, and Elbe the sea.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-100">His name was Ottocar; and in swaddling-clothes
Far better he than bearded Winceslaus
His son, who feeds in luxury and ease.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-103">And the small-nosed, who close in council seems
With him that has an aspect so benign,
Died fleeing and disflowering the lily;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-106">Look there, how he is beating at his breast!
Behold the other one, who for his cheek
Sighing has made of his own palm a bed;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-109">Father and father-in-law of France's Pest
Are they, and know his vicious life and lewd,
And hence proceeds the grief that so doth pierce them.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-112">He who appears so stalwart, and chimes in,
Singing, with that one of the manly nose,
The cord of every valour wore begirt;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-115">And if as King had after him remained
The stripling who in rear of him is sitting,
Well had the valour passed from vase to vase,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-118">Which cannot of the other heirs be said.
Frederick and Jacomo possess the realms,
But none the better heritage possesses.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-121">Not oftentimes upriseth through the branches
The probity of man; and this He wills
Who gives it, so that we may ask of Him.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-124">Eke to the large-nosed reach my words, no less
Than to the other, Pier, who with him sings;
Whence Provence and Apulia grieve already
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-127">The plant is as inferior to its seed,
As more than Beatrice and Margaret
Costanza boasteth of her husband still.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-130">Behold the monarch of the simple life,
Harry of England, sitting there alone;
He in his branches has a better issue.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-133">He who the lowest on the ground among them
Sits looking upward, is the Marquis William,
For whose sake Alessandria and her war
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-vii-136">Make Monferrat and Canavese weep."
</p>

## Purgatorio - Canto VIII

<p id="purgatorio-viii-1">'Twas now the hour that turneth back desire
In those who sail the sea, and melts the heart,
The day they've said to their sweet friends farewell,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-4">And the new pilgrim penetrates with love,
If he doth hear from far away a bell
That seemeth to deplore the dying day,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-7">When I began to make of no avail
My hearing, and to watch one of the souls
Uprisen, that begged attention with its hand.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-10">It joined and lifted upward both its palms,
Fixing its eyes upon the orient,
As if it said to God, "Naught else I care for."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-13">"Te lucis ante" so devoutly issued
Forth from its mouth, and with such dulcet notes,
It made me issue forth from my own mind.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-16">And then the others, sweetly and devoutly,
Accompanied it through all the hymn entire,
Having their eyes on the supernal wheels.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-19">Here, Reader, fix thine eyes well on the truth,
For now indeed so subtile is the veil,
Surely to penetrate within is easy.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-22">I saw that army of the gentle-born
Thereafterward in silence upward gaze,
As if in expectation, pale and humble;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-25">And from on high come forth and down descend,
I saw two Angels with two flaming swords,
Truncated and deprived of their points.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-28">Green as the little leaflets just now born
Their garments were, which, by their verdant pinions
Beaten and blown abroad, they trailed behind.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-31">One just above us came to take his station,
And one descended to the opposite bank,
So that the people were contained between them.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-34">Clearly in them discerned I the blond head;
But in their faces was the eye bewildered,
As faculty confounded by excess.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-37">"From Mary's bosom both of them have come,"
Sordello said, "as guardians of the valley
Against the serpent, that will come anon."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-40">Whereupon I, who knew not by what road,
Turned round about, and closely drew myself,
Utterly frozen, to the faithful shoulders.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-43">And once again Sordello: "Now descend we
'Mid the grand shades, and we will speak to them;
Right pleasant will it be for them to see you."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-46">Only three steps I think that I descended,
And was below, and saw one who was looking
Only at me, as if he fain would know me.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-49">Already now the air was growing dark,
But not so that between his eyes and mine
It did not show what it before locked up.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-52">Tow'rds me he moved, and I tow'rds him did move;
Noble Judge Nino! how it me delighted,
When I beheld thee not among the damned!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-55">No greeting fair was left unsaid between us;
Then asked he: "How long is it since thou camest
O'er the far waters to the mountain's foot?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-58">"Oh!" said I to him, "through the dismal places
I came this morn; and am in the first life,
Albeit the other, going thus, I gain."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-61">And on the instant my reply was heard,
He and Sordello both shrank back from me,
Like people who are suddenly bewildered.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-64">One to Virgilius, and the other turned
To one who sat there, crying, "Up, Currado!
Come and behold what God in grace has willed!"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-67">Then, turned to me: "By that especial grace
Thou owest unto Him, who so conceals
His own first wherefore, that it has no ford,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-70">When thou shalt be beyond the waters wide,
Tell my Giovanna that she pray for me,
Where answer to the innocent is made.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-73">I do not think her mother loves me more,
Since she has laid aside her wimple white,
Which she, unhappy, needs must wish again.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-76">Through her full easily is comprehended
How long in woman lasts the fire of love,
If eye or touch do not relight it often.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-79">So fair a hatchment will not make for her
The Viper marshalling the Milanese
A-field, as would have made Gallura's Cock."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-82">In this wise spake he, with the stamp impressed
Upon his aspect of that righteous zeal
Which measurably burneth in the heart.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-85">My greedy eyes still wandered up to heaven,
Still to that point where slowest are the stars,
Even as a wheel the nearest to its axle.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-88">And my Conductor: "Son, what dost thou gaze at
Up there?"  And I to him: "At those three torches
With which this hither pole is all on fire."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-91">And he to me: "The four resplendent stars
Thou sawest this morning are down yonder low,
And these have mounted up to where those were."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-94">As he was speaking, to himself Sordello
Drew him, and said, "Lo there our Adversary!"
And pointed with his finger to look thither.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-97">Upon the side on which the little valley
No barrier hath, a serpent was; perchance
The same which gave to Eve the bitter food.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-100">'Twixt grass and flowers came on the evil streak,
Turning at times its head about, and licking
Its back like to a beast that smoothes itself.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-103">I did not see, and therefore cannot say
How the celestial falcons 'gan to move,
But well I saw that they were both in motion.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-106">Hearing the air cleft by their verdant wings,
The serpent fled, and round the Angels wheeled,
Up to their stations flying back alike.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-109">The shade that to the Judge had near approached
When he had called, throughout that whole assault
Had not a moment loosed its gaze on me.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-112">"So may the light that leadeth thee on high
Find in thine own free-will as much of wax
As needful is up to the highest azure,"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-115">Began it, "if some true intelligence
Of Valdimagra or its neighbourhood
Thou knowest, tell it me, who once was great there.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-118">Currado Malaspina was I called;
I'm not the elder, but from him descended;
To mine I bore the love which here refineth."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-121">"O," said I unto him, "through your domains
I never passed, but where is there a dwelling
Throughout all Europe, where they are not known?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-124">That fame, which doeth honour to your house,
Proclaims its Signors and proclaims its land,
So that he knows of them who ne'er was there.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-127">And, as I hope for heaven, I swear to you
Your honoured family in naught abates
The glory of the purse and of the sword.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-130">It is so privileged by use and nature,
That though a guilty head misguide the world,
Sole it goes right, and scorns the evil way."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-133">And he: "Now go; for the sun shall not lie
Seven times upon the pillow which the Ram
With all his four feet covers and bestrides,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-136">Before that such a courteous opinion
Shall in the middle of thy head be nailed
With greater nails than of another's speech,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-viii-139">Unless the course of justice standeth still."
</p>

## Purgatorio - Canto IX

<p id="purgatorio-ix-1">The concubine of old Tithonus now
Gleamed white upon the eastern balcony,
Forth from the arms of her sweet paramour;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-4">With gems her forehead all relucent was,
Set in the shape of that cold animal
Which with its tail doth smite amain the nations,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-7">And of the steps, with which she mounts, the Night
Had taken two in that place where we were,
And now the third was bending down its wings;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-10">When I, who something had of Adam in me,
Vanquished by sleep, upon the grass reclined,
There were all five of us already sat.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-13">Just at the hour when her sad lay begins
The little swallow, near unto the morning,
Perchance in memory of her former woes,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-16">And when the mind of man, a wanderer
More from the flesh, and less by thought imprisoned,
Almost prophetic in its visions is,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-19">In dreams it seemed to me I saw suspended
An eagle in the sky, with plumes of gold,
With wings wide open, and intent to stoop,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-22">And this, it seemed to me, was where had been
By Ganymede his kith and kin abandoned,
When to the high consistory he was rapt.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-25">I thought within myself, perchance he strikes
From habit only here, and from elsewhere
Disdains to bear up any in his feet.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-28">Then wheeling somewhat more, it seemed to me,
Terrible as the lightning he descended,
And snatched me upward even to the fire.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-31">Therein it seemed that he and I were burning,
And the imagined fire did scorch me so,
That of necessity my sleep was broken.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-34">Not otherwise Achilles started up,
Around him turning his awakened eyes,
And knowing not the place in which he was,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-37">What time from Chiron stealthily his mother
Carried him sleeping in her arms to Scyros,
Wherefrom the Greeks withdrew him afterwards,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-40">Than I upstarted, when from off my face
Sleep fled away; and pallid I became,
As doth the man who freezes with affright.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-43">Only my Comforter was at my side,
And now the sun was more than two hours high,
And turned towards the sea-shore was my face.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-46">"Be not intimidated," said my Lord,
"Be reassured, for all is well with us;
Do not restrain, but put forth all thy strength.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-49">Thou hast at length arrived at Purgatory;
See there the cliff that closes it around;
See there the entrance, where it seems disjoined.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-52">Whilom at dawn, which doth precede the day,
When inwardly thy spirit was asleep
Upon the flowers that deck the land below,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-55">There came a Lady and said: 'I am Lucia;
Let me take this one up, who is asleep;
So will I make his journey easier for him.'
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-58">Sordello and the other noble shapes
Remained; she took thee, and, as day grew bright,
Upward she came, and I upon her footsteps.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-61">She laid thee here; and first her beauteous eyes
That open entrance pointed out to me;
Then she and sleep together went away."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-64">In guise of one whose doubts are reassured,
And who to confidence his fear doth change,
After the truth has been discovered to him,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-67">So did I change; and when without disquiet
My Leader saw me, up along the cliff
He moved, and I behind him, tow'rd the height.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-70">Reader, thou seest well how I exalt
My theme, and therefore if with greater art
I fortify it, marvel not thereat.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-73">Nearer approached we, and were in such place,
That there, where first appeared to me a rift
Like to a crevice that disparts a wall,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-76">I saw a portal, and three stairs beneath,
Diverse in colour, to go up to it,
And a gate-keeper, who yet spake no word.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-79">And as I opened more and more mine eyes,
I saw him seated on the highest stair,
Such in the face that I endured it not.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-82">And in his hand he had a naked sword,
Which so reflected back the sunbeams tow'rds us,
That oft in vain I lifted up mine eyes.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-85">"Tell it from where you are, what is't you wish?"
Began he to exclaim; "where is the escort?
Take heed your coming hither harm you not!"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-88">"A Lady of Heaven, with these things conversant,"
My Master answered him, "but even now
Said to us, 'Thither go; there is the portal.'"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-91">"And may she speed your footsteps in all good,"
Again began the courteous janitor;
"Come forward then unto these stairs of ours."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-94">Thither did we approach; and the first stair
Was marble white, so polished and so smooth,
I mirrored myself therein as I appear.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-97">The second, tinct of deeper hue than perse,
Was of a calcined and uneven stone,
Cracked all asunder lengthwise and across.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-100">The third, that uppermost rests massively,
Porphyry seemed to me, as flaming red
As blood that from a vein is spirting forth.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-103">Both of his feet was holding upon this
The Angel of God, upon the threshold seated,
Which seemed to me a stone of diamond.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-106">Along the three stairs upward with good will
Did my Conductor draw me, saying: "Ask
Humbly that he the fastening may undo."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-109">Devoutly at the holy feet I cast me,
For mercy's sake besought that he would open,
But first upon my breast three times I smote.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-112">Seven P's upon my forehead he described
With the sword's point, and, "Take heed that thou wash
These wounds, when thou shalt be within," he said.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-115">Ashes, or earth that dry is excavated,
Of the same colour were with his attire,
And from beneath it he drew forth two keys.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-118">One was of gold, and the other was of silver;
First with the white, and after with the yellow,
Plied he the door, so that I was content.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-121">"Whenever faileth either of these keys
So that it turn not rightly in the lock,"
He said to us, "this entrance doth not open.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-124">More precious one is, but the other needs
More art and intellect ere it unlock,
For it is that which doth the knot unloose.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-127">From Peter I have them; and he bade me err
Rather in opening than in keeping shut,
If people but fall down before my feet."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-130">Then pushed the portals of the sacred door,
Exclaiming: "Enter; but I give you warning
That forth returns whoever looks behind."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-133">And when upon their hinges were turned round
The swivels of that consecrated gate,
Which are of metal, massive and sonorous,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-136">Roared not so loud, nor so discordant seemed
Tarpeia, when was ta'en from it the good
Metellus, wherefore meagre it remained.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-139">At the first thunder-peal I turned attentive,
And "Te Deum laudamus" seemed to hear
In voices mingled with sweet melody.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-142">Exactly such an image rendered me
That which I heard, as we are wont to catch,
When people singing with the organ stand;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-ix-145">For now we hear, and now hear not, the words.
</p>

## Purgatorio - Canto X

<p id="purgatorio-x-1">When we had crossed the threshold of the door
Which the perverted love of souls disuses,
Because it makes the crooked way seem straight,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-4">Re-echoing I heard it closed again;
And if I had turned back mine eyes upon it,
What for my failing had been fit excuse?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-7">We mounted upward through a rifted rock,
Which undulated to this side and that,
Even as a wave receding and advancing.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-10">"Here it behoves us use a little art,"
Began my Leader, "to adapt ourselves
Now here, now there, to the receding side."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-13">And this our footsteps so infrequent made,
That sooner had the moon's decreasing disk
Regained its bed to sink again to rest,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-16">Than we were forth from out that needle's eye;
But when we free and in the open were,
There where the mountain backward piles itself,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-19">I wearied out, and both of us uncertain
About our way, we stopped upon a plain
More desolate than roads across the deserts.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-22">From where its margin borders on the void,
To foot of the high bank that ever rises,
A human body three times told would measure;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-25">And far as eye of mine could wing its flight,
Now on the left, and on the right flank now,
The same this cornice did appear to me.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-28">Thereon our feet had not been moved as yet,
When I perceived the embankment round about,
Which all right of ascent had interdicted,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-31">To be of marble white, and so adorned
With sculptures, that not only Polycletus,
But Nature's self, had there been put to shame.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-34">The Angel, who came down to earth with tidings
Of peace, that had been wept for many a year,
And opened Heaven from its long interdict,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-37">In front of us appeared so truthfully
There sculptured in a gracious attitude,
He did not seem an image that is silent.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-40">One would have sworn that he was saying, "Ave;"
For she was there in effigy portrayed
Who turned the key to ope the exalted love,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-43">And in her mien this language had impressed,
"Ecce ancilla Dei," as distinctly
As any figure stamps itself in wax.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-46">"Keep not thy mind upon one place alone,"
The gentle Master said, who had me standing
Upon that side where people have their hearts;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-49">Whereat I moved mine eyes, and I beheld
In rear of Mary, and upon that side
Where he was standing who conducted me,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-52">Another story on the rock imposed;
Wherefore I passed Virgilius and drew near,
So that before mine eyes it might be set.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-55">There sculptured in the self-same marble were
The cart and oxen, drawing the holy ark,
Wherefore one dreads an office not appointed.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-58">People appeared in front, and all of them
In seven choirs divided, of two senses
Made one say "No," the other, "Yes, they sing."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-61">Likewise unto the smoke of the frankincense,
Which there was imaged forth, the eyes and nose
Were in the yes and no discordant made.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-x-64">Preceded there the vessel benedight,
Dancing with girded loins, the humble Psalmist,
And more and less than King was he in this.
</p>
