<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-1">"O thou who art beyond the sacred river,"
Turning to me the point of her discourse,
That edgewise even had seemed to me so keen,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-4">She recommenced, continuing without pause,
"Say, say if this be true; to such a charge,
Thy own confession needs must be conjoined."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-7">My faculties were in so great confusion,
That the voice moved, but sooner was extinct
Than by its organs it was set at large.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-10">Awhile she waited; then she said: "What thinkest?
Answer me; for the mournful memories
In thee not yet are by the waters injured."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-13">Confusion and dismay together mingled
Forced such a Yes! from out my mouth, that sight
Was needful to the understanding of it.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-16">Even as a cross-bow breaks, when 'tis discharged
Too tensely drawn the bowstring and the bow,
And with less force the arrow hits the mark,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-19">So I gave way beneath that heavy burden,
Outpouring in a torrent tears and sighs,
And the voice flagged upon its passage forth.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-22">Whence she to me: "In those desires of mine
Which led thee to the loving of that good,
Beyond which there is nothing to aspire to,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-25">What trenches lying traverse or what chains
Didst thou discover, that of passing onward
Thou shouldst have thus despoiled thee of the hope?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-28">And what allurements or what vantages
Upon the forehead of the others showed,
That thou shouldst turn thy footsteps unto them?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-31">After the heaving of a bitter sigh,
Hardly had I the voice to make response,
And with fatigue my lips did fashion it.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-34">Weeping I said: "The things that present were
With their false pleasure turned aside my steps,
Soon as your countenance concealed itself."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-37">And she: "Shouldst thou be silent, or deny
What thou confessest, not less manifest
Would be thy fault, by such a Judge 'tis known.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-40">But when from one's own cheeks comes bursting forth
The accusal of the sin, in our tribunal
Against the edge the wheel doth turn itself.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-43">But still, that thou mayst feel a greater shame
For thy transgression, and another time
Hearing the Sirens thou mayst be more strong,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-46">Cast down the seed of weeping and attend;
So shalt thou hear, how in an opposite way
My buried flesh should have directed thee.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-49">Never to thee presented art or nature
Pleasure so great as the fair limbs wherein
I was enclosed, which scattered are in earth.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-52">And if the highest pleasure thus did fail thee
By reason of my death, what mortal thing
Should then have drawn thee into its desire?
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-55">Thou oughtest verily at the first shaft
Of things fallacious to have risen up
To follow me, who was no longer such.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-58">Thou oughtest not to have stooped thy pinions downward
To wait for further blows, or little girl,
Or other vanity of such brief use.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-61">The callow birdlet waits for two or three,
But to the eyes of those already fledged,
In vain the net is spread or shaft is shot."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-64">Even as children silent in their shame
Stand listening with their eyes upon the ground,
And conscious of their fault, and penitent;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-67">So was I standing; and she said: "If thou
In hearing sufferest pain, lift up thy beard
And thou shalt feel a greater pain in seeing."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-70">With less resistance is a robust holm
Uprooted, either by a native wind
Or else by that from regions of Iarbas,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-73">Than I upraised at her command my chin;
And when she by the beard the face demanded,
Well I perceived the venom of her meaning.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-76">And as my countenance was lifted up,
Mine eye perceived those creatures beautiful
Had rested from the strewing of the flowers;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-79">And, still but little reassured, mine eyes
Saw Beatrice turned round towards the monster,
That is one person only in two natures.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-82">Beneath her veil, beyond the margent green,
She seemed to me far more her ancient self
To excel, than others here, when she was here.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-85">So pricked me then the thorn of penitence,
That of all other things the one which turned me
Most to its love became the most my foe.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-88">Such self-conviction stung me at the heart
O'erpowered I fell, and what I then became
She knoweth who had furnished me the cause.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-91">Then, when the heart restored my outward sense,
The lady I had found alone, above me
I saw, and she was saying, "Hold me, hold me."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-94">Up to my throat she in the stream had drawn me,
And, dragging me behind her, she was moving
Upon the water lightly as a shuttle.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-97">When I was near unto the blessed shore,
"Asperges me," I heard so sweetly sung,
Remember it I cannot, much less write it.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-100">The beautiful lady opened wide her arms,
Embraced my head, and plunged me underneath,
Where I was forced to swallow of the water.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-103">Then forth she drew me, and all dripping brought
Into the dance of the four beautiful,
And each one with her arm did cover me.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-106">'We here are Nymphs, and in the Heaven are stars;
Ere Beatrice descended to the world,
We as her handmaids were appointed her.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-109">We'll lead thee to her eyes; but for the pleasant
Light that within them is, shall sharpen thine
The three beyond, who more profoundly look.'
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-112">Thus singing they began; and afterwards
Unto the Griffin's breast they led me with them,
Where Beatrice was standing, turned towards us.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-115">"See that thou dost not spare thine eyes," they said;
"Before the emeralds have we stationed thee,
Whence Love aforetime drew for thee his weapons."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-118">A thousand longings, hotter than the flame,
Fastened mine eyes upon those eyes relucent,
That still upon the Griffin steadfast stayed.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-121">As in a glass the sun, not otherwise
Within them was the twofold monster shining,
Now with the one, now with the other nature.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-124">Think, Reader, if within myself I marvelled,
When I beheld the thing itself stand still,
And in its image it transformed itself.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-127">While with amazement filled and jubilant,
My soul was tasting of the food, that while
It satisfies us makes us hunger for it,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-130">Themselves revealing of the highest rank
In bearing, did the other three advance,
Singing to their angelic saraband.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-133">"Turn, Beatrice, O turn thy holy eyes,"
Such was their song, "unto thy faithful one,
Who has to see thee ta'en so many steps.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-136">In grace do us the grace that thou unveil
Thy face to him, so that he may discern
The second beauty which thou dost conceal."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-139">O splendour of the living light eternal!
Who underneath the shadow of Parnassus
Has grown so pale, or drunk so at its cistern,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-142">He would not seem to have his mind encumbered
Striving to paint thee as thou didst appear,
Where the harmonious heaven o'ershadowed thee,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxi-145">When in the open air thou didst unveil?
</p>

## Purgatorio - Canto XXXII

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-1">So steadfast and attentive were mine eyes
In satisfying their decennial thirst,
That all my other senses were extinct,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-4">And upon this side and on that they had
Walls of indifference, so the holy smile
Drew them unto itself with the old net
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-7">When forcibly my sight was turned away
Towards my left hand by those goddesses,
Because I heard from them a "Too intently!"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-10">And that condition of the sight which is
In eyes but lately smitten by the sun
Bereft me of my vision some short while;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-13">But to the less when sight re-shaped itself,
I say the less in reference to the greater
Splendour from which perforce I had withdrawn,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-16">I saw upon its right wing wheeled about
The glorious host returning with the sun
And with the sevenfold flames upon their faces.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-19">As underneath its shields, to save itself,
A squadron turns, and with its banner wheels,
Before the whole thereof can change its front,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-22">That soldiery of the celestial kingdom
Which marched in the advance had wholly passed us
Before the chariot had turned its pole.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-25">Then to the wheels the maidens turned themselves,
And the Griffin moved his burden benedight,
But so that not a feather of him fluttered.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-28">The lady fair who drew me through the ford
Followed with Statius and myself the wheel
Which made its orbit with the lesser arc.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-31">So passing through the lofty forest, vacant
By fault of her who in the serpent trusted,
Angelic music made our steps keep time.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-34">Perchance as great a space had in three flights
An arrow loosened from the string o'erpassed,
As we had moved when Beatrice descended.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-37">I heard them murmur altogether, "Adam!"
Then circled they about a tree despoiled
Of blooms and other leafage on each bough.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-40">Its tresses, which so much the more dilate
As higher they ascend, had been by Indians
Among their forests marvelled at for height.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-43">"Blessed art thou, O Griffin, who dost not
Pluck with thy beak these branches sweet to taste,
Since appetite by this was turned to evil."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-46">After this fashion round the tree robust
The others shouted; and the twofold creature:
"Thus is preserved the seed of all the just."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-49">And turning to the pole which he had dragged,
He drew it close beneath the widowed bough,
And what was of it unto it left bound.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-52">In the same manner as our trees (when downward
Falls the great light, with that together mingled
Which after the celestial Lasca shines)
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-55">Begin to swell, and then renew themselves,
Each one with its own colour, ere the Sun
Harness his steeds beneath another star:
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-58">Less than of rose and more than violet
A hue disclosing, was renewed the tree
That had erewhile its boughs so desolate.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-61">I never heard, nor here below is sung,
The hymn which afterward that people sang,
Nor did I bear the melody throughout.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-64">Had I the power to paint how fell asleep
Those eyes compassionless, of Syrinx hearing,
Those eyes to which more watching cost so dear,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-67">Even as a painter who from model paints
I would portray how I was lulled asleep;
He may, who well can picture drowsihood.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-70">Therefore I pass to what time I awoke,
And say a splendour rent from me the veil
Of slumber, and a calling: "Rise, what dost thou?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-73">As to behold the apple-tree in blossom
Which makes the Angels greedy for its fruit,
And keeps perpetual bridals in the Heaven,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-76">Peter and John and James conducted were,
And, overcome, recovered at the word
By which still greater slumbers have been broken,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-79">And saw their school diminished by the loss
Not only of Elias, but of Moses,
And the apparel of their Master changed;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-82">So I revived, and saw that piteous one
Above me standing, who had been conductress
Aforetime of my steps beside the river,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-85">And all in doubt I said, "Where's Beatrice?"
And she: "Behold her seated underneath
The leafage new, upon the root of it.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-88">Behold the company that circles her;
The rest behind the Griffin are ascending
With more melodious song, and more profound."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-91">And if her speech were more diffuse I know not,
Because already in my sight was she
Who from the hearing of aught else had shut me.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-94">Alone she sat upon the very earth,
Left there as guardian of the chariot
Which I had seen the biform monster fasten.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-97">Encircling her, a cloister made themselves
The seven Nymphs, with those lights in their hands
Which are secure from Aquilon and Auster.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-100">"Short while shalt thou be here a forester,
And thou shalt be with me for evermore
A citizen of that Rome where Christ is Roman.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-103">Therefore, for that world's good which liveth ill,
Fix on the car thine eyes, and what thou seest,
Having returned to earth, take heed thou write."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-106">Thus Beatrice; and I, who at the feet
Of her commandments all devoted was,
My mind and eyes directed where she willed.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-109">Never descended with so swift a motion
Fire from a heavy cloud, when it is raining
From out the region which is most remote,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-112">As I beheld the bird of Jove descend
Down through the tree, rending away the bark,
As well as blossoms and the foliage new,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-115">And he with all his might the chariot smote,
Whereat it reeled, like vessel in a tempest
Tossed by the waves, now starboard and now larboard.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-118">Thereafter saw I leap into the body
Of the triumphal vehicle a Fox,
That seemed unfed with any wholesome food.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-121">But for his hideous sins upbraiding him,
My Lady put him to as swift a flight
As such a fleshless skeleton could bear.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-124">Then by the way that it before had come,
Into the chariot's chest I saw the Eagle
Descend, and leave it feathered with his plumes.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-127">And such as issues from a heart that mourns,
A voice from Heaven there issued, and it said:
"My little bark, how badly art thou freighted!"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-130">Methought, then, that the earth did yawn between
Both wheels, and I saw rise from it a Dragon,
Who through the chariot upward fixed his tail,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-133">And as a wasp that draweth back its sting,
Drawing unto himself his tail malign,
Drew out the floor, and went his way rejoicing.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-136">That which remained behind, even as with grass
A fertile region, with the feathers, offered
Perhaps with pure intention and benign,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-139">Reclothed itself, and with them were reclothed
The pole and both the wheels so speedily,
A sigh doth longer keep the lips apart.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-142">Transfigured thus the holy edifice
Thrust forward heads upon the parts of it,
Three on the pole and one at either corner.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-145">The first were horned like oxen; but the four
Had but a single horn upon the forehead;
A monster such had never yet been seen!
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-148">Firm as a rock upon a mountain high,
Seated upon it, there appeared to me
A shameless whore, with eyes swift glancing round,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-151">And, as if not to have her taken from him,
Upright beside her I beheld a giant;
And ever and anon they kissed each other.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-154">But because she her wanton, roving eye
Turned upon me, her angry paramour
Did scourge her from her head unto her feet.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-157">Then full of jealousy, and fierce with wrath,
He loosed the monster, and across the forest
Dragged it so far, he made of that alone
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxii-160">A shield unto the whore and the strange beast.
</p>

## Purgatorio - Canto XXXIII

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-1">"Deus venerunt gentes," alternating
Now three, now four, melodious psalmody
The maidens in the midst of tears began;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-4">And Beatrice, compassionate and sighing,
Listened to them with such a countenance,
That scarce more changed was Mary at the cross.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-7">But when the other virgins place had given
For her to speak, uprisen to her feet
With colour as of fire, she made response:
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-10">"'Modicum, et non videbitis me;
Et iterum,' my sisters predilect,
'Modicum, et vos videbitis me.'"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-13">Then all the seven in front of her she placed;
And after her, by beckoning only, moved
Me and the lady and the sage who stayed.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-16">So she moved onward; and I do not think
That her tenth step was placed upon the ground,
When with her eyes upon mine eyes she smote,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-19">And with a tranquil aspect, "Come more quickly,"
To me she said, "that, if I speak with thee,
To listen to me thou mayst be well placed."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-22">As soon as I was with her as I should be,
She said to me: "Why, brother, dost thou not
Venture to question now, in coming with me?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-25">As unto those who are too reverential,
Speaking in presence of superiors,
Who drag no living utterance to their teeth,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-28">It me befell, that without perfect sound
Began I: "My necessity, Madonna,
You know, and that which thereunto is good."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-31">And she to me: "Of fear and bashfulness
Henceforward I will have thee strip thyself,
So that thou speak no more as one who dreams.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-34">Know that the vessel which the serpent broke
Was, and is not; but let him who is guilty
Think that God's vengeance does not fear a sop.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-37">Without an heir shall not for ever be
The Eagle that left his plumes upon the car,
Whence it became a monster, then a prey;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-40">For verily I see, and hence narrate it,
The stars already near to bring the time,
From every hindrance safe, and every bar,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-43">Within which a Five-hundred, Ten, and Five,
One sent from God, shall slay the thievish woman
And that same giant who is sinning with her.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-46">And peradventure my dark utterance,
Like Themis and the Sphinx, may less persuade thee,
Since, in their mode, it clouds the intellect;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-49">But soon the facts shall be the Naiades
Who shall this difficult enigma solve,
Without destruction of the flocks and harvests.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-52">Note thou; and even as by me are uttered
These words, so teach them unto those who live
That life which is a running unto death;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-55">And bear in mind, whene'er thou writest them,
Not to conceal what thou hast seen the plant,
That twice already has been pillaged here.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-58">Whoever pillages or shatters it,
With blasphemy of deed offendeth God,
Who made it holy for his use alone.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-61">For biting that, in pain and in desire
Five thousand years and more the first-born soul
Craved Him, who punished in himself the bite.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-64">Thy genius slumbers, if it deem it not
For special reason so pre-eminent
In height, and so inverted in its summit.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-67">And if thy vain imaginings had not been
Water of Elsa round about thy mind,
And Pyramus to the mulberry, their pleasure,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-70">Thou by so many circumstances only
The justice of the interdict of God
Morally in the tree wouldst recognize.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-73">But since I see thee in thine intellect
Converted into stone and stained with sin,
So that the light of my discourse doth daze thee,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-76">I will too, if not written, at least painted,
Thou bear it back within thee, for the reason
That cinct with palm the pilgrim's staff is borne."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-79">And I: "As by a signet is the wax
Which does not change the figure stamped upon it,
My brain is now imprinted by yourself.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-82">But wherefore so beyond my power of sight
Soars your desirable discourse, that aye
The more I strive, so much the more I lose it?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-85">"That thou mayst recognize," she said, "the school
Which thou hast followed, and mayst see how far
Its doctrine follows after my discourse,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-88">And mayst behold your path from the divine
Distant as far as separated is
From earth the heaven that highest hastens on."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-91">Whence her I answered: "I do not remember
That ever I estranged myself from you,
Nor have I conscience of it that reproves me."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-94">"And if thou art not able to remember,"
Smiling she answered, "recollect thee now
That thou this very day hast drunk of Lethe;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-97">And if from smoke a fire may be inferred,
Such an oblivion clearly demonstrates
Some error in thy will elsewhere intent.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-100">Truly from this time forward shall my words
Be naked, so far as it is befitting
To lay them open unto thy rude gaze."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-103">And more coruscant and with slower steps
The sun was holding the meridian circle,
Which, with the point of view, shifts here and there
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-106">When halted (as he cometh to a halt,
Who goes before a squadron as its escort,
If something new he find upon his way)
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-109">The ladies seven at a dark shadow's edge,
Such as, beneath green leaves and branches black,
The Alp upon its frigid border wears.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-112">In front of them the Tigris and Euphrates
Methought I saw forth issue from one fountain,
And slowly part, like friends, from one another.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-115">"O light, O glory of the human race!
What stream is this which here unfolds itself
From out one source, and from itself withdraws?"
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-118">For such a prayer, 'twas said unto me, "Pray
Matilda that she tell thee;" and here answered,
As one does who doth free himself from blame,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-121">The beautiful lady: "This and other things
Were told to him by me; and sure I am
The water of Lethe has not hid them from him."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-124">And Beatrice: "Perhaps a greater care,
Which oftentimes our memory takes away,
Has made the vision of his mind obscure.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-127">But Eunoe behold, that yonder rises;
Lead him to it, and, as thou art accustomed,
Revive again the half-dead virtue in him."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-130">Like gentle soul, that maketh no excuse,
But makes its own will of another's will
As soon as by a sign it is disclosed,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-133">Even so, when she had taken hold of me,
The beautiful lady moved, and unto Statius
Said, in her womanly manner, "Come with him."
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-136">If, Reader, I possessed a longer space
For writing it, I yet would sing in part
Of the sweet draught that ne'er would satiate me;
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-139">But inasmuch as full are all the leaves
Made ready for this second canticle,
The curb of art no farther lets me go.
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-142">From the most holy water I returned
Regenerate, in the manner of new trees
That are renewed with a new foliage,
</p>

<p id="purgatorio-xxxiii-145">Pure and disposed to mount unto the stars.
</p>

# PARADISO

## Paradiso - Canto I

<p id="paradiso-i-1">The glory of Him who moveth everything
Doth penetrate the universe, and shine
In one part more and in another less.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-4">Within that heaven which most his light receives
Was I, and things beheld which to repeat
Nor knows, nor can, who from above descends;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-7">Because in drawing near to its desire
Our intellect ingulphs itself so far,
That after it the memory cannot go.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-10">Truly whatever of the holy realm
I had the power to treasure in my mind
Shall now become the subject of my song.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-13">O good Apollo, for this last emprise
Make of me such a vessel of thy power
As giving the beloved laurel asks!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-16">One summit of Parnassus hitherto
Has been enough for me, but now with both
I needs must enter the arena left.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-19">Enter into my bosom, thou, and breathe
As at the time when Marsyas thou didst draw
Out of the scabbard of those limbs of his.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-22">O power divine, lend'st thou thyself to me
So that the shadow of the blessed realm
Stamped in my brain I can make manifest,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-25">Thou'lt see me come unto thy darling tree,
And crown myself thereafter with those leaves
Of which the theme and thou shall make me worthy.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-28">So seldom, Father, do we gather them
For triumph or of Caesar or of Poet,
(The fault and shame of human inclinations,)
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-31">That the Peneian foliage should bring forth
Joy to the joyous Delphic deity,
When any one it makes to thirst for it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-34">A little spark is followed by great flame;
Perchance with better voices after me
Shall prayer be made that Cyrrha may respond!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-37">To mortal men by passages diverse
Uprises the world's lamp; but by that one
Which circles four uniteth with three crosses,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-40">With better course and with a better star
Conjoined it issues, and the mundane wax
Tempers and stamps more after its own fashion.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-43">Almost that passage had made morning there
And evening here, and there was wholly white
That hemisphere, and black the other part,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-46">When Beatrice towards the left-hand side
I saw turned round, and gazing at the sun;
Never did eagle fasten so upon it!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-49">And even as a second ray is wont
To issue from the first and reascend,
Like to a pilgrim who would fain return,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-52">Thus of her action, through the eyes infused
In my imagination, mine I made,
And sunward fixed mine eyes beyond our wont.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-55">There much is lawful which is here unlawful
Unto our powers, by virtue of the place
Made for the human species as its own.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-58">Not long I bore it, nor so little while
But I beheld it sparkle round about
Like iron that comes molten from the fire;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-61">And suddenly it seemed that day to day
Was added, as if He who has the power
Had with another sun the heaven adorned.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-64">With eyes upon the everlasting wheels
Stood Beatrice all intent, and I, on her
Fixing my vision from above removed,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-67">Such at her aspect inwardly became
As Glaucus, tasting of the herb that made him
Peer of the other gods beneath the sea.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-70">To represent transhumanise in words
Impossible were; the example, then, suffice
Him for whom Grace the experience reserves.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-73">If I was merely what of me thou newly
Createdst, Love who governest the heaven,
Thou knowest, who didst lift me with thy light!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-76">When now the wheel, which thou dost make eternal
Desiring thee, made me attentive to it
By harmony thou dost modulate and measure,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-79">Then seemed to me so much of heaven enkindled
By the sun's flame, that neither rain nor river
E'er made a lake so widely spread abroad.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-82">The newness of the sound and the great light
Kindled in me a longing for their cause,
Never before with such acuteness felt;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-85">Whence she, who saw me as I saw myself,
To quiet in me my perturbed mind,
Opened her mouth, ere I did mine to ask,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-88">And she began: "Thou makest thyself so dull
With false imagining, that thou seest not
What thou wouldst see if thou hadst shaken it off.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-91">Thou art not upon earth, as thou believest;
But lightning, fleeing its appropriate site,
Ne'er ran as thou, who thitherward returnest."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-94">If of my former doubt I was divested
By these brief little words more smiled than spoken,
I in a new one was the more ensnared;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-97">And said: "Already did I rest content
From great amazement; but am now amazed
In what way I transcend these bodies light."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-100">Whereupon she, after a pitying sigh,
Her eyes directed tow'rds me with that look
A mother casts on a delirious child;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-103">And she began: "All things whate'er they be
Have order among themselves, and this is form,
That makes the universe resemble God.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-106">Here do the higher creatures see the footprints
Of the Eternal Power, which is the end
Whereto is made the law already mentioned.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-109">In the order that I speak of are inclined
All natures, by their destinies diverse,
More or less near unto their origin;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-112">Hence they move onward unto ports diverse
O'er the great sea of being; and each one
With instinct given it which bears it on.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-115">This bears away the fire towards the moon;
This is in mortal hearts the motive power
This binds together and unites the earth.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-118">Nor only the created things that are
Without intelligence this bow shoots forth,
But those that have both intellect and love.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-121">The Providence that regulates all this
Makes with its light the heaven forever quiet,
Wherein that turns which has the greatest haste.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-124">And thither now, as to a site decreed,
Bears us away the virtue of that cord
Which aims its arrows at a joyous mark.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-127">True is it, that as oftentimes the form
Accords not with the intention of the art,
Because in answering is matter deaf,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-130">So likewise from this course doth deviate
Sometimes the creature, who the power possesses,
Though thus impelled, to swerve some other way,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-133">(In the same wise as one may see the fire
Fall from a cloud,) if the first impetus
Earthward is wrested by some false delight.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-136">Thou shouldst not wonder more, if well I judge,
At thine ascent, than at a rivulet
From some high mount descending to the lowland.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-139">Marvel it would be in thee, if deprived
Of hindrance, thou wert seated down below,
As if on earth the living fire were quiet."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-i-142">Thereat she heavenward turned again her face.
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto II

<p id="paradiso-ii-1">O Ye, who in some pretty little boat,
Eager to listen, have been following
Behind my ship, that singing sails along,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-4">Turn back to look again upon your shores;
Do not put out to sea, lest peradventure,
In losing me, you might yourselves be lost.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-7">The sea I sail has never yet been passed;
Minerva breathes, and pilots me Apollo,
And Muses nine point out to me the Bears.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-10">Ye other few who have the neck uplifted
Betimes to th' bread of Angels upon which
One liveth here and grows not sated by it,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-13">Well may you launch upon the deep salt-sea
Your vessel, keeping still my wake before you
Upon the water that grows smooth again.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-16">Those glorious ones who unto Colchos passed
Were not so wonder-struck as you shall be,
When Jason they beheld a ploughman made!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-19">The con-created and perpetual thirst
For the realm deiform did bear us on,
As swift almost as ye the heavens behold.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-22">Upward gazed Beatrice, and I at her;
And in such space perchance as strikes a bolt
And flies, and from the notch unlocks itself,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-25">Arrived I saw me where a wondrous thing
Drew to itself my sight; and therefore she
From whom no care of mine could be concealed,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-28">Towards me turning, blithe as beautiful,
Said unto me: "Fix gratefully thy mind
On God, who unto the first star has brought us."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-31">It seemed to me a cloud encompassed us,
Luminous, dense, consolidate and bright
As adamant on which the sun is striking.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-34">Into itself did the eternal pearl
Receive us, even as water doth receive
A ray of light, remaining still unbroken.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-37">If I was body, (and we here conceive not
How one dimension tolerates another,
Which needs must be if body enter body,)
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-40">More the desire should be enkindled in us
That essence to behold, wherein is seen
How God and our own nature were united.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-43">There will be seen what we receive by faith,
Not demonstrated, but self-evident
In guise of the first truth that man believes.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-46">I made reply: "Madonna, as devoutly
As most I can do I give thanks to Him
Who has removed me from the mortal world.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-49">But tell me what the dusky spots may be
Upon this body, which below on earth
Make people tell that fabulous tale of Cain?"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-52">Somewhat she smiled; and then, "If the opinion
Of mortals be erroneous," she said,
"Where'er the key of sense doth not unlock,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-55">Certes, the shafts of wonder should not pierce thee
Now, forasmuch as, following the senses,
Thou seest that the reason has short wings.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-58">But tell me what thou think'st of it thyself."
And I: "What seems to us up here diverse,
Is caused, I think, by bodies rare and dense."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-61">And she: "Right truly shalt thou see immersed
In error thy belief, if well thou hearest
The argument that I shall make against it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-64">Lights many the eighth sphere displays to you
Which in their quality and quantity
May noted be of aspects different.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-67">If this were caused by rare and dense alone,
One only virtue would there be in all
Or more or less diffused, or equally.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-70">Virtues diverse must be perforce the fruits
Of formal principles; and these, save one,
Of course would by thy reasoning be destroyed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-73">Besides, if rarity were of this dimness
The cause thou askest, either through and through
This planet thus attenuate were of matter,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-76">Or else, as in a body is apportioned
The fat and lean, so in like manner this
Would in its volume interchange the leaves.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-79">Were it the former, in the sun's eclipse
It would be manifest by the shining through
Of light, as through aught tenuous interfused.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-82">This is not so; hence we must scan the other,
And if it chance the other I demolish,
Then falsified will thy opinion be.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-85">But if this rarity go not through and through,
There needs must be a limit, beyond which
Its contrary prevents the further passing,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-88">And thence the foreign radiance is reflected,
Even as a colour cometh back from glass,
The which behind itself concealeth lead.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-91">Now thou wilt say the sunbeam shows itself
More dimly there than in the other parts,
By being there reflected farther back.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-94">From this reply experiment will free thee
If e'er thou try it, which is wont to be
The fountain to the rivers of your arts.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-97">Three mirrors shalt thou take, and two remove
Alike from thee, the other more remote
Between the former two shall meet thine eyes.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-100">Turned towards these, cause that behind thy back
Be placed a light, illuming the three mirrors
And coming back to thee by all reflected.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-103">Though in its quantity be not so ample
The image most remote, there shalt thou see
How it perforce is equally resplendent.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-106">Now, as beneath the touches of warm rays
Naked the subject of the snow remains
Both of its former colour and its cold,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-109">Thee thus remaining in thy intellect,
Will I inform with such a living light,
That it shall tremble in its aspect to thee.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-112">Within the heaven of the divine repose
Revolves a body, in whose virtue lies
The being of whatever it contains.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-115">The following heaven, that has so many eyes,
Divides this being by essences diverse,
Distinguished from it, and by it contained.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-118">The other spheres, by various differences,
All the distinctions which they have within them
Dispose unto their ends and their effects.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-121">Thus do these organs of the world proceed,
As thou perceivest now, from grade to grade;
Since from above they take, and act beneath.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-124">Observe me well, how through this place I come
Unto the truth thou wishest, that hereafter
Thou mayst alone know how to keep the ford
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-127">The power and motion of the holy spheres,
As from the artisan the hammer's craft,
Forth from the blessed motors must proceed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-130">The heaven, which lights so manifold make fair,
From the Intelligence profound, which turns it,
The image takes, and makes of it a seal.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-133">And even as the soul within your dust
Through members different and accommodated
To faculties diverse expands itself,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-136">So likewise this Intelligence diffuses
Its virtue multiplied among the stars.
Itself revolving on its unity.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-139">Virtue diverse doth a diverse alloyage
Make with the precious body that it quickens,
In which, as life in you, it is combined.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-142">From the glad nature whence it is derived,
The mingled virtue through the body shines,
Even as gladness through the living pupil.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-145">From this proceeds whate'er from light to light
Appeareth different, not from dense and rare:
This is the formal principle that produces,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-ii-148">According to its goodness, dark and bright."
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto III

<p id="paradiso-iii-1">That Sun, which erst with love my bosom warmed,
Of beauteous truth had unto me discovered,
By proving and reproving, the sweet aspect.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-4">And, that I might confess myself convinced
And confident, so far as was befitting,
I lifted more erect my head to speak.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-7">But there appeared a vision, which withdrew me
So close to it, in order to be seen,
That my confession I remembered not.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-10">Such as through polished and transparent glass,
Or waters crystalline and undisturbed,
But not so deep as that their bed be lost,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-13">Come back again the outlines of our faces
So feeble, that a pearl on forehead white
Comes not less speedily unto our eyes;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-16">Such saw I many faces prompt to speak,
So that I ran in error opposite
To that which kindled love 'twixt man and fountain.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-19">As soon as I became aware of them,
Esteeming them as mirrored semblances,
To see of whom they were, mine eyes I turned,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-22">And nothing saw, and once more turned them forward
Direct into the light of my sweet Guide,
Who smiling kindled in her holy eyes.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-25">"Marvel thou not," she said to me, "because
I smile at this thy puerile conceit,
Since on the truth it trusts not yet its foot,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-28">But turns thee, as 'tis wont, on emptiness.
True substances are these which thou beholdest,
Here relegate for breaking of some vow.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-31">Therefore speak with them, listen and believe;
For the true light, which giveth peace to them,
Permits them not to turn from it their feet."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-34">And I unto the shade that seemed most wishful
To speak directed me, and I began,
As one whom too great eagerness bewilders:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-37">"O well-created spirit, who in the rays
Of life eternal dost the sweetness taste
Which being untasted ne'er is comprehended,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-40">Grateful 'twill be to me, if thou content me
Both with thy name and with your destiny."
Whereat she promptly and with laughing eyes:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-43">"Our charity doth never shut the doors
Against a just desire, except as one
Who wills that all her court be like herself.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-46">I was a virgin sister in the world;
And if thy mind doth contemplate me well,
The being more fair will not conceal me from thee,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-49">But thou shalt recognise I am Piccarda,
Who, stationed here among these other blessed,
Myself am blessed in the slowest sphere.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-52">All our affections, that alone inflamed
Are in the pleasure of the Holy Ghost,
Rejoice at being of his order formed;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-55">And this allotment, which appears so low,
Therefore is given us, because our vows
Have been neglected and in some part void."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-58">Whence I to her: "In your miraculous aspects
There shines I know not what of the divine,
Which doth transform you from our first conceptions.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-61">Therefore I was not swift in my remembrance;
But what thou tellest me now aids me so,
That the refiguring is easier to me.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-64">But tell me, ye who in this place are happy,
Are you desirous of a higher place,
To see more or to make yourselves more friends?"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-67">First with those other shades she smiled a little;
Thereafter answered me so full of gladness,
She seemed to burn in the first fire of love:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-70">"Brother, our will is quieted by virtue
Of charity, that makes us wish alone
For what we have, nor gives us thirst for more.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-73">If to be more exalted we aspired,
Discordant would our aspirations be
Unto the will of Him who here secludes us;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-76">Which thou shalt see finds no place in these circles,
If being in charity is needful here,
And if thou lookest well into its nature;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-79">Nay, 'tis essential to this blest existence
To keep itself within the will divine,
Whereby our very wishes are made one;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-82">So that, as we are station above station
Throughout this realm, to all the realm 'tis pleasing,
As to the King, who makes his will our will.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-85">And his will is our peace; this is the sea
To which is moving onward whatsoever
It doth create, and all that nature makes."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-88">Then it was clear to me how everywhere
In heaven is Paradise, although the grace
Of good supreme there rain not in one measure.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-91">But as it comes to pass, if one food sates,
And for another still remains the longing,
We ask for this, and that decline with thanks,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-94">E'en thus did I; with gesture and with word,
To learn from her what was the web wherein
She did not ply the shuttle to the end.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-97">"A perfect life and merit high in-heaven
A lady o'er us," said she, "by whose rule
Down in your world they vest and veil themselves,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-100">That until death they may both watch and sleep
Beside that Spouse who every vow accepts
Which charity conformeth to his pleasure.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-103">To follow her, in girlhood from the world
I fled, and in her habit shut myself,
And pledged me to the pathway of her sect.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-106">Then men accustomed unto evil more
Than unto good, from the sweet cloister tore me;
God knows what afterward my life became.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-109">This other splendour, which to thee reveals
Itself on my right side, and is enkindled
With all the illumination of our sphere,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-112">What of myself I say applies to her;
A nun was she, and likewise from her head
Was ta'en the shadow of the sacred wimple.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-115">But when she too was to the world returned
Against her wishes and against good usage,
Of the heart's veil she never was divested.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-118">Of great Costanza this is the effulgence,
Who from the second wind of Suabia
Brought forth the third and latest puissance."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-121">Thus unto me she spake, and then began
"Ave Maria" singing, and in singing
Vanished, as through deep water something heavy.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-124">My sight, that followed her as long a time
As it was possible, when it had lost her
Turned round unto the mark of more desire,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-127">And wholly unto Beatrice reverted;
But she such lightnings flashed into mine eyes,
That at the first my sight endured it not;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iii-130">And this in questioning more backward made me.
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto IV

<p id="paradiso-iv-1">Between two viands, equally removed
And tempting, a free man would die of hunger
Ere either he could bring unto his teeth.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-4">So would a lamb between the ravenings
Of two fierce wolves stand fearing both alike;
And so would stand a dog between two does.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-7">Hence, if I held my peace, myself I blame not,
Impelled in equal measure by my doubts,
Since it must be so, nor do I commend.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-10">I held my peace; but my desire was painted
Upon my face, and questioning with that
More fervent far than by articulate speech.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-13">Beatrice did as Daniel had done
Relieving Nebuchadnezzar from the wrath
Which rendered him unjustly merciless,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-16">And said: "Well see I how attracteth thee
One and the other wish, so that thy care
Binds itself so that forth it does not breathe.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-19">Thou arguest, if good will be permanent,
The violence of others, for what reason
Doth it decrease the measure of my merit?
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-22">Again for doubting furnish thee occasion
Souls seeming to return unto the stars,
According to the sentiment of Plato.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-25">These are the questions which upon thy wish
Are thrusting equally; and therefore first
Will I treat that which hath the most of gall.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-28">He of the Seraphim most absorbed in God,
Moses, and Samuel, and whichever John
Thou mayst select, I say, and even Mary,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-31">Have not in any other heaven their seats,
Than have those spirits that just appeared to thee,
Nor of existence more or fewer years;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-34">But all make beautiful the primal circle,
And have sweet life in different degrees,
By feeling more or less the eternal breath.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-37">They showed themselves here, not because allotted
This sphere has been to them, but to give sign
Of the celestial which is least exalted.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-40">To speak thus is adapted to your mind,
Since only through the sense it apprehendeth
What then it worthy makes of intellect.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-43">On this account the Scripture condescends
Unto your faculties, and feet and hands
To God attributes, and means something else;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-46">And Holy Church under an aspect human
Gabriel and Michael represent to you,
And him who made Tobias whole again.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-49">That which Timaeus argues of the soul
Doth not resemble that which here is seen,
Because it seems that as he speaks he thinks.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-52">He says the soul unto its star returns,
Believing it to have been severed thence
Whenever nature gave it as a form.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-55">Perhaps his doctrine is of other guise
Than the words sound, and possibly may be
With meaning that is not to be derided.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-58">If he doth mean that to these wheels return
The honour of their influence and the blame,
Perhaps his bow doth hit upon some truth.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-61">This principle ill understood once warped
The whole world nearly, till it went astray
Invoking Jove and Mercury and Mars.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-64">The other doubt which doth disquiet thee
Less venom has, for its malevolence
Could never lead thee otherwhere from me.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-67">That as unjust our justice should appear
In eyes of mortals, is an argument
Of faith, and not of sin heretical.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-70">But still, that your perception may be able
To thoroughly penetrate this verity,
As thou desirest, I will satisfy thee.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-73">If it be violence when he who suffers
Co-operates not with him who uses force,
These souls were not on that account excused;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-76">For will is never quenched unless it will,
But operates as nature doth in fire
If violence a thousand times distort it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-79">Hence, if it yieldeth more or less, it seconds
The force; and these have done so, having power
Of turning back unto the holy place.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-82">If their will had been perfect, like to that
Which Lawrence fast upon his gridiron held,
And Mutius made severe to his own hand,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-85">It would have urged them back along the road
Whence they were dragged, as soon as they were free;
But such a solid will is all too rare.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-88">And by these words, if thou hast gathered them
As thou shouldst do, the argument is refuted
That would have still annoyed thee many times.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-91">But now another passage runs across
Before thine eyes, and such that by thyself
Thou couldst not thread it ere thou wouldst be weary.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-94">I have for certain put into thy mind
That soul beatified could never lie,
For it is near the primal Truth,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-97">And then thou from Piccarda might'st have heard
Costanza kept affection for the veil,
So that she seemeth here to contradict me.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-100">Many times, brother, has it come to pass,
That, to escape from peril, with reluctance
That has been done it was not right to do,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-103">E'en as Alcmaeon (who, being by his father
Thereto entreated, his own mother slew)
Not to lose pity pitiless became.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-106">At this point I desire thee to remember
That force with will commingles, and they cause
That the offences cannot be excused.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-109">Will absolute consenteth not to evil;
But in so far consenteth as it fears,
If it refrain, to fall into more harm.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-112">Hence when Piccarda uses this expression,
She meaneth the will absolute, and I
The other, so that both of us speak truth."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-115">Such was the flowing of the holy river
That issued from the fount whence springs all truth;
This put to rest my wishes one and all.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-118">"O love of the first lover, O divine,"
Said I forthwith, "whose speech inundates me
And warms me so, it more and more revives me,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-121">My own affection is not so profound
As to suffice in rendering grace for grace;
Let Him, who sees and can, thereto respond.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-124">Well I perceive that never sated is
Our intellect unless the Truth illume it,
Beyond which nothing true expands itself.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-127">It rests therein, as wild beast in his lair,
When it attains it; and it can attain it;
If not, then each desire would frustrate be.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-130">Therefore springs up, in fashion of a shoot,
Doubt at the foot of truth; and this is nature,
Which to the top from height to height impels us.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-133">This doth invite me, this assurance give me
With reverence, Lady, to inquire of you
Another truth, which is obscure to me.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-136">I wish to know if man can satisfy you
For broken vows with other good deeds, so
That in your balance they will not be light."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-139">Beatrice gazed upon me with her eyes
Full of the sparks of love, and so divine,
That, overcome my power, I turned my back
</p>

<p id="paradiso-iv-142">And almost lost myself with eyes downcast.
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto V

<p id="paradiso-v-1">"If in the heat of love I flame upon thee
Beyond the measure that on earth is seen,
So that the valour of thine eyes I vanquish,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-4">Marvel thou not thereat; for this proceeds
From perfect sight, which as it apprehends
To the good apprehended moves its feet.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-7">Well I perceive how is already shining
Into thine intellect the eternal light,
That only seen enkindles always love;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-10">And if some other thing your love seduce,
'Tis nothing but a vestige of the same,
Ill understood, which there is shining through.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-13">Thou fain wouldst know if with another service
For broken vow can such return be made
As to secure the soul from further claim."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-16">This Canto thus did Beatrice begin;
And, as a man who breaks not off his speech,
Continued thus her holy argument:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-19">"The greatest gift that in his largess God
Creating made, and unto his own goodness
Nearest conformed, and that which he doth prize
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-22">Most highly, is the freedom of the will,
Wherewith the creatures of intelligence
Both all and only were and are endowed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-25">Now wilt thou see, if thence thou reasonest,
The high worth of a vow, if it he made
So that when thou consentest God consents:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-28">For, closing between God and man the compact,
A sacrifice is of this treasure made,
Such as I say, and made by its own act.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-31">What can be rendered then as compensation?
Think'st thou to make good use of what thou'st offered,
With gains ill gotten thou wouldst do good deed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-34">Now art thou certain of the greater point;
But because Holy Church in this dispenses,
Which seems against the truth which I have shown thee,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-37">Behoves thee still to sit awhile at table,
Because the solid food which thou hast taken
Requireth further aid for thy digestion.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-40">Open thy mind to that which I reveal,
And fix it there within; for 'tis not knowledge,
The having heard without retaining it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-43">In the essence of this sacrifice two things
Convene together; and the one is that
Of which 'tis made, the other is the agreement.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-46">This last for evermore is cancelled not
Unless complied with, and concerning this
With such precision has above been spoken.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-49">Therefore it was enjoined upon the Hebrews
To offer still, though sometimes what was offered
Might be commuted, as thou ought'st to know.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-52">The other, which is known to thee as matter,
May well indeed be such that one errs not
If it for other matter be exchanged.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-55">But let none shift the burden on his shoulder
At his arbitrament, without the turning
Both of the white and of the yellow key;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-58">And every permutation deem as foolish,
If in the substitute the thing relinquished,
As the four is in six, be not contained.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-61">Therefore whatever thing has so great weight
In value that it drags down every balance,
Cannot be satisfied with other spending.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-64">Let mortals never take a vow in jest;
Be faithful and not blind in doing that,
As Jephthah was in his first offering,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-67">Whom more beseemed to say, 'I have done wrong,
Than to do worse by keeping; and as foolish
Thou the great leader of the Greeks wilt find,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-70">Whence wept Iphigenia her fair face,
And made for her both wise and simple weep,
Who heard such kind of worship spoken of.'
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-73">Christians, be ye more serious in your movements;
Be ye not like a feather at each wind,
And think not every water washes you.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-76">Ye have the Old and the New Testament,
And the Pastor of the Church who guideth you
Let this suffice you unto your salvation.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-79">If evil appetite cry aught else to you,
Be ye as men, and not as silly sheep,
So that the Jew among you may not mock you.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-82">Be ye not as the lamb that doth abandon
Its mother's milk, and frolicsome and simple
Combats at its own pleasure with itself."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-85">Thus Beatrice to me even as I write it;
Then all desireful turned herself again
To that part where the world is most alive.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-88">Her silence and her change of countenance
Silence imposed upon my eager mind,
That had already in advance new questions;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-91">And as an arrow that upon the mark
Strikes ere the bowstring quiet hath become,
So did we speed into the second realm.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-94">My Lady there so joyful I beheld,
As into the brightness of that heaven she entered,
More luminous thereat the planet grew;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-97">And if the star itself was changed and smiled,
What became I, who by my nature am
Exceeding mutable in every guise!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-100">As, in a fish-pond which is pure and tranquil,
The fishes draw to that which from without
Comes in such fashion that their food they deem it;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-103">So I beheld more than a thousand splendours
Drawing towards us, and in each was heard:
"Lo, this is she who shall increase our love."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-106">And as each one was coming unto us,
Full of beatitude the shade was seen,
By the effulgence clear that issued from it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-109">Think, Reader, if what here is just beginning
No farther should proceed, how thou wouldst have
An agonizing need of knowing more;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-112">And of thyself thou'lt see how I from these
Was in desire of hearing their conditions,
As they unto mine eyes were manifest.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-115">"O thou well-born, unto whom Grace concedes
To see the thrones of the eternal triumph,
Or ever yet the warfare be abandoned
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-118">With light that through the whole of heaven is spread
Kindled are we, and hence if thou desirest
To know of us, at thine own pleasure sate thee."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-121">Thus by some one among those holy spirits
Was spoken, and by Beatrice: "Speak, speak
Securely, and believe them even as Gods."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-124">"Well I perceive how thou dost nest thyself
In thine own light, and drawest it from thine eyes,
Because they coruscate when thou dost smile,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-127">But know not who thou art, nor why thou hast,
Spirit august, thy station in the sphere
That veils itself to men in alien rays."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-130">This said I in direction of the light
Which first had spoken to me; whence it became
By far more lucent than it was before.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-133">Even as the sun, that doth conceal himself
By too much light, when heat has worn away
The tempering influence of the vapours dense,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-136">By greater rapture thus concealed itself
In its own radiance the figure saintly,
And thus close, close enfolded answered me
</p>

<p id="paradiso-v-139">In fashion as the following Canto sings.
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto VI

<p id="paradiso-vi-1">"After that Constantine the eagle turned
Against the course of heaven, which it had followed
Behind the ancient who Lavinia took,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-4">Two hundred years and more the bird of God
In the extreme of Europe held itself,
Near to the mountains whence it issued first;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-7">And under shadow of the sacred plumes
It governed there the world from hand to hand,
And, changing thus, upon mine own alighted.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-10">Caesar I was, and am Justinian,
Who, by the will of primal Love I feel,
Took from the laws the useless and redundant;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-13">And ere unto the work I was attent,
One nature to exist in Christ, not more,
Believed, and with such faith was I contented.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-16">But blessed Agapetus, he who was
The supreme pastor, to the faith sincere
Pointed me out the way by words of his.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-19">Him I believed, and what was his assertion
I now see clearly, even as thou seest
Each contradiction to be false and true.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-22">As soon as with the Church I moved my feet,
God in his grace it pleased with this high task
To inspire me, and I gave me wholly to it,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-25">And to my Belisarius I commended
The arms, to which was heaven's right hand so joined
It was a signal that I should repose.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-28">Now here to the first question terminates
My answer; but the character thereof
Constrains me to continue with a sequel,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-31">In order that thou see with how great reason
Men move against the standard sacrosanct,
Both who appropriate and who oppose it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-34">Behold how great a power has made it worthy
Of reverence, beginning from the hour
When Pallas died to give it sovereignty.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-37">Thou knowest it made in Alba its abode
Three hundred years and upward, till at last
The three to three fought for it yet again.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-40">Thou knowest what it achieved from Sabine wrong
Down to Lucretia's sorrow, in seven kings
O'ercoming round about the neighboring nations;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-43">Thou knowest what it achieved, borne by the Romans
Illustrious against Brennus, against Pyrrhus,
Against the other princes and confederates.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-46">Torquatus thence and Quinctius, who from locks
Unkempt was named, Decii and Fabii,
Received the fame I willingly embalm;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-49">It struck to earth the pride of the Arabians,
Who, following Hannibal, had passed across
The Alpine ridges, Po, from which thou glidest;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-52">Beneath it triumphed while they yet were young
Pompey and Scipio, and to the hill
Beneath which thou wast born it bitter seemed;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-55">Then, near unto the time when heaven had willed
To bring the whole world to its mood serene,
Did Caesar by the will of Rome assume it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-58">What it achieved from Var unto the Rhine,
Isere beheld and Saone, beheld the Seine,
And every valley whence the Rhone is filled;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-61">What it achieved when it had left Ravenna,
And leaped the Rubicon, was such a flight
That neither tongue nor pen could follow it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-64">Round towards Spain it wheeled its legions; then
Towards Durazzo, and Pharsalia smote
That to the calid Nile was felt the pain.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-67">Antandros and the Simois, whence it started,
It saw again, and there where Hector lies,
And ill for Ptolemy then roused itself.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-70">From thence it came like lightning upon Juba;
Then wheeled itself again into your West,
Where the Pompeian clarion it heard.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-73">From what it wrought with the next standard-bearer
Brutus and Cassius howl in Hell together,
And Modena and Perugia dolent were;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-76">Still doth the mournful Cleopatra weep
Because thereof, who, fleeing from before it,
Took from the adder sudden and black death.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-79">With him it ran even to the Red Sea shore;
With him it placed the world in so great peace,
That unto Janus was his temple closed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-82">But what the standard that has made me speak
Achieved before, and after should achieve
Throughout the mortal realm that lies beneath it,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-85">Becometh in appearance mean and dim,
If in the hand of the third Caesar seen
With eye unclouded and affection pure,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-88">Because the living Justice that inspires me
Granted it, in the hand of him I speak of,
The glory of doing vengeance for its wrath.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-91">Now here attend to what I answer thee;
Later it ran with Titus to do vengeance
Upon the vengeance of the ancient sin.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-94">And when the tooth of Lombardy had bitten
The Holy Church, then underneath its wings
Did Charlemagne victorious succor her.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-97">Now hast thou power to judge of such as those
Whom I accused above, and of their crimes,
Which are the cause of all your miseries.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-100">To the public standard one the yellow lilies
Opposes, the other claims it for a party,
So that 'tis hard to see which sins the most.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-103">Let, let the Ghibellines ply their handicraft
Beneath some other standard; for this ever
Ill follows he who it and justice parts.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-106">And let not this new Charles e'er strike it down,
He and his Guelfs, but let him fear the talons
That from a nobler lion stripped the fell.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-109">Already oftentimes the sons have wept
The father's crime; and let him not believe
That God will change His scutcheon for the lilies.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-112">This little planet doth adorn itself
With the good spirits that have active been,
That fame and honour might come after them;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-115">And whensoever the desires mount thither,
Thus deviating, must perforce the rays
Of the true love less vividly mount upward.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-118">But in commensuration of our wages
With our desert is portion of our joy,
Because we see them neither less nor greater.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-121">Herein doth living Justice sweeten so
Affection in us, that for evermore
It cannot warp to any iniquity.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-124">Voices diverse make up sweet melodies;
So in this life of ours the seats diverse
Render sweet harmony among these spheres;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-127">And in the compass of this present pearl
Shineth the sheen of Romeo, of whom
The grand and beauteous work was ill rewarded.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-130">But the Provencals who against him wrought,
They have not laughed, and therefore ill goes he
Who makes his hurt of the good deeds of others.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-133">Four daughters, and each one of them a queen,
Had Raymond Berenger, and this for him
Did Romeo, a poor man and a pilgrim;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-136">And then malicious words incited him
To summon to a reckoning this just man,
Who rendered to him seven and five for ten.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-139">Then he departed poor and stricken in years,
And if the world could know the heart he had,
In begging bit by bit his livelihood,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vi-142">Though much it laud him, it would laud him more."
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto VII

<p id="paradiso-vii-1">"Osanna sanctus Deus Sabaoth,
Superillustrans claritate tua
Felices ignes horum malahoth!"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-4">In this wise, to his melody returning,
This substance, upon which a double light
Doubles itself, was seen by me to sing,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-7">And to their dance this and the others moved,
And in the manner of swift-hurrying sparks
Veiled themselves from me with a sudden distance.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-10">Doubting was I, and saying, "Tell her, tell her,"
Within me, "tell her," saying, "tell my Lady,"
Who slakes my thirst with her sweet effluences;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-13">And yet that reverence which doth lord it over
The whole of me only by B and ICE,
Bowed me again like unto one who drowses.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-16">Short while did Beatrice endure me thus;
And she began, lighting me with a smile
Such as would make one happy in the fire:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-19">"According to infallible advisement,
After what manner a just vengeance justly
Could be avenged has put thee upon thinking,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-22">But I will speedily thy mind unloose;
And do thou listen, for these words of mine
Of a great doctrine will a present make thee.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-25">By not enduring on the power that wills
Curb for his good, that man who ne'er was born,
Damning himself damned all his progeny;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-28">Whereby the human species down below
Lay sick for many centuries in great error,
Till to descend it pleased the Word of God
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-31">To where the nature, which from its own Maker
Estranged itself, he joined to him in person
By the sole act of his eternal love.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-34">Now unto what is said direct thy sight;
This nature when united to its Maker,
Such as created, was sincere and good;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-37">But by itself alone was banished forth
From Paradise, because it turned aside
Out of the way of truth and of its life.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-40">Therefore the penalty the cross held out,
If measured by the nature thus assumed,
None ever yet with so great justice stung,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-43">And none was ever of so great injustice,
Considering who the Person was that suffered,
Within whom such a nature was contracted.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-46">From one act therefore issued things diverse;
To God and to the Jews one death was pleasing;
Earth trembled at it and the Heaven was opened.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-49">It should no longer now seem difficult
To thee, when it is said that a just vengeance
By a just court was afterward avenged.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-52">But now do I behold thy mind entangled
From thought to thought within a knot, from which
With great desire it waits to free itself.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-55">Thou sayest, 'Well discern I what I hear;
But it is hidden from me why God willed
For our redemption only this one mode.'
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-58">Buried remaineth, brother, this decree
Unto the eyes of every one whose nature
Is in the flame of love not yet adult.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-61">Verily, inasmuch as at this mark
One gazes long and little is discerned,
Wherefore this mode was worthiest will I say.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-64">Goodness Divine, which from itself doth spurn
All envy, burning in itself so sparkles
That the eternal beauties it unfolds.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-67">Whate'er from this immediately distils
Has afterwards no end, for ne'er removed
Is its impression when it sets its seal.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-70">Whate'er from this immediately rains down
Is wholly free, because it is not subject
Unto the influences of novel things.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-73">The more conformed thereto, the more it pleases;
For the blest ardour that irradiates all things
In that most like itself is most vivacious.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-76">With all of these things has advantaged been
The human creature; and if one be wanting,
From his nobility he needs must fall.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-79">'Tis sin alone which doth disfranchise him,
And render him unlike the Good Supreme,
So that he little with its light is blanched,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-82">And to his dignity no more returns,
Unless he fill up where transgression empties
With righteous pains for criminal delights.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-85">Your nature when it sinned so utterly
In its own seed, out of these dignities
Even as out of Paradise was driven,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-88">Nor could itself recover, if thou notest
With nicest subtilty, by any way,
Except by passing one of these two fords:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-91">Either that God through clemency alone
Had pardon granted, or that man himself
Had satisfaction for his folly made.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-94">Fix now thine eye deep into the abyss
Of the eternal counsel, to my speech
As far as may be fastened steadfastly!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-97">Man in his limitations had not power
To satisfy, not having power to sink
In his humility obeying then,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-100">Far as he disobeying thought to rise;
And for this reason man has been from power
Of satisfying by himself excluded.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-103">Therefore it God behoved in his own ways
Man to restore unto his perfect life,
I say in one, or else in both of them.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-106">But since the action of the doer is
So much more grateful, as it more presents
The goodness of the heart from which it issues,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-109">Goodness Divine, that doth imprint the world,
Has been contented to proceed by each
And all its ways to lift you up again;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-112">Nor 'twixt the first day and the final night
Such high and such magnificent proceeding
By one or by the other was or shall be;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-115">For God more bounteous was himself to give
To make man able to uplift himself,
Than if he only of himself had pardoned;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-118">And all the other modes were insufficient
For justice, were it not the Son of God
Himself had humbled to become incarnate.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-121">Now, to fill fully each desire of thine,
Return I to elucidate one place,
In order that thou there mayst see as I do.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-124">Thou sayst: 'I see the air, I see the fire,
The water, and the earth, and all their mixtures
Come to corruption, and short while endure;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-127">And these things notwithstanding were created;'
Therefore if that which I have said were true,
They should have been secure against corruption.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-130">The Angels, brother, and the land sincere
In which thou art, created may be called
Just as they are in their entire existence;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-133">But all the elements which thou hast named,
And all those things which out of them are made,
By a created virtue are informed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-136">Created was the matter which they have;
Created was the informing influence
Within these stars that round about them go.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-139">The soul of every brute and of the plants
By its potential temperament attracts
The ray and motion of the holy lights;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-142">But your own life immediately inspires
Supreme Beneficence, and enamours it
So with herself, it evermore desires her.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-145">And thou from this mayst argue furthermore
Your resurrection, if thou think again
How human flesh was fashioned at that time
</p>

<p id="paradiso-vii-148">When the first parents both of them were made."
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto VIII

<p id="paradiso-viii-1">The world used in its peril to believe
That the fair Cypria delirious love
Rayed out, in the third epicycle turning;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-4">Wherefore not only unto her paid honour
Of sacrifices and of votive cry
The ancient nations in the ancient error,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-7">But both Dione honoured they and Cupid,
That as her mother, this one as her son,
And said that he had sat in Dido's lap;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-10">And they from her, whence I beginning take,
Took the denomination of the star
That woos the sun, now following, now in front.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-13">I was not ware of our ascending to it;
But of our being in it gave full faith
My Lady whom I saw more beauteous grow.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-16">And as within a flame a spark is seen,
And as within a voice a voice discerned,
When one is steadfast, and one comes and goes,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-19">Within that light beheld I other lamps
Move in a circle, speeding more and less,
Methinks in measure of their inward vision.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-22">From a cold cloud descended never winds,
Or visible or not, so rapidly
They would not laggard and impeded seem
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-25">To any one who had those lights divine
Seen come towards us, leaving the gyration
Begun at first in the high Seraphim.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-28">And behind those that most in front appeared
Sounded "Osanna!" so that never since
To hear again was I without desire.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-31">Then unto us more nearly one approached,
And it alone began: "We all are ready
Unto thy pleasure, that thou joy in us.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-34">We turn around with the celestial Princes,
One gyre and one gyration and one thirst,
To whom thou in the world of old didst say,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-37">'Ye who, intelligent, the third heaven are moving;'
And are so full of love, to pleasure thee
A little quiet will not be less sweet."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-40">After these eyes of mine themselves had offered
Unto my Lady reverently, and she
Content and certain of herself had made them,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-43">Back to the light they turned, which so great promise
Made of itself, and "Say, who art thou?" was
My voice, imprinted with a great affection.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-46">O how and how much I beheld it grow
With the new joy that superadded was
Unto its joys, as soon as I had spoken!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-49">Thus changed, it said to me: "The world possessed me
Short time below; and, if it had been more,
Much evil will be which would not have been.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-52">My gladness keepeth me concealed from thee,
Which rayeth round about me, and doth hide me
Like as a creature swathed in its own silk.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-55">Much didst thou love me, and thou hadst good reason;
For had I been below, I should have shown thee
Somewhat beyond the foliage of my love.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-58">That left-hand margin, which doth bathe itself
In Rhone, when it is mingled with the Sorgue,
Me for its lord awaited in due time,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-61">And that horn of Ausonia, which is towned
With Bari, with Gaeta and Catona,
Whence Tronto and Verde in the sea disgorge.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-64">Already flashed upon my brow the crown
Of that dominion which the Danube waters
After the German borders it abandons;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-67">And beautiful Trinacria, that is murky
'Twixt Pachino and Peloro, (on the gulf
Which greatest scath from Eurus doth receive,)
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-70">Not through Typhoeus, but through nascent sulphur,
Would have awaited her own monarchs still,
Through me from Charles descended and from Rudolph,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-viii-73">If evil lordship, that exasperates ever
The subject populations, had not moved
