Of the eternal joy, that only one
Make me perceive your odours manifold,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-25">Exhaling, break within me the great fast
Which a long season has in hunger held me,
Not finding for it any food on earth.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-28">Well do I know, that if in heaven its mirror
Justice Divine another realm doth make,
Yours apprehends it not through any veil.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-31">You know how I attentively address me
To listen; and you know what is the doubt
That is in me so very old a fast."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-34">Even as a falcon, issuing from his hood,
Doth move his head, and with his wings applaud him,
Showing desire, and making himself fine,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-37">Saw I become that standard, which of lauds
Was interwoven of the grace divine,
With such songs as he knows who there rejoices.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-40">Then it began: "He who a compass turned
On the world's outer verge, and who within it
Devised so much occult and manifest,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-43">Could not the impress of his power so make
On all the universe, as that his Word
Should not remain in infinite excess.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-46">And this makes certain that the first proud being,
Who was the paragon of every creature,
By not awaiting light fell immature.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-49">And hence appears it, that each minor nature
Is scant receptacle unto that good
Which has no end, and by itself is measured.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-52">In consequence our vision, which perforce
Must be some ray of that intelligence
With which all things whatever are replete,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-55">Cannot in its own nature be so potent,
That it shall not its origin discern
Far beyond that which is apparent to it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-58">Therefore into the justice sempiternal
The power of vision that your world receives,
As eye into the ocean, penetrates;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-61">Which, though it see the bottom near the shore,
Upon the deep perceives it not, and yet
'Tis there, but it is hidden by the depth.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-64">There is no light but comes from the serene
That never is o'ercast, nay, it is darkness
Or shadow of the flesh, or else its poison.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-67">Amply to thee is opened now the cavern
Which has concealed from thee the living justice
Of which thou mad'st such frequent questioning.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-70">For saidst thou: 'Born a man is on the shore
Of Indus, and is none who there can speak
Of Christ, nor who can read, nor who can write;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-73">And all his inclinations and his actions
Are good, so far as human reason sees,
Without a sin in life or in discourse:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-76">He dieth unbaptised and without faith;
Where is this justice that condemneth him?
Where is his fault, if he do not believe?'
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-79">Now who art thou, that on the bench wouldst sit
In judgment at a thousand miles away,
With the short vision of a single span?
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-82">Truly to him who with me subtilizes,
If so the Scripture were not over you,
For doubting there were marvellous occasion.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-85">O animals terrene, O stolid minds,
The primal will, that in itself is good,
Ne'er from itself, the Good Supreme, has moved.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-88">So much is just as is accordant with it;
No good created draws it to itself,
But it, by raying forth, occasions that."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-91">Even as above her nest goes circling round
The stork when she has fed her little ones,
And he who has been fed looks up at her,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-94">So lifted I my brows, and even such
Became the blessed image, which its wings
Was moving, by so many counsels urged.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-97">Circling around it sang, and said: "As are
My notes to thee, who dost not comprehend them,
Such is the eternal judgment to you mortals."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-100">Those lucent splendours of the Holy Spirit
Grew quiet then, but still within the standard
That made the Romans reverend to the world.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-103">It recommenced: "Unto this kingdom never
Ascended one who had not faith in Christ,
Before or since he to the tree was nailed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-106">But look thou, many crying are, 'Christ, Christ!'
Who at the judgment shall be far less near
To him than some shall be who knew not Christ.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-109">Such Christians shall the Ethiop condemn,
When the two companies shall be divided,
The one for ever rich, the other poor.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-112">What to your kings may not the Persians say,
When they that volume opened shall behold
In which are written down all their dispraises?
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-115">There shall be seen, among the deeds of Albert,
That which ere long shall set the pen in motion,
For which the realm of Prague shall be deserted.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-118">There shall be seen the woe that on the Seine
He brings by falsifying of the coin,
Who by the blow of a wild boar shall die.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-121">There shall be seen the pride that causes thirst,
Which makes the Scot and Englishman so mad
That they within their boundaries cannot rest;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-124">Be seen the luxury and effeminate life
Of him of Spain, and the Bohemian,
Who valour never knew and never wished;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-127">Be seen the Cripple of Jerusalem,
His goodness represented by an I,
While the reverse an M shall represent;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-130">Be seen the avarice and poltroonery
Of him who guards the Island of the Fire,
Wherein Anchises finished his long life;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-133">And to declare how pitiful he is
Shall be his record in contracted letters
Which shall make note of much in little space.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-136">And shall appear to each one the foul deeds
Of uncle and of brother who a nation
So famous have dishonoured, and two crowns.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-139">And he of Portugal and he of Norway
Shall there be known, and he of Rascia too,
Who saw in evil hour the coin of Venice.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-142">O happy Hungary, if she let herself
Be wronged no farther! and Navarre the happy,
If with the hills that gird her she be armed!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-145">And each one may believe that now, as hansel
Thereof, do Nicosia and Famagosta
Lament and rage because of their own beast,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xix-148">Who from the others' flank departeth not."
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XX

<p id="paradiso-xx-1">When he who all the world illuminates
Out of our hemisphere so far descends
That on all sides the daylight is consumed,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-4">The heaven, that erst by him alone was kindled,
Doth suddenly reveal itself again
By many lights, wherein is one resplendent.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-7">And came into my mind this act of heaven,
When the ensign of the world and of its leaders
Had silent in the blessed beak become;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-10">Because those living luminaries all,
By far more luminous, did songs begin
Lapsing and falling from my memory.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-13">O gentle Love, that with a smile dost cloak thee,
How ardent in those sparks didst thou appear,
That had the breath alone of holy thoughts!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-16">After the precious and pellucid crystals,
With which begemmed the sixth light I beheld,
Silence imposed on the angelic bells,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-19">I seemed to hear the murmuring of a river
That clear descendeth down from rock to rock,
Showing the affluence of its mountain-top.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-22">And as the sound upon the cithern's neck
Taketh its form, and as upon the vent
Of rustic pipe the wind that enters it,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-25">Even thus, relieved from the delay of waiting,
That murmuring of the eagle mounted up
Along its neck, as if it had been hollow.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-28">There it became a voice, and issued thence
From out its beak, in such a form of words
As the heart waited for wherein I wrote them.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-31">"The part in me which sees and bears the sun
In mortal eagles," it began to me,
"Now fixedly must needs be looked upon;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-34">For of the fires of which I make my figure,
Those whence the eye doth sparkle in my head
Of all their orders the supremest are.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-37">He who is shining in the midst as pupil
Was once the singer of the Holy Spirit,
Who bore the ark from city unto city;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-40">Now knoweth he the merit of his song,
In so far as effect of his own counsel,
By the reward which is commensurate.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-43">Of five, that make a circle for my brow,
He that approacheth nearest to my beak
Did the poor widow for her son console;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-46">Now knoweth he how dearly it doth cost
Not following Christ, by the experience
Of this sweet life and of its opposite.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-49">He who comes next in the circumference
Of which I speak, upon its highest arc,
Did death postpone by penitence sincere;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-52">Now knoweth he that the eternal judgment
Suffers no change, albeit worthy prayer
Maketh below to-morrow of to-day.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-55">The next who follows, with the laws and me,
Under the good intent that bore bad fruit
Became a Greek by ceding to the pastor;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-58">Now knoweth he how all the ill deduced
From his good action is not harmful to him,
Although the world thereby may be destroyed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-61">And he, whom in the downward arc thou seest,
Guglielmo was, whom the same land deplores
That weepeth Charles and Frederick yet alive;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-64">Now knoweth he how heaven enamoured is
With a just king; and in the outward show
Of his effulgence he reveals it still.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-67">Who would believe, down in the errant world,
That e'er the Trojan Ripheus in this round
Could be the fifth one of the holy lights?
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-70">Now knoweth he enough of what the world
Has not the power to see of grace divine,
Although his sight may not discern the bottom."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-73">Like as a lark that in the air expatiates,
First singing and then silent with content
Of the last sweetness that doth satisfy her,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-76">Such seemed to me the image of the imprint
Of the eternal pleasure, by whose will
Doth everything become the thing it is.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-79">And notwithstanding to my doubt I was
As glass is to the colour that invests it,
To wait the time in silence it endured not,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-82">But forth from out my mouth, "What things are these?"
Extorted with the force of its own weight;
Whereat I saw great joy of coruscation.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-85">Thereafterward with eye still more enkindled
The blessed standard made to me reply,
To keep me not in wonderment suspended:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-88">"I see that thou believest in these things
Because I say them, but thou seest not how;
So that, although believed in, they are hidden.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-91">Thou doest as he doth who a thing by name
Well apprehendeth, but its quiddity
Cannot perceive, unless another show it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-94">'Regnum coelorum' suffereth violence
From fervent love, and from that living hope
That overcometh the Divine volition;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-97">Not in the guise that man o'ercometh man,
But conquers it because it will be conquered,
And conquered conquers by benignity.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-100">The first life of the eyebrow and the fifth
Cause thee astonishment, because with them
Thou seest the region of the angels painted.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-103">They passed not from their bodies, as thou thinkest,
Gentiles, but Christians in the steadfast faith
Of feet that were to suffer and had suffered.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-106">For one from Hell, where no one e'er turns back
Unto good will, returned unto his bones,
And that of living hope was the reward,--
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-109">Of living hope, that placed its efficacy
In prayers to God made to resuscitate him,
So that 'twere possible to move his will.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-112">The glorious soul concerning which I speak,
Returning to the flesh, where brief its stay,
Believed in Him who had the power to aid it;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-115">And, in believing, kindled to such fire
Of genuine love, that at the second death
Worthy it was to come unto this joy.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-118">The other one, through grace, that from so deep
A fountain wells that never hath the eye
Of any creature reached its primal wave,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-121">Set all his love below on righteousness;
Wherefore from grace to grace did God unclose
His eye to our redemption yet to be,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-124">Whence he believed therein, and suffered not
From that day forth the stench of paganism,
And he reproved therefor the folk perverse.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-127">Those Maidens three, whom at the right-hand wheel
Thou didst behold, were unto him for baptism
More than a thousand years before baptizing.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-130">O thou predestination, how remote
Thy root is from the aspect of all those
Who the First Cause do not behold entire!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-133">And you, O mortals! hold yourselves restrained
In judging; for ourselves, who look on God,
We do not know as yet all the elect;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-136">And sweet to us is such a deprivation,
Because our good in this good is made perfect,
That whatsoe'er God wills, we also will."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-139">After this manner by that shape divine,
To make clear in me my short-sightedness,
Was given to me a pleasant medicine;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-142">And as good singer a good lutanist
Accompanies with vibrations of the chords,
Whereby more pleasantness the song acquires,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-145">So, while it spake, do I remember me
That I beheld both of those blessed lights,
Even as the winking of the eyes concords,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xx-148">Moving unto the words their little flames.
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXI

<p id="paradiso-xxi-1">Already on my Lady's face mine eyes
Again were fastened, and with these my mind,
And from all other purpose was withdrawn;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-4">And she smiled not; but "If I were to smile,"
She unto me began, "thou wouldst become
Like Semele, when she was turned to ashes.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-7">Because my beauty, that along the stairs
Of the eternal palace more enkindles,
As thou hast seen, the farther we ascend,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-10">If it were tempered not, is so resplendent
That all thy mortal power in its effulgence
Would seem a leaflet that the thunder crushes.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-13">We are uplifted to the seventh splendour,
That underneath the burning Lion's breast
Now radiates downward mingled with his power.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-16">Fix in direction of thine eyes the mind,
And make of them a mirror for the figure
That in this mirror shall appear to thee."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-19">He who could know what was the pasturage
My sight had in that blessed countenance,
When I transferred me to another care,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-22">Would recognize how grateful was to me
Obedience unto my celestial escort,
By counterpoising one side with the other.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-25">Within the crystal which, around the world
Revolving, bears the name of its dear leader,
Under whom every wickedness lay dead,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-28">Coloured like gold, on which the sunshine gleams,
A stairway I beheld to such a height
Uplifted, that mine eye pursued it not.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-31">Likewise beheld I down the steps descending
So many splendours, that I thought each light
That in the heaven appears was there diffused.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-34">And as accordant with their natural custom
The rooks together at the break of day
Bestir themselves to warm their feathers cold;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-37">Then some of them fly off without return,
Others come back to where they started from,
And others, wheeling round, still keep at home;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-40">Such fashion it appeared to me was there
Within the sparkling that together came,
As soon as on a certain step it struck,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-43">And that which nearest unto us remained
Became so clear, that in my thought I said,
"Well I perceive the love thou showest me;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-46">But she, from whom I wait the how and when
Of speech and silence, standeth still; whence I
Against desire do well if I ask not."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-49">She thereupon, who saw my silentness
In the sight of Him who seeth everything,
Said unto me, "Let loose thy warm desire."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-52">And I began: "No merit of my own
Renders me worthy of response from thee;
But for her sake who granteth me the asking,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-55">Thou blessed life that dost remain concealed
In thy beatitude, make known to me
The cause which draweth thee so near my side;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-58">And tell me why is silent in this wheel
The dulcet symphony of Paradise,
That through the rest below sounds so devoutly."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-61">"Thou hast thy hearing mortal as thy sight,"
It answer made to me; "they sing not here,
For the same cause that Beatrice has not smiled.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-64">Thus far adown the holy stairway's steps
Have I descended but to give thee welcome
With words, and with the light that mantles me;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-67">Nor did more love cause me to be more ready,
For love as much and more up there is burning,
As doth the flaming manifest to thee.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-70">But the high charity, that makes us servants
Prompt to the counsel which controls the world,
Allotteth here, even as thou dost observe."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-73">"I see full well," said I, "O sacred lamp!
How love unfettered in this court sufficeth
To follow the eternal Providence;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-76">But this is what seems hard for me to see,
Wherefore predestinate wast thou alone
Unto this office from among thy consorts."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-79">No sooner had I come to the last word,
Than of its middle made the light a centre,
Whirling itself about like a swift millstone.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-82">When answer made the love that was therein:
"On me directed is a light divine,
Piercing through this in which I am embosomed,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-85">Of which the virtue with my sight conjoined
Lifts me above myself so far, I see
The supreme essence from which this is drawn.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-88">Hence comes the joyfulness with which I flame,
For to my sight, as far as it is clear,
The clearness of the flame I equal make.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-91">But that soul in the heaven which is most pure,
That seraph which his eye on God most fixes,
Could this demand of thine not satisfy;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-94">Because so deeply sinks in the abyss
Of the eternal statute what thou askest,
From all created sight it is cut off.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-97">And to the mortal world, when thou returnest,
This carry back, that it may not presume
Longer tow'rd such a goal to move its feet.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-100">The mind, that shineth here, on earth doth smoke;
From this observe how can it do below
That which it cannot though the heaven assume it?"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-103">Such limit did its words prescribe to me,
The question I relinquished, and restricted
Myself to ask it humbly who it was.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-106">"Between two shores of Italy rise cliffs,
And not far distant from thy native place,
So high, the thunders far below them sound,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-109">And form a ridge that Catria is called,
'Neath which is consecrate a hermitage
Wont to be dedicate to worship only."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-112">Thus unto me the third speech recommenced,
And then, continuing, it said: "Therein
Unto God's service I became so steadfast,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-115">That feeding only on the juice of olives
Lightly I passed away the heats and frosts,
Contented in my thoughts contemplative.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-118">That cloister used to render to these heavens
Abundantly, and now is empty grown,
So that perforce it soon must be revealed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-121">I in that place was Peter Damiano;
And Peter the Sinner was I in the house
Of Our Lady on the Adriatic shore.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-124">Little of mortal life remained to me,
When I was called and dragged forth to the hat
Which shifteth evermore from bad to worse.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-127">Came Cephas, and the mighty Vessel came
Of the Holy Spirit, meagre and barefooted,
Taking the food of any hostelry.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-130">Now some one to support them on each side
The modern shepherds need, and some to lead them,
So heavy are they, and to hold their trains.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-133">They cover up their palfreys with their cloaks,
So that two beasts go underneath one skin;
O Patience, that dost tolerate so much!"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-136">At this voice saw I many little flames
From step to step descending and revolving,
And every revolution made them fairer.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-139">Round about this one came they and stood still,
And a cry uttered of so loud a sound,
It here could find no parallel, nor I
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxi-142">Distinguished it, the thunder so o'ercame me.
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXII

<p id="paradiso-xxii-1">Oppressed with stupor, I unto my guide
Turned like a little child who always runs
For refuge there where he confideth most;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-4">And she, even as a mother who straightway
Gives comfort to her pale and breathless boy
With voice whose wont it is to reassure him,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-7">Said to me: "Knowest thou not thou art in heaven,
And knowest thou not that heaven is holy all
And what is done here cometh from good zeal?
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-10">After what wise the singing would have changed thee
And I by smiling, thou canst now imagine,
Since that the cry has startled thee so much,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-13">In which if thou hadst understood its prayers
Already would be known to thee the vengeance
Which thou shalt look upon before thou diest.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-16">The sword above here smiteth not in haste
Nor tardily, howe'er it seem to him
Who fearing or desiring waits for it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-19">But turn thee round towards the others now,
For very illustrious spirits shalt thou see,
If thou thy sight directest as I say."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-22">As it seemed good to her mine eyes I turned,
And saw a hundred spherules that together
With mutual rays each other more embellished.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-25">I stood as one who in himself represses
The point of his desire, and ventures not
To question, he so feareth the too much.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-28">And now the largest and most luculent
Among those pearls came forward, that it might
Make my desire concerning it content.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-31">Within it then I heard: "If thou couldst see
Even as myself the charity that burns
Among us, thy conceits would be expressed;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-34">But, that by waiting thou mayst not come late
To the high end, I will make answer even
Unto the thought of which thou art so chary.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-37">That mountain on whose slope Cassino stands
Was frequented of old upon its summit
By a deluded folk and ill-disposed;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-40">And I am he who first up thither bore
The name of Him who brought upon the earth
The truth that so much sublimateth us.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-43">And such abundant grace upon me shone
That all the neighbouring towns I drew away
From the impious worship that seduced the world.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-46">These other fires, each one of them, were men
Contemplative, enkindled by that heat
Which maketh holy flowers and fruits spring up.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-49">Here is Macarius, here is Romualdus,
Here are my brethren, who within the cloisters
Their footsteps stayed and kept a steadfast heart."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-52">And I to him: "The affection which thou showest
Speaking with me, and the good countenance
Which I behold and note in all your ardours,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-55">In me have so my confidence dilated
As the sun doth the rose, when it becomes
As far unfolded as it hath the power.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-58">Therefore I pray, and thou assure me, father,
If I may so much grace receive, that I
May thee behold with countenance unveiled."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-61">He thereupon: "Brother, thy high desire
In the remotest sphere shall be fulfilled,
Where are fulfilled all others and my own.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-64">There perfect is, and ripened, and complete,
Every desire; within that one alone
Is every part where it has always been;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-67">For it is not in space, nor turns on poles,
And unto it our stairway reaches up,
Whence thus from out thy sight it steals away.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-70">Up to that height the Patriarch Jacob saw it
Extending its supernal part, what time
So thronged with angels it appeared to him.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-73">But to ascend it now no one uplifts
His feet from off the earth, and now my Rule
Below remaineth for mere waste of paper.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-76">The walls that used of old to be an Abbey
Are changed to dens of robbers, and the cowls
Are sacks filled full of miserable flour.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-79">But heavy usury is not taken up
So much against God's pleasure as that fruit
Which maketh so insane the heart of monks;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-82">For whatsoever hath the Church in keeping
Is for the folk that ask it in God's name,
Not for one's kindred or for something worse.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-85">The flesh of mortals is so very soft,
That good beginnings down below suffice not
From springing of the oak to bearing acorns.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-88">Peter began with neither gold nor silver,
And I with orison and abstinence,
And Francis with humility his convent.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-91">And if thou lookest at each one's beginning,
And then regardest whither he has run,
Thou shalt behold the white changed into brown.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-94">In verity the Jordan backward turned,
And the sea's fleeing, when God willed were more
A wonder to behold, than succour here."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-97">Thus unto me he said; and then withdrew
To his own band, and the band closed together;
Then like a whirlwind all was upward rapt.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-100">The gentle Lady urged me on behind them
Up o'er that stairway by a single sign,
So did her virtue overcome my nature;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-103">Nor here below, where one goes up and down
By natural law, was motion e'er so swift
That it could be compared unto my wing.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-106">Reader, as I may unto that devout
Triumph return, on whose account I often
For my transgressions weep and beat my breast,--
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-109">Thou hadst not thrust thy finger in the fire
And drawn it out again, before I saw
The sign that follows Taurus, and was in it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-112">O glorious stars, O light impregnated
With mighty virtue, from which I acknowledge
All of my genius, whatsoe'er it be,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-115">With you was born, and hid himself with you,
He who is father of all mortal life,
When first I tasted of the Tuscan air;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-118">And then when grace was freely given to me
To enter the high wheel which turns you round,
Your region was allotted unto me.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-121">To you devoutly at this hour my soul
Is sighing, that it virtue may acquire
For the stern pass that draws it to itself.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-124">"Thou art so near unto the last salvation,"
Thus Beatrice began, "thou oughtest now
To have thine eves unclouded and acute;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-127">And therefore, ere thou enter farther in,
Look down once more, and see how vast a world
Thou hast already put beneath thy feet;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-130">So that thy heart, as jocund as it may,
Present itself to the triumphant throng
That comes rejoicing through this rounded ether."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-133">I with my sight returned through one and all
The sevenfold spheres, and I beheld this globe
Such that I smiled at its ignoble semblance;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-136">And that opinion I approve as best
Which doth account it least; and he who thinks
Of something else may truly be called just.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-139">I saw the daughter of Latona shining
Without that shadow, which to me was cause
That once I had believed her rare and dense.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-142">The aspect of thy son, Hyperion,
Here I sustained, and saw how move themselves
Around and near him Maia and Dione.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-145">Thence there appeared the temperateness of Jove
'Twixt son and father, and to me was clear
The change that of their whereabout they make;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-148">And all the seven made manifest to me
How great they are, and eke how swift they are,
And how they are in distant habitations.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-151">The threshing-floor that maketh us so proud,
To me revolving with the eternal Twins,
Was all apparent made from hill to harbour!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxii-154">Then to the beauteous eyes mine eyes I turned.
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXIII

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-1">Even as a bird, 'mid the beloved leaves,
Quiet upon the nest of her sweet brood
Throughout the night, that hideth all things from us,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-4">Who, that she may behold their longed-for looks
And find the food wherewith to nourish them,
In which, to her, grave labours grateful are,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-7">Anticipates the time on open spray
And with an ardent longing waits the sun,
Gazing intent as soon as breaks the dawn:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-10">Even thus my Lady standing was, erect
And vigilant, turned round towards the zone
Underneath which the sun displays less haste;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-13">So that beholding her distraught and wistful,
Such I became as he is who desiring
For something yearns, and hoping is appeased.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-16">But brief the space from one When to the other;
Of my awaiting, say I, and the seeing
The welkin grow resplendent more and more.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-19">And Beatrice exclaimed: "Behold the hosts
Of Christ's triumphal march, and all the fruit
Harvested by the rolling of these spheres!"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-22">It seemed to me her face was all aflame;
And eyes she had so full of ecstasy
That I must needs pass on without describing.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-25">As when in nights serene of the full moon
Smiles Trivia among the nymphs eternal
Who paint the firmament through all its gulfs,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-28">Saw I, above the myriads of lamps,
A Sun that one and all of them enkindled,
E'en as our own doth the supernal sights,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-31">And through the living light transparent shone
The lucent substance so intensely clear
Into my sight, that I sustained it not.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-34">O Beatrice, thou gentle guide and dear!
To me she said: "What overmasters thee
A virtue is from which naught shields itself.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-37">There are the wisdom and the omnipotence
That oped the thoroughfares 'twixt heaven and earth,
For which there erst had been so long a yearning."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-40">As fire from out a cloud unlocks itself,
Dilating so it finds not room therein,
And down, against its nature, falls to earth,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-43">So did my mind, among those aliments
Becoming larger, issue from itself,
And that which it became cannot remember.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-46">"Open thine eyes, and look at what I am:
Thou hast beheld such things, that strong enough
Hast thou become to tolerate my smile."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-49">I was as one who still retains the feeling
Of a forgotten vision, and endeavours
In vain to bring it back into his mind,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-52">When I this invitation heard, deserving
Of so much gratitude, it never fades
Out of the book that chronicles the past.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-55">If at this moment sounded all the tongues
That Polyhymnia and her sisters made
Most lubrical with their delicious milk,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-58">To aid me, to a thousandth of the truth
It would not reach, singing the holy smile
And how the holy aspect it illumed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-61">And therefore, representing Paradise,
The sacred poem must perforce leap over,
Even as a man who finds his way cut off;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-64">But whoso thinketh of the ponderous theme,
And of the mortal shoulder laden with it,
Should blame it not, if under this it tremble.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-67">It is no passage for a little boat
This which goes cleaving the audacious prow,
Nor for a pilot who would spare himself.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-70">"Why doth my face so much enamour thee,
That to the garden fair thou turnest not,
Which under the rays of Christ is blossoming?
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-73">There is the Rose in which the Word Divine
Became incarnate; there the lilies are
By whose perfume the good way was discovered."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-76">Thus Beatrice; and I, who to her counsels
Was wholly ready, once again betook me
Unto the battle of the feeble brows.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-79">As in the sunshine, that unsullied streams
Through fractured cloud, ere now a meadow of flowers
Mine eyes with shadow covered o'er have seen,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-82">So troops of splendours manifold I saw
Illumined from above with burning rays,
Beholding not the source of the effulgence.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-85">O power benignant that dost so imprint them!
Thou didst exalt thyself to give more scope
There to mine eyes, that were not strong enough.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-88">The name of that fair flower I e'er invoke
Morning and evening utterly enthralled
My soul to gaze upon the greater fire.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-91">And when in both mine eyes depicted were
The glory and greatness of the living star
Which there excelleth, as it here excelled,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-94">Athwart the heavens a little torch descended
Formed in a circle like a coronal,
And cinctured it, and whirled itself about it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-97">Whatever melody most sweetly soundeth
On earth, and to itself most draws the soul,
Would seem a cloud that, rent asunder, thunders,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-100">Compared unto the sounding of that lyre
Wherewith was crowned the sapphire beautiful,
Which gives the clearest heaven its sapphire hue.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-103">"I am Angelic Love, that circle round
The joy sublime which breathes from out the womb
That was the hostelry of our Desire;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-106">And I shall circle, Lady of Heaven, while
Thou followest thy Son, and mak'st diviner
The sphere supreme, because thou enterest there."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-109">Thus did the circulated melody
Seal itself up; and all the other lights
Were making to resound the name of Mary.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-112">The regal mantle of the volumes all
Of that world, which most fervid is and living
With breath of God and with his works and ways,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-115">Extended over us its inner border,
So very distant, that the semblance of it
There where I was not yet appeared to me.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-118">Therefore mine eyes did not possess the power
Of following the incoronated flame,
Which mounted upward near to its own seed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-121">And as a little child, that towards its mother
Stretches its arms, when it the milk has taken,
Through impulse kindled into outward flame,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-124">Each of those gleams of whiteness upward reached
So with its summit, that the deep affection
They had for Mary was revealed to me.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-127">Thereafter they remained there in my sight,
'Regina coeli' singing with such sweetness,
That ne'er from me has the delight departed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-130">O, what exuberance is garnered up
Within those richest coffers, which had been
Good husbandmen for sowing here below!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-133">There they enjoy and live upon the treasure
Which was acquired while weeping in the exile
Of Babylon, wherein the gold was left.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-136">There triumpheth, beneath the exalted Son
Of God and Mary, in his victory,
Both with the ancient council and the new,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiii-139">He who doth keep the keys of such a glory.
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXIV

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-1">"O company elect to the great supper
Of the Lamb benedight, who feedeth you
So that for ever full is your desire,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-4">If by the grace of God this man foretaste
Something of that which falleth from your table,
Or ever death prescribe to him the time,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-7">Direct your mind to his immense desire,
And him somewhat bedew; ye drinking are
For ever at the fount whence comes his thought."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-10">Thus Beatrice; and those souls beatified
Transformed themselves to spheres on steadfast poles,
Flaming intensely in the guise of comets.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-13">And as the wheels in works of horologes
Revolve so that the first to the beholder
Motionless seems, and the last one to fly,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-16">So in like manner did those carols, dancing
In different measure, of their affluence
Give me the gauge, as they were swift or slow.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-19">From that one which I noted of most beauty
Beheld I issue forth a fire so happy
That none it left there of a greater brightness;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-22">And around Beatrice three several times
It whirled itself with so divine a song,
My fantasy repeats it not to me;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-25">Therefore the pen skips, and I write it not,
Since our imagination for such folds,
Much more our speech, is of a tint too glaring.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-28">"O holy sister mine, who us implorest
With such devotion, by thine ardent love
Thou dost unbind me from that beautiful sphere!"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-31">Thereafter, having stopped, the blessed fire
Unto my Lady did direct its breath,
Which spake in fashion as I here have said.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-34">And she: "O light eterne of the great man
To whom our Lord delivered up the keys
He carried down of this miraculous joy,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-37">This one examine on points light and grave,
As good beseemeth thee, about the Faith
By means of which thou on the sea didst walk.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-40">If he love well, and hope well, and believe,
From thee 'tis hid not; for thou hast thy sight
There where depicted everything is seen.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-43">But since this kingdom has made citizens
By means of the true Faith, to glorify it
'Tis well he have the chance to speak thereof."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-46">As baccalaureate arms himself, and speaks not
Until the master doth propose the question,
To argue it, and not to terminate it,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-49">So did I arm myself with every reason,
While she was speaking, that I might be ready
For such a questioner and such profession.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-52">"Say, thou good Christian; manifest thyself;
What is the Faith?"  Whereat I raised my brow
Unto that light wherefrom was this breathed forth.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-55">Then turned I round to Beatrice, and she
Prompt signals made to me that I should pour
The water forth from my internal fountain.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-58">"May grace, that suffers me to make confession,"
Began I, "to the great centurion,
Cause my conceptions all to be explicit!"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-61">And I continued: "As the truthful pen,
Father, of thy dear brother wrote of it,
Who put with thee Rome into the good way,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-64">Faith is the substance of the things we hope for,
And evidence of those that are not seen;
And this appears to me its quiddity."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-67">Then heard I: "Very rightly thou perceivest,
If well thou understandest why he placed it
With substances and then with evidences."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-70">And I thereafterward: "The things profound,
That here vouchsafe to me their apparition,
Unto all eyes below are so concealed,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-73">That they exist there only in belief,
Upon the which is founded the high hope,
And hence it takes the nature of a substance.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-76">And it behoveth us from this belief
To reason without having other sight,
And hence it has the nature of evidence."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-79">Then heard I: "If whatever is acquired
Below by doctrine were thus understood,
No sophist's subtlety would there find place."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-82">Thus was breathed forth from that enkindled love;
Then added: "Very well has been gone over
Already of this coin the alloy and weight;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-85">But tell me if thou hast it in thy purse?"
And I: "Yes, both so shining and so round
That in its stamp there is no peradventure."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-88">Thereafter issued from the light profound
That there resplendent was: "This precious jewel,
Upon the which is every virtue founded,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-91">Whence hadst thou it?"  And I: "The large outpouring
Of Holy Spirit, which has been diffused
Upon the ancient parchments and the new,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-94">A syllogism is, which proved it to me
With such acuteness, that, compared therewith,
All demonstration seems to me obtuse."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-97">And then I heard: "The ancient and the new
Postulates, that to thee are so conclusive,
Why dost thou take them for the word divine?"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-100">And I: "The proofs, which show the truth to me,
Are the works subsequent, whereunto Nature
Ne'er heated iron yet, nor anvil beat."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-103">'Twas answered me: "Say, who assureth thee
That those works ever were? the thing itself
That must be proved, nought else to thee affirms it."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-106">"Were the world to Christianity converted,"
I said, "withouten miracles, this one
Is such, the rest are not its hundredth part;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-109">Because that poor and fasting thou didst enter
Into the field to sow there the good plant,
Which was a vine and has become a thorn!"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-112">This being finished, the high, holy Court
Resounded through the spheres, "One God we praise!"
In melody that there above is chanted.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-115">And then that Baron, who from branch to branch,
Examining, had thus conducted me,
Till the extremest leaves we were approaching,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-118">Again began: "The Grace that dallying
Plays with thine intellect thy mouth has opened,
Up to this point, as it should opened be,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-121">So that I do approve what forth emerged;
But now thou must express what thou believest,
And whence to thy belief it was presented."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-124">"O holy father, spirit who beholdest
What thou believedst so that thou o'ercamest,
Towards the sepulchre, more youthful feet,"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-127">Began I, "thou dost wish me in this place
The form to manifest of my prompt belief,
And likewise thou the cause thereof demandest.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-130">And I respond: In one God I believe,
Sole and eterne, who moveth all the heavens
With love and with desire, himself unmoved;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-133">And of such faith not only have I proofs
Physical and metaphysical, but gives them
Likewise the truth that from this place rains down
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-136">Through Moses, through the Prophets and the Psalms,
Through the Evangel, and through you, who wrote
After the fiery Spirit sanctified you;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-139">In Persons three eterne believe, and these
One essence I believe, so one and trine
They bear conjunction both with 'sunt' and 'est.'
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-142">With the profound condition and divine
Which now I touch upon, doth stamp my mind
Ofttimes the doctrine evangelical.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-145">This the beginning is, this is the spark
Which afterwards dilates to vivid flame,
And, like a star in heaven, is sparkling in me."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-148">Even as a lord who hears what pleaseth him
His servant straight embraces, gratulating
For the good news as soon as he is silent;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-151">So, giving me its benediction, singing,
Three times encircled me, when I was silent,
The apostolic light, at whose command
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxiv-154">I spoken had, in speaking I so pleased him.
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXV

<p id="paradiso-xxv-1">If e'er it happen that the Poem Sacred,
To which both heaven and earth have set their hand,
So that it many a year hath made me lean,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-4">O'ercome the cruelty that bars me out
From the fair sheepfold, where a lamb I slumbered,
An enemy to the wolves that war upon it,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-7">With other voice forthwith, with other fleece
Poet will I return, and at my font
Baptismal will I take the laurel crown;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-10">Because into the Faith that maketh known
All souls to God there entered I, and then
Peter for her sake thus my brow encircled.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-13">Thereafterward towards us moved a light
Out of that band whence issued the first-fruits
Which of his vicars Christ behind him left,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-16">And then my Lady, full of ecstasy,
Said unto me: "Look, look! behold the Baron
For whom below Galicia is frequented."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-19">In the same way as, when a dove alights
Near his companion, both of them pour forth,
Circling about and murmuring, their affection,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-22">So one beheld I by the other grand
Prince glorified to be with welcome greeted,
Lauding the food that there above is eaten.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-25">But when their gratulations were complete,
Silently 'coram me' each one stood still,
So incandescent it o'ercame my sight.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-28">Smiling thereafterwards, said Beatrice:
"Illustrious life, by whom the benefactions
Of our Basilica have been described,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-31">Make Hope resound within this altitude;
Thou knowest as oft thou dost personify it
As Jesus to the three gave greater clearness."--
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-34">"Lift up thy head, and make thyself assured;
For what comes hither from the mortal world
Must needs be ripened in our radiance."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-37">This comfort came to me from the second fire;
Wherefore mine eyes I lifted to the hills,
Which bent them down before with too great weight.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-40">"Since, through his grace, our Emperor wills that thou
Shouldst find thee face to face, before thy death,
In the most secret chamber, with his Counts,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-43">So that, the truth beholden of this court,
Hope, which below there rightfully enamours,
Thereby thou strengthen in thyself and others,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-46">Say what it is, and how is flowering with it
Thy mind, and say from whence it came to thee."
Thus did the second light again continue.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-49">And the Compassionate, who piloted
The plumage of my wings in such high flight,
Did in reply anticipate me thus:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-52">"No child whatever the Church Militant
Of greater hope possesses, as is written
In that Sun which irradiates all our band;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-55">Therefore it is conceded him from Egypt
To come into Jerusalem to see,
Or ever yet his warfare be completed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-58">The two remaining points, that not for knowledge
Have been demanded, but that he report
How much this virtue unto thee is pleasing,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-61">To him I leave; for hard he will not find them,
Nor of self-praise; and let him answer them;
And may the grace of God in this assist him!"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-64">As a disciple, who his teacher follows,
Ready and willing, where he is expert,
That his proficiency may be displayed,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-67">"Hope," said I, "is the certain expectation
Of future glory, which is the effect
Of grace divine and merit precedent.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-70">From many stars this light comes unto me;
But he instilled it first into my heart
Who was chief singer unto the chief captain.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-73">'Sperent in te,' in the high Theody
He sayeth, 'those who know thy name;' and who
Knoweth it not, if he my faith possess?
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-76">Thou didst instil me, then, with his instilling
In the Epistle, so that I am full,
And upon others rain again your rain."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-79">While I was speaking, in the living bosom
Of that combustion quivered an effulgence,
Sudden and frequent, in the guise of lightning;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-82">Then breathed: "The love wherewith I am inflamed
Towards the virtue still which followed me
Unto the palm and issue of the field,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-85">Wills that I breathe to thee that thou delight
In her; and grateful to me is thy telling
Whatever things Hope promises to thee."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-88">And I: "The ancient Scriptures and the new
The mark establish, and this shows it me,
Of all the souls whom God hath made his friends.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-91">Isaiah saith, that each one garmented
In his own land shall be with twofold garments,
And his own land is this delightful life.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-94">Thy brother, too, far more explicitly,
There where he treateth of the robes of white,
This revelation manifests to us."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-97">And first, and near the ending of these words,
"Sperent in te" from over us was heard,
To which responsive answered all the carols.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-100">Thereafterward a light among them brightened,
So that, if Cancer one such crystal had,
Winter would have a month of one sole day.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-103">And as uprises, goes, and enters the dance
A winsome maiden, only to do honour
To the new bride, and not from any failing,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-106">Even thus did I behold the brightened splendour
Approach the two, who in a wheel revolved
As was beseeming to their ardent love.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-109">Into the song and music there it entered;
And fixed on them my Lady kept her look,
Even as a bride silent and motionless.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-112">"This is the one who lay upon the breast
Of him our Pelican; and this is he
To the great office from the cross elected."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-115">My Lady thus; but therefore none the more
Did move her sight from its attentive gaze
Before or afterward these words of hers.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-118">Even as a man who gazes, and endeavours
To see the eclipsing of the sun a little,
And who, by seeing, sightless doth become,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-121">So I became before that latest fire,
While it was said, "Why dost thou daze thyself
To see a thing which here hath no existence?
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-124">Earth in the earth my body is, and shall be
With all the others there, until our number
With the eternal proposition tallies.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-127">With the two garments in the blessed cloister
Are the two lights alone that have ascended:
And this shalt thou take back into your world."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-130">And at this utterance the flaming circle
Grew quiet, with the dulcet intermingling
Of sound that by the trinal breath was made,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-133">As to escape from danger or fatigue
The oars that erst were in the water beaten
Are all suspended at a whistle's sound.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-136">Ah, how much in my mind was I disturbed,
When I turned round to look on Beatrice,
That her I could not see, although I was
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxv-139">Close at her side and in the Happy World!
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXVI

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-1">While I was doubting for my vision quenched,
Out of the flame refulgent that had quenched it
Issued a breathing, that attentive made me,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-4">Saying: "While thou recoverest the sense
Of seeing which in me thou hast consumed,
'Tis well that speaking thou shouldst compensate it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-7">Begin then, and declare to what thy soul
Is aimed, and count it for a certainty,
Sight is in thee bewildered and not dead;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-10">Because the Lady, who through this divine
Region conducteth thee, has in her look
The power the hand of Ananias had."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-13">I said: "As pleaseth her, or soon or late
Let the cure come to eyes that portals were
When she with fire I ever burn with entered.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-16">The Good, that gives contentment to this Court,
The Alpha and Omega is of all
The writing that love reads me low or loud."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-19">The selfsame voice, that taken had from me
The terror of the sudden dazzlement,
To speak still farther put it in my thought;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-22">And said: "In verity with finer sieve
Behoveth thee to sift; thee it behoveth
To say who aimed thy bow at such a target."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-25">And I: "By philosophic arguments,
And by authority that hence descends,
Such love must needs imprint itself in me;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-28">For Good, so far as good, when comprehended
Doth straight enkindle love, and so much greater
As more of goodness in itself it holds;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-31">Then to that Essence (whose is such advantage
That every good which out of it is found
Is nothing but a ray of its own light)
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-34">More than elsewhither must the mind be moved
Of every one, in loving, who discerns
The truth in which this evidence is founded.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-37">Such truth he to my intellect reveals
Who demonstrates to me the primal love
Of all the sempiternal substances.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-40">The voice reveals it of the truthful Author,
Who says to Moses, speaking of Himself,
'I will make all my goodness pass before thee.'
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-43">Thou too revealest it to me, beginning
The loud Evangel, that proclaims the secret
Of heaven to earth above all other edict."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-46">And I heard say: "By human intellect
And by authority concordant with it,
Of all thy loves reserve for God the highest.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-49">But say again if other cords thou feelest,
Draw thee towards Him, that thou mayst proclaim
With how many teeth this love is biting thee."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-52">The holy purpose of the Eagle of Christ
Not latent was, nay, rather I perceived
Whither he fain would my profession lead.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-55">Therefore I recommenced: "All of those bites
Which have the power to turn the heart to God
Unto my charity have been concurrent.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-58">The being of the world, and my own being,
The death which He endured that I may live,
And that which all the faithful hope, as I do,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-61">With the forementioned vivid consciousness
Have drawn me from the sea of love perverse,
And of the right have placed me on the shore.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-64">The leaves, wherewith embowered is all the garden
Of the Eternal Gardener, do I love
As much as he has granted them of good."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-67">As soon as I had ceased, a song most sweet
Throughout the heaven resounded, and my Lady
Said with the others, "Holy, holy, holy!"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-70">And as at some keen light one wakes from sleep
By reason of the visual spirit that runs
Unto the splendour passed from coat to coat,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-73">And he who wakes abhorreth what he sees,
So all unconscious is his sudden waking,
Until the judgment cometh to his aid,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-76">So from before mine eyes did Beatrice
Chase every mote with radiance of her own,
That cast its light a thousand miles and more.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-79">Whence better after than before I saw,
And in a kind of wonderment I asked
About a fourth light that I saw with us.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-82">And said my Lady: "There within those rays
Gazes upon its Maker the first soul
That ever the first virtue did create."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-85">Even as the bough that downward bends its top
At transit of the wind, and then is lifted
By its own virtue, which inclines it upward,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-88">Likewise did I, the while that she was speaking,
Being amazed, and then I was made bold
By a desire to speak wherewith I burned.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-91">And I began: "O apple, that mature
Alone hast been produced, O ancient father,
To whom each wife is daughter and daughter-in-law,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-94">Devoutly as I can I supplicate thee
That thou wouldst speak to me; thou seest my wish;
And I, to hear thee quickly, speak it not."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-97">Sometimes an animal, when covered, struggles
So that his impulse needs must be apparent,
By reason of the wrappage following it;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-100">And in like manner the primeval soul
Made clear to me athwart its covering
How jubilant it was to give me pleasure.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-103">Then breathed: "Without thy uttering it to me,
Thine inclination better I discern
Than thou whatever thing is surest to thee;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-106">For I behold it in the truthful mirror,
That of Himself all things parhelion makes,
And none makes Him parhelion of itself.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-109">Thou fain wouldst hear how long ago God placed me
Within the lofty garden, where this Lady
Unto so long a stairway thee disposed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-112">And how long to mine eyes it was a pleasure,
And of the great disdain the proper cause,
And the language that I used and that I made.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-115">Now, son of mine, the tasting of the tree
Not in itself was cause of so great exile,
But solely the o'erstepping of the bounds.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-118">There, whence thy Lady moved Virgilius,
Four thousand and three hundred and two circuits
Made by the sun, this Council I desired;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-121">And him I saw return to all the lights
Of his highway nine hundred times and thirty,
Whilst I upon the earth was tarrying.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-124">The language that I spake was quite extinct
Before that in the work interminable
The people under Nimrod were employed;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-127">For nevermore result of reasoning
(Because of human pleasure that doth change,
Obedient to the heavens) was durable.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-130">A natural action is it that man speaks;
But whether thus or thus, doth nature leave
To your own art, as seemeth best to you.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-133">Ere I descended to the infernal anguish,
'El' was on earth the name of the Chief Good,
From whom comes all the joy that wraps me round
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-136">'Eli' he then was called, and that is proper,
Because the use of men is like a leaf
On bough, which goeth and another cometh.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-139">Upon the mount that highest o'er the wave
Rises was I, in life or pure or sinful,
From the first hour to that which is the second,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvi-142">As the sun changes quadrant, to the sixth."
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXVII

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-1">"Glory be to the Father, to the Son,
And Holy Ghost!" all Paradise began,
So that the melody inebriate made me.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-4">What I beheld seemed unto me a smile
Of the universe; for my inebriation
Found entrance through the hearing and the sight.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-7">O joy!  O gladness inexpressible!
O perfect life of love and peacefulness!
O riches without hankering secure!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-10">Before mine eyes were standing the four torches
Enkindled, and the one that first had come
Began to make itself more luminous;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-13">And even such in semblance it became
As Jupiter would become, if he and Mars
Were birds, and they should interchange their feathers.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-16">That Providence, which here distributeth
Season and service, in the blessed choir
Had silence upon every side imposed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-19">When I heard say: "If I my colour change,
Marvel not at it; for while I am speaking
Thou shalt behold all these their colour change.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-22">He who usurps upon the earth my place,
My place, my place, which vacant has become
Before the presence of the Son of God,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-25">Has of my cemetery made a sewer
Of blood and stench, whereby the Perverse One,
Who fell from here, below there is appeased!"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-28">With the same colour which, through sun adverse,
Painteth the clouds at evening or at morn,
Beheld I then the whole of heaven suffused.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-31">And as a modest woman, who abides
Sure of herself, and at another's failing,
From listening only, timorous becomes,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-34">Even thus did Beatrice change countenance;
And I believe in heaven was such eclipse,
When suffered the supreme Omnipotence;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-37">Thereafterward proceeded forth his words
With voice so much transmuted from itself,
The very countenance was not more changed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-40">"The spouse of Christ has never nurtured been
On blood of mine, of Linus and of Cletus,
To be made use of in acquest of gold;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-43">But in acquest of this delightful life
Sixtus and Pius, Urban and Calixtus,
After much lamentation, shed their blood.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-46">Our purpose was not, that on the right hand
Of our successors should in part be seated
The Christian folk, in part upon the other;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-49">Nor that the keys which were to me confided
Should e'er become the escutcheon on a banner,
That should wage war on those who are baptized;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-52">Nor I be made the figure of a seal
To privileges venal and mendacious,
Whereat I often redden and flash with fire.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-55">In garb of shepherds the rapacious wolves
Are seen from here above o'er all the pastures!
O wrath of God, why dost thou slumber still?
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-58">To drink our blood the Caorsines and Gascons
Are making ready.  O thou good beginning,
Unto how vile an end must thou needs fall!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-61">But the high Providence, that with Scipio
At Rome the glory of the world defended,
Will speedily bring aid, as I conceive;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-64">And thou, my son, who by thy mortal weight
Shalt down return again, open thy mouth;
What I conceal not, do not thou conceal."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-67">As with its frozen vapours downward falls
In flakes our atmosphere, what time the horn
Of the celestial Goat doth touch the sun,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-70">Upward in such array saw I the ether
Become, and flaked with the triumphant vapours,
Which there together with us had remained.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-73">My sight was following up their semblances,
And followed till the medium, by excess,
The passing farther onward took from it;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-76">Whereat the Lady, who beheld me freed
From gazing upward, said to me: "Cast down
Thy sight, and see how far thou art turned round."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-79">Since the first time that I had downward looked,
I saw that I had moved through the whole arc
Which the first climate makes from midst to end;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-82">So that I saw the mad track of Ulysses
Past Gades, and this side, well nigh the shore
Whereon became Europa a sweet burden.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-85">And of this threshing-floor the site to me
Were more unveiled, but the sun was proceeding
Under my feet, a sign and more removed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-88">My mind enamoured, which is dallying
At all times with my Lady, to bring back
To her mine eyes was more than ever ardent.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-91">And if or Art or Nature has made bait
To catch the eyes and so possess the mind,
In human flesh or in its portraiture,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-94">All joined together would appear as nought
To the divine delight which shone upon me
When to her smiling face I turned me round.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-97">The virtue that her look endowed me with
From the fair nest of Leda tore me forth,
And up into the swiftest heaven impelled me.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-100">Its parts exceeding full of life and lofty
Are all so uniform, I cannot say
Which Beatrice selected for my place.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-103">But she, who was aware of my desire,
Began, the while she smiled so joyously
That God seemed in her countenance to rejoice:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-106">"The nature of that motion, which keeps quiet
The centre and all the rest about it moves,
From hence begins as from its starting point.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-109">And in this heaven there is no other Where
Than in the Mind Divine, wherein is kindled
The love that turns it, and the power it rains.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-112">Within a circle light and love embrace it,
Even as this doth the others, and that precinct
He who encircles it alone controls.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-115">Its motion is not by another meted,
But all the others measured are by this,
As ten is by the half and by the fifth.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-118">And in what manner time in such a pot
May have its roots, and in the rest its leaves,
Now unto thee can manifest be made.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-121">O Covetousness, that mortals dost ingulf
Beneath thee so, that no one hath the power
Of drawing back his eyes from out thy waves!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-124">Full fairly blossoms in mankind the will;
But the uninterrupted rain converts
Into abortive wildings the true plums.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-127">Fidelity and innocence are found
Only in children; afterwards they both
Take flight or e'er the cheeks with down are covered.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-130">One, while he prattles still, observes the fasts,
Who, when his tongue is loosed, forthwith devours
Whatever food under whatever moon;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-133">Another, while he prattles, loves and listens
Unto his mother, who when speech is perfect
Forthwith desires to see her in her grave.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-136">Even thus is swarthy made the skin so white
In its first aspect of the daughter fair
Of him who brings the morn, and leaves the night.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-139">Thou, that it may not be a marvel to thee,
Think that on earth there is no one who governs;
Whence goes astray the human family.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-142">Ere January be unwintered wholly
By the centesimal on earth neglected,
Shall these supernal circles roar so loud
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-145">The tempest that has been so long awaited
Shall whirl the poops about where are the prows;
So that the fleet shall run its course direct,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxvii-148">And the true fruit shall follow on the flower."
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXVIII

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-1">After the truth against the present life
Of miserable mortals was unfolded
By her who doth imparadise my mind,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-4">As in a looking-glass a taper's flame
He sees who from behind is lighted by it,
Before he has it in his sight or thought,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-7">And turns him round to see if so the glass
Tell him the truth, and sees that it accords
Therewith as doth a music with its metre,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-10">In similar wise my memory recollecteth
That I did, looking into those fair eyes,
Of which Love made the springes to ensnare me.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-13">And as I turned me round, and mine were touched
By that which is apparent in that volume,
Whenever on its gyre we gaze intent,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-16">A point beheld I, that was raying out
Light so acute, the sight which it enkindles
Must close perforce before such great acuteness.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-19">And whatsoever star seems smallest here
Would seem to be a moon, if placed beside it.
As one star with another star is placed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-22">Perhaps at such a distance as appears
A halo cincturing the light that paints it,
When densest is the vapour that sustains it,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-25">Thus distant round the point a circle of fire
So swiftly whirled, that it would have surpassed
Whatever motion soonest girds the world;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-28">And this was by another circumcinct,
That by a third, the third then by a fourth,
By a fifth the fourth, and then by a sixth the fifth;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-31">The seventh followed thereupon in width
So ample now, that Juno's messenger
Entire would be too narrow to contain it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-34">Even so the eighth and ninth; and every one
More slowly moved, according as it was
In number distant farther from the first.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-37">And that one had its flame most crystalline
From which less distant was the stainless spark,
I think because more with its truth imbued.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-40">My Lady, who in my anxiety
Beheld me much perplexed, said: "From that point
Dependent is the heaven and nature all.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-43">Behold that circle most conjoined to it,
And know thou, that its motion is so swift
Through burning love whereby it is spurred on."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-46">And I to her: "If the world were arranged
In the order which I see in yonder wheels,
What's set before me would have satisfied me;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-49">But in the world of sense we can perceive
That evermore the circles are diviner
As they are from the centre more remote
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-52">Wherefore if my desire is to be ended
In this miraculous and angelic temple,
That has for confines only love and light,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-55">To hear behoves me still how the example
And the exemplar go not in one fashion,
Since for myself in vain I contemplate it."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-58">"If thine own fingers unto such a knot
Be insufficient, it is no great wonder,
So hard hath it become for want of trying."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-61">My Lady thus; then said she: "Do thou take
What I shall tell thee, if thou wouldst be sated,
And exercise on that thy subtlety.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-64">The circles corporal are wide and narrow
According to the more or less of virtue
Which is distributed through all their parts.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-67">The greater goodness works the greater weal,
The greater weal the greater body holds,
If perfect equally are all its parts.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-70">Therefore this one which sweeps along with it
The universe sublime, doth correspond
Unto the circle which most loves and knows.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-73">On which account, if thou unto the virtue
Apply thy measure, not to the appearance
Of substances that unto thee seem round,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-76">Thou wilt behold a marvellous agreement,
Of more to greater, and of less to smaller,
In every heaven, with its Intelligence."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-79">Even as remaineth splendid and serene
The hemisphere of air, when Boreas
Is blowing from that cheek where he is mildest,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-82">Because is purified and resolved the rack
That erst disturbed it, till the welkin laughs
With all the beauties of its pageantry;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-85">Thus did I likewise, after that my Lady
Had me provided with her clear response,
And like a star in heaven the truth was seen.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-88">And soon as to a stop her words had come,
Not otherwise does iron scintillate
When molten, than those circles scintillated.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-91">Their coruscation all the sparks repeated,
And they so many were, their number makes
More millions than the doubling of the chess.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-94">I heard them sing hosanna choir by choir
To the fixed point which holds them at the 'Ubi,'
And ever will, where they have ever been.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-97">And she, who saw the dubious meditations
Within my mind, "The primal circles," said,
"Have shown thee Seraphim and Cherubim.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-100">Thus rapidly they follow their own bonds,
To be as like the point as most they can,
And can as far as they are high in vision.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-103">Those other Loves, that round about them go,
Thrones of the countenance divine are called,
Because they terminate the primal Triad.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-106">And thou shouldst know that they all have delight
As much as their own vision penetrates
The Truth, in which all intellect finds rest.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-109">From this it may be seen how blessedness
Is founded in the faculty which sees,
And not in that which loves, and follows next;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-112">And of this seeing merit is the measure,
Which is brought forth by grace, and by good will;
Thus on from grade to grade doth it proceed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-115">The second Triad, which is germinating
In such wise in this sempiternal spring,
That no nocturnal Aries despoils,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-118">Perpetually hosanna warbles forth
With threefold melody, that sounds in three
Orders of joy, with which it is intrined.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-121">The three Divine are in this hierarchy,
First the Dominions, and the Virtues next;
And the third order is that of the Powers.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-124">Then in the dances twain penultimate
The Principalities and Archangels wheel;
The last is wholly of angelic sports.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-127">These orders upward all of them are gazing,
And downward so prevail, that unto God
They all attracted are and all attract.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-130">And Dionysius with so great desire
To contemplate these Orders set himself,
He named them and distinguished them as I do.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-133">But Gregory afterwards dissented from him;
Wherefore, as soon as he unclosed his eyes
Within this heaven, he at himself did smile.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-136">And if so much of secret truth a mortal
Proffered on earth, I would not have thee marvel,
For he who saw it here revealed it to him,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxviii-139">With much more of the truth about these circles."
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXIX

<p id="paradiso-xxix-1">At what time both the children of Latona,
Surmounted by the Ram and by the Scales,
Together make a zone of the horizon,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-4">As long as from the time the zenith holds them
In equipoise, till from that girdle both
Changing their hemisphere disturb the balance,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-7">So long, her face depicted with a smile,
Did Beatrice keep silence while she gazed
Fixedly at the point which had o'ercome me.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-10">Then she began: "I say, and I ask not
What thou dost wish to hear, for I have seen it
Where centres every When and every 'Ubi.'
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-13">Not to acquire some good unto himself,
Which is impossible, but that his splendour
In its resplendency may say, 'Subsisto,'
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-16">In his eternity outside of time,
Outside all other limits, as it pleased him,
Into new Loves the Eternal Love unfolded.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-19">Nor as if torpid did he lie before;
For neither after nor before proceeded
The going forth of God upon these waters.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-22">Matter and Form unmingled and conjoined
Came into being that had no defect,
E'en as three arrows from a three-stringed bow.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-25">And as in glass, in amber, or in crystal
A sunbeam flashes so, that from its coming
To its full being is no interval,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-28">So from its Lord did the triform effect
Ray forth into its being all together,
Without discrimination of beginning.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-31">Order was con-created and constructed
In substances, and summit of the world
Were those wherein the pure act was produced.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-34">Pure potentiality held the lowest part;
Midway bound potentiality with act
Such bond that it shall never be unbound.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-37">Jerome has written unto you of angels
Created a long lapse of centuries
Or ever yet the other world was made;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-40">But written is this truth in many places
By writers of the Holy Ghost, and thou
Shalt see it, if thou lookest well thereat.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-43">And even reason seeth it somewhat,
For it would not concede that for so long
Could be the motors without their perfection.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-46">Now dost thou know both where and when these Loves
Created were, and how; so that extinct
In thy desire already are three fires.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-49">Nor could one reach, in counting, unto twenty
So swiftly, as a portion of these angels
Disturbed the subject of your elements.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-52">The rest remained, and they began this art
Which thou discernest, with so great delight
That never from their circling do they cease.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-55">The occasion of the fall was the accursed
Presumption of that One, whom thou hast seen
By all the burden of the world constrained.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-58">Those whom thou here beholdest modest were
To recognise themselves as of that goodness
Which made them apt for so much understanding;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-61">On which account their vision was exalted
By the enlightening grace and their own merit,
So that they have a full and steadfast will.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-64">I would not have thee doubt, but certain be,
'Tis meritorious to receive this grace,
According as the affection opens to it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-67">Now round about in this consistory
Much mayst thou contemplate, if these my words
Be gathered up, without all further aid.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-70">But since upon the earth, throughout your schools,
They teach that such is the angelic nature
That it doth hear, and recollect, and will,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-73">More will I say, that thou mayst see unmixed
The truth that is confounded there below,
Equivocating in such like prelections.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-76">These substances, since in God's countenance
They jocund were, turned not away their sight
From that wherefrom not anything is hidden;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-79">Hence they have not their vision intercepted
By object new, and hence they do not need
