To recollect, through interrupted thought.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-82">So that below, not sleeping, people dream,
Believing they speak truth, and not believing;
And in the last is greater sin and shame.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-85">Below you do not journey by one path
Philosophising; so transporteth you
Love of appearance and the thought thereof.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-88">And even this above here is endured
With less disdain, than when is set aside
The Holy Writ, or when it is distorted.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-91">They think not there how much of blood it costs
To sow it in the world, and how he pleases
Who in humility keeps close to it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-94">Each striveth for appearance, and doth make
His own inventions; and these treated are
By preachers, and the Evangel holds its peace.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-97">One sayeth that the moon did backward turn,
In the Passion of Christ, and interpose herself
So that the sunlight reached not down below;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-100">And lies; for of its own accord the light
Hid itself; whence to Spaniards and to Indians,
As to the Jews, did such eclipse respond.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-103">Florence has not so many Lapi and Bindi
As fables such as these, that every year
Are shouted from the pulpit back and forth,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-106">In such wise that the lambs, who do not know,
Come back from pasture fed upon the wind,
And not to see the harm doth not excuse them.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-109">Christ did not to his first disciples say,
'Go forth, and to the world preach idle tales,'
But unto them a true foundation gave;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-112">And this so loudly sounded from their lips,
That, in the warfare to enkindle Faith,
They made of the Evangel shields and lances.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-115">Now men go forth with jests and drolleries
To preach, and if but well the people laugh,
The hood puffs out, and nothing more is asked.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-118">But in the cowl there nestles such a bird,
That, if the common people were to see it,
They would perceive what pardons they confide in,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-121">For which so great on earth has grown the folly,
That, without proof of any testimony,
To each indulgence they would flock together.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-124">By this Saint Anthony his pig doth fatten,
And many others, who are worse than pigs,
Paying in money without mark of coinage.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-127">But since we have digressed abundantly,
Turn back thine eyes forthwith to the right path,
So that the way be shortened with the time.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-130">This nature doth so multiply itself
In numbers, that there never yet was speech
Nor mortal fancy that can go so far.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-133">And if thou notest that which is revealed
By Daniel, thou wilt see that in his thousands
Number determinate is kept concealed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-136">The primal light, that all irradiates it,
By modes as many is received therein,
As are the splendours wherewith it is mated.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-139">Hence, inasmuch as on the act conceptive
The affection followeth, of love the sweetness
Therein diversely fervid is or tepid.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-142">The height behold now and the amplitude
Of the eternal power, since it hath made
Itself so many mirrors, where 'tis broken,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxix-145">One in itself remaining as before."
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXX

<p id="paradiso-xxx-1">Perchance six thousand miles remote from us
Is glowing the sixth hour, and now this world
Inclines its shadow almost to a level,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-4">When the mid-heaven begins to make itself
So deep to us, that here and there a star
Ceases to shine so far down as this depth,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-7">And as advances bright exceedingly
The handmaid of the sun, the heaven is closed
Light after light to the most beautiful;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-10">Not otherwise the Triumph, which for ever
Plays round about the point that vanquished me,
Seeming enclosed by what itself encloses,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-13">Little by little from my vision faded;
Whereat to turn mine eyes on Beatrice
My seeing nothing and my love constrained me.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-16">If what has hitherto been said of her
Were all concluded in a single praise,
Scant would it be to serve the present turn.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-19">Not only does the beauty I beheld
Transcend ourselves, but truly I believe
Its Maker only may enjoy it all.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-22">Vanquished do I confess me by this passage
More than by problem of his theme was ever
O'ercome the comic or the tragic poet;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-25">For as the sun the sight that trembles most,
Even so the memory of that sweet smile
My mind depriveth of its very self.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-28">From the first day that I beheld her face
In this life, to the moment of this look,
The sequence of my song has ne'er been severed;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-31">But now perforce this sequence must desist
From following her beauty with my verse,
As every artist at his uttermost.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-34">Such as I leave her to a greater fame
Than any of my trumpet, which is bringing
Its arduous matter to a final close,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-37">With voice and gesture of a perfect leader
She recommenced: "We from the greatest body
Have issued to the heaven that is pure light;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-40">Light intellectual replete with love,
Love of true good replete with ecstasy,
Ecstasy that transcendeth every sweetness.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-43">Here shalt thou see the one host and the other
Of Paradise, and one in the same aspects
Which at the final judgment thou shalt see."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-46">Even as a sudden lightning that disperses
The visual spirits, so that it deprives
The eye of impress from the strongest objects,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-49">Thus round about me flashed a living light,
And left me swathed around with such a veil
Of its effulgence, that I nothing saw.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-52">"Ever the Love which quieteth this heaven
Welcomes into itself with such salute,
To make the candle ready for its flame."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-55">No sooner had within me these brief words
An entrance found, than I perceived myself
To be uplifted over my own power,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-58">And I with vision new rekindled me,
Such that no light whatever is so pure
But that mine eyes were fortified against it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-61">And light I saw in fashion of a river
Fulvid with its effulgence, 'twixt two banks
Depicted with an admirable Spring.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-64">Out of this river issued living sparks,
And on all sides sank down into the flowers,
Like unto rubies that are set in gold;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-67">And then, as if inebriate with the odours,
They plunged again into the wondrous torrent,
And as one entered issued forth another.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-70">"The high desire, that now inflames and moves thee
To have intelligence of what thou seest,
Pleaseth me all the more, the more it swells.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-73">But of this water it behoves thee drink
Before so great a thirst in thee be slaked."
Thus said to me the sunshine of mine eyes;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-76">And added: "The river and the topazes
Going in and out, and the laughing of the herbage,
Are of their truth foreshadowing prefaces;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-79">Not that these things are difficult in themselves,
But the deficiency is on thy side,
For yet thou hast not vision so exalted."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-82">There is no babe that leaps so suddenly
With face towards the milk, if he awake
Much later than his usual custom is,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-85">As I did, that I might make better mirrors
Still of mine eyes, down stooping to the wave
Which flows that we therein be better made.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-88">And even as the penthouse of mine eyelids
Drank of it, it forthwith appeared to me
Out of its length to be transformed to round.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-91">Then as a folk who have been under masks
Seem other than before, if they divest
The semblance not their own they disappeared in,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-94">Thus into greater pomp were changed for me
The flowerets and the sparks, so that I saw
Both of the Courts of Heaven made manifest.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-97">O splendour of God! by means of which I saw
The lofty triumph of the realm veracious,
Give me the power to say how it I saw!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-100">There is a light above, which visible
Makes the Creator unto every creature,
Who only in beholding Him has peace,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-103">And it expands itself in circular form
To such extent, that its circumference
Would be too large a girdle for the sun.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-106">The semblance of it is all made of rays
Reflected from the top of Primal Motion,
Which takes therefrom vitality and power.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-109">And as a hill in water at its base
Mirrors itself, as if to see its beauty
When affluent most in verdure and in flowers,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-112">So, ranged aloft all round about the light,
Mirrored I saw in more ranks than a thousand
All who above there have from us returned.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-115">And if the lowest row collect within it
So great a light, how vast the amplitude
Is of this Rose in its extremest leaves!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-118">My vision in the vastness and the height
Lost not itself, but comprehended all
The quantity and quality of that gladness.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-121">There near and far nor add nor take away;
For there where God immediately doth govern,
The natural law in naught is relevant.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-124">Into the yellow of the Rose Eternal
That spreads, and multiplies, and breathes an odour
Of praise unto the ever-vernal Sun,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-127">As one who silent is and fain would speak,
Me Beatrice drew on, and said: "Behold
Of the white stoles how vast the convent is!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-130">Behold how vast the circuit of our city!
Behold our seats so filled to overflowing,
That here henceforward are few people wanting!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-133">On that great throne whereon thine eyes are fixed
For the crown's sake already placed upon it,
Before thou suppest at this wedding feast
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-136">Shall sit the soul (that is to be Augustus
On earth) of noble Henry, who shall come
To redress Italy ere she be ready.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-139">Blind covetousness, that casts its spell upon you,
Has made you like unto the little child,
Who dies of hunger and drives off the nurse.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-142">And in the sacred forum then shall be
A Prefect such, that openly or covert
On the same road he will not walk with him.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-145">But long of God he will not be endured
In holy office; he shall be thrust down
Where Simon Magus is for his deserts,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxx-148">And make him of Alagna lower go!"
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXXI

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-1">In fashion then as of a snow-white rose
Displayed itself to me the saintly host,
Whom Christ in his own blood had made his bride,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-4">But the other host, that flying sees and sings
The glory of Him who doth enamour it,
And the goodness that created it so noble,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-7">Even as a swarm of bees, that sinks in flowers
One moment, and the next returns again
To where its labour is to sweetness turned,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-10">Sank into the great flower, that is adorned
With leaves so many, and thence reascended
To where its love abideth evermore.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-13">Their faces had they all of living flame,
And wings of gold, and all the rest so white
No snow unto that limit doth attain.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-16">From bench to bench, into the flower descending,
They carried something of the peace and ardour
Which by the fanning of their flanks they won.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-19">Nor did the interposing 'twixt the flower
And what was o'er it of such plenitude
Of flying shapes impede the sight and splendour;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-22">Because the light divine so penetrates
The universe, according to its merit,
That naught can be an obstacle against it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-25">This realm secure and full of gladsomeness,
Crowded with ancient people and with modern,
Unto one mark had all its look and love.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-28">O Trinal Light, that in a single star
Sparkling upon their sight so satisfies them,
Look down upon our tempest here below!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-31">If the barbarians, coming from some region
That every day by Helice is covered,
Revolving with her son whom she delights in,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-34">Beholding Rome and all her noble works,
Were wonder-struck, what time the Lateran
Above all mortal things was eminent,--
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-37">I who to the divine had from the human,
From time unto eternity, had come,
From Florence to a people just and sane,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-40">With what amazement must I have been filled!
Truly between this and the joy, it was
My pleasure not to hear, and to be mute.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-43">And as a pilgrim who delighteth him
In gazing round the temple of his vow,
And hopes some day to retell how it was,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-46">So through the living light my way pursuing
Directed I mine eyes o'er all the ranks,
Now up, now down, and now all round about.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-49">Faces I saw of charity persuasive,
Embellished by His light and their own smile,
And attitudes adorned with every grace.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-52">The general form of Paradise already
My glance had comprehended as a whole,
In no part hitherto remaining fixed,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-55">And round I turned me with rekindled wish
My Lady to interrogate of things
Concerning which my mind was in suspense.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-58">One thing I meant, another answered me;
I thought I should see Beatrice, and saw
An Old Man habited like the glorious people.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-61">O'erflowing was he in his eyes and cheeks
With joy benign, in attitude of pity
As to a tender father is becoming.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-64">And "She, where is she?" instantly I said;
Whence he: "To put an end to thy desire,
Me Beatrice hath sent from mine own place.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-67">And if thou lookest up to the third round
Of the first rank, again shalt thou behold her
Upon the throne her merits have assigned her."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-70">Without reply I lifted up mine eyes,
And saw her, as she made herself a crown
Reflecting from herself the eternal rays.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-73">Not from that region which the highest thunders
Is any mortal eye so far removed,
In whatsoever sea it deepest sinks,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-76">As there from Beatrice my sight; but this
Was nothing unto me; because her image
Descended not to me by medium blurred.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-79">"O Lady, thou in whom my hope is strong,
And who for my salvation didst endure
In Hell to leave the imprint of thy feet,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-82">Of whatsoever things I have beheld,
As coming from thy power and from thy goodness
I recognise the virtue and the grace.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-85">Thou from a slave hast brought me unto freedom,
By all those ways, by all the expedients,
Whereby thou hadst the power of doing it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-88">Preserve towards me thy magnificence,
So that this soul of mine, which thou hast healed,
Pleasing to thee be loosened from the body."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-91">Thus I implored; and she, so far away,
Smiled, as it seemed, and looked once more at me;
Then unto the eternal fountain turned.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-94">And said the Old Man holy: "That thou mayst
Accomplish perfectly thy journeying,
Whereunto prayer and holy love have sent me,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-97">Fly with thine eyes all round about this garden;
For seeing it will discipline thy sight
Farther to mount along the ray divine.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-100">And she, the Queen of Heaven, for whom I burn
Wholly with love, will grant us every grace,
Because that I her faithful Bernard am."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-103">As he who peradventure from Croatia
Cometh to gaze at our Veronica,
Who through its ancient fame is never sated,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-106">But says in thought, the while it is displayed,
"My Lord, Christ Jesus, God of very God,
Now was your semblance made like unto this?"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-109">Even such was I while gazing at the living
Charity of the man, who in this world
By contemplation tasted of that peace.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-112">"Thou son of grace, this jocund life," began he,
"Will not be known to thee by keeping ever
Thine eyes below here on the lowest place;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-115">But mark the circles to the most remote,
Until thou shalt behold enthroned the Queen
To whom this realm is subject and devoted."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-118">I lifted up mine eyes, and as at morn
The oriental part of the horizon
Surpasses that wherein the sun goes down,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-121">Thus, as if going with mine eyes from vale
To mount, I saw a part in the remoteness
Surpass in splendour all the other front.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-124">And even as there where we await the pole
That Phaeton drove badly, blazes more
The light, and is on either side diminished,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-127">So likewise that pacific oriflamme
Gleamed brightest in the centre, and each side
In equal measure did the flame abate.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-130">And at that centre, with their wings expanded,
More than a thousand jubilant Angels saw I,
Each differing in effulgence and in kind.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-133">I saw there at their sports and at their songs
A beauty smiling, which the gladness was
Within the eyes of all the other saints;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-136">And if I had in speaking as much wealth
As in imagining, I should not dare
To attempt the smallest part of its delight.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-139">Bernard, as soon as he beheld mine eyes
Fixed and intent upon its fervid fervour,
His own with such affection turned to her
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxi-142">That it made mine more ardent to behold.
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXXII

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-1">Absorbed in his delight, that contemplator
Assumed the willing office of a teacher,
And gave beginning to these holy words:
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-4">"The wound that Mary closed up and anointed,
She at her feet who is so beautiful,
She is the one who opened it and pierced it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-7">Within that order which the third seats make
Is seated Rachel, lower than the other,
With Beatrice, in manner as thou seest.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-10">Sarah, Rebecca, Judith, and her who was
Ancestress of the Singer, who for dole
Of the misdeed said, 'Miserere mei,'
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-13">Canst thou behold from seat to seat descending
Down in gradation, as with each one's name
I through the Rose go down from leaf to leaf.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-16">And downward from the seventh row, even as
Above the same, succeed the Hebrew women,
Dividing all the tresses of the flower;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-19">Because, according to the view which Faith
In Christ had taken, these are the partition
By which the sacred stairways are divided.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-22">Upon this side, where perfect is the flower
With each one of its petals, seated are
Those who believed in Christ who was to come.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-25">Upon the other side, where intersected
With vacant spaces are the semicircles,
Are those who looked to Christ already come.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-28">And as, upon this side, the glorious seat
Of the Lady of Heaven, and the other seats
Below it, such a great division make,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-31">So opposite doth that of the great John,
Who, ever holy, desert and martyrdom
Endured, and afterwards two years in Hell.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-34">And under him thus to divide were chosen
Francis, and Benedict, and Augustine,
And down to us the rest from round to round.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-37">Behold now the high providence divine;
For one and other aspect of the Faith
In equal measure shall this garden fill.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-40">And know that downward from that rank which cleaves
Midway the sequence of the two divisions,
Not by their proper merit are they seated;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-43">But by another's under fixed conditions;
For these are spirits one and all assoiled
Before they any true election had.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-46">Well canst thou recognise it in their faces,
And also in their voices puerile,
If thou regard them well and hearken to them.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-49">Now doubtest thou, and doubting thou art silent;
But I will loosen for thee the strong bond
In which thy subtile fancies hold thee fast.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-52">Within the amplitude of this domain
No casual point can possibly find place,
No more than sadness can, or thirst, or hunger;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-55">For by eternal law has been established
Whatever thou beholdest, so that closely
The ring is fitted to the finger here.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-58">And therefore are these people, festinate
Unto true life, not 'sine causa' here
More and less excellent among themselves.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-61">The King, by means of whom this realm reposes
In so great love and in so great delight
That no will ventureth to ask for more,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-64">In his own joyous aspect every mind
Creating, at his pleasure dowers with grace
Diversely; and let here the effect suffice.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-67">And this is clearly and expressly noted
For you in Holy Scripture, in those twins
Who in their mother had their anger roused.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-70">According to the colour of the hair,
Therefore, with such a grace the light supreme
Consenteth that they worthily be crowned.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-73">Without, then, any merit of their deeds,
Stationed are they in different gradations,
Differing only in their first acuteness.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-76">'Tis true that in the early centuries,
With innocence, to work out their salvation
Sufficient was the faith of parents only.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-79">After the earlier ages were completed,
Behoved it that the males by circumcision
Unto their innocent wings should virtue add;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-82">But after that the time of grace had come
Without the baptism absolute of Christ,
Such innocence below there was retained.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-85">Look now into the face that unto Christ
Hath most resemblance; for its brightness only
Is able to prepare thee to see Christ."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-88">On her did I behold so great a gladness
Rain down, borne onward in the holy minds
Created through that altitude to fly,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-91">That whatsoever I had seen before
Did not suspend me in such admiration,
Nor show me such similitude of God.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-94">And the same Love that first descended there,
"Ave Maria, gratia plena," singing,
In front of her his wings expanded wide.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-97">Unto the canticle divine responded
From every part the court beatified,
So that each sight became serener for it.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-100">"O holy father, who for me endurest
To be below here, leaving the sweet place
In which thou sittest by eternal lot,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-103">Who is the Angel that with so much joy
Into the eyes is looking of our Queen,
Enamoured so that he seems made of fire?"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-106">Thus I again recourse had to the teaching
Of that one who delighted him in Mary
As doth the star of morning in the sun.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-109">And he to me: "Such gallantry and grace
As there can be in Angel and in soul,
All is in him; and thus we fain would have it;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-112">Because he is the one who bore the palm
Down unto Mary, when the Son of God
To take our burden on himself decreed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-115">But now come onward with thine eyes, as I
Speaking shall go, and note the great patricians
Of this most just and merciful of empires.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-118">Those two that sit above there most enrapture
As being very near unto Augusta,
Are as it were the two roots of this Rose.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-121">He who upon the left is near her placed
The father is, by whose audacious taste
The human species so much bitter tastes.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-124">Upon the right thou seest that ancient father
Of Holy Church, into whose keeping Christ
The keys committed of this lovely flower.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-127">And he who all the evil days beheld,
Before his death, of her the beauteous bride
Who with the spear and with the nails was won,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-130">Beside him sits, and by the other rests
That leader under whom on manna lived
The people ingrate, fickle, and stiff-necked.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-133">Opposite Peter seest thou Anna seated,
So well content to look upon her daughter,
Her eyes she moves not while she sings Hosanna.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-136">And opposite the eldest household father
Lucia sits, she who thy Lady moved
When to rush downward thou didst bend thy brows.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-139">But since the moments of thy vision fly,
Here will we make full stop, as a good tailor
Who makes the gown according to his cloth,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-142">And unto the first Love will turn our eyes,
That looking upon Him thou penetrate
As far as possible through his effulgence.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-145">Truly, lest peradventure thou recede,
Moving thy wings believing to advance,
By prayer behoves it that grace be obtained;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-148">Grace from that one who has the power to aid thee;
And thou shalt follow me with thy affection
That from my words thy heart turn not aside."
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxii-151">And he began this holy orison.
</p>

## Paradiso - Canto XXXIII

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-1">"Thou Virgin Mother, daughter of thy Son,
Humble and high beyond all other creature,
The limit fixed of the eternal counsel,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-4">Thou art the one who such nobility
To human nature gave, that its Creator
Did not disdain to make himself its creature.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-7">Within thy womb rekindled was the love,
By heat of which in the eternal peace
After such wise this flower has germinated.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-10">Here unto us thou art a noonday torch
Of charity, and below there among mortals
Thou art the living fountain-head of hope.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-13">Lady, thou art so great, and so prevailing,
That he who wishes grace, nor runs to thee,
His aspirations without wings would fly.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-16">Not only thy benignity gives succour
To him who asketh it, but oftentimes
Forerunneth of its own accord the asking.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-19">In thee compassion is, in thee is pity,
In thee magnificence; in thee unites
Whate'er of goodness is in any creature.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-22">Now doth this man, who from the lowest depth
Of the universe as far as here has seen
One after one the spiritual lives,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-25">Supplicate thee through grace for so much power
That with his eyes he may uplift himself
Higher towards the uttermost salvation.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-28">And I, who never burned for my own seeing
More than I do for his, all of my prayers
Proffer to thee, and pray they come not short,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-31">That thou wouldst scatter from him every cloud
Of his mortality so with thy prayers,
That the Chief Pleasure be to him displayed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-34">Still farther do I pray thee, Queen, who canst
Whate'er thou wilt, that sound thou mayst preserve
After so great a vision his affections.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-37">Let thy protection conquer human movements;
See Beatrice and all the blessed ones
My prayers to second clasp their hands to thee!"
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-40">The eyes beloved and revered of God,
Fastened upon the speaker, showed to us
How grateful unto her are prayers devout;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-43">Then unto the Eternal Light they turned,
On which it is not credible could be
By any creature bent an eye so clear.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-46">And I, who to the end of all desires
Was now approaching, even as I ought
The ardour of desire within me ended.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-49">Bernard was beckoning unto me, and smiling,
That I should upward look; but I already
Was of my own accord such as he wished;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-52">Because my sight, becoming purified,
Was entering more and more into the ray
Of the High Light which of itself is true.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-55">From that time forward what I saw was greater
Than our discourse, that to such vision yields,
And yields the memory unto such excess.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-58">Even as he is who seeth in a dream,
And after dreaming the imprinted passion
Remains, and to his mind the rest returns not,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-61">Even such am I, for almost utterly
Ceases my vision, and distilleth yet
Within my heart the sweetness born of it;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-64">Even thus the snow is in the sun unsealed,
Even thus upon the wind in the light leaves
Were the soothsayings of the Sibyl lost.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-67">O Light Supreme, that dost so far uplift thee
From the conceits of mortals, to my mind
Of what thou didst appear re-lend a little,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-70">And make my tongue of so great puissance,
That but a single sparkle of thy glory
It may bequeath unto the future people;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-73">For by returning to my memory somewhat,
And by a little sounding in these verses,
More of thy victory shall be conceived!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-76">I think the keenness of the living ray
Which I endured would have bewildered me,
If but mine eyes had been averted from it;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-79">And I remember that I was more bold
On this account to bear, so that I joined
My aspect with the Glory Infinite.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-82">O grace abundant, by which I presumed
To fix my sight upon the Light Eternal,
So that the seeing I consumed therein!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-85">I saw that in its depth far down is lying
Bound up with love together in one volume,
What through the universe in leaves is scattered;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-88">Substance, and accident, and their operations,
All interfused together in such wise
That what I speak of is one simple light.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-91">The universal fashion of this knot
Methinks I saw, since more abundantly
In saying this I feel that I rejoice.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-94">One moment is more lethargy to me,
Than five and twenty centuries to the emprise
That startled Neptune with the shade of Argo!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-97">My mind in this wise wholly in suspense,
Steadfast, immovable, attentive gazed,
And evermore with gazing grew enkindled.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-100">In presence of that light one such becomes,
That to withdraw therefrom for other prospect
It is impossible he e'er consent;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-103">Because the good, which object is of will,
Is gathered all in this, and out of it
That is defective which is perfect there.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-106">Shorter henceforward will my language fall
Of what I yet remember, than an infant's
Who still his tongue doth moisten at the breast.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-109">Not because more than one unmingled semblance
Was in the living light on which I looked,
For it is always what it was before;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-112">But through the sight, that fortified itself
In me by looking, one appearance only
To me was ever changing as I changed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-115">Within the deep and luminous subsistence
Of the High Light appeared to me three circles,
Of threefold colour and of one dimension,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-118">And by the second seemed the first reflected
As Iris is by Iris, and the third
Seemed fire that equally from both is breathed.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-121">O how all speech is feeble and falls short
Of my conceit, and this to what I saw
Is such, 'tis not enough to call it little!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-124">O Light Eterne, sole in thyself that dwellest,
Sole knowest thyself, and, known unto thyself
And knowing, lovest and smilest on thyself!
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-127">That circulation, which being thus conceived
Appeared in thee as a reflected light,
When somewhat contemplated by mine eyes,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-130">Within itself, of its own very colour
Seemed to me painted with our effigy,
Wherefore my sight was all absorbed therein.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-133">As the geometrician, who endeavours
To square the circle, and discovers not,
By taking thought, the principle he wants,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-136">Even such was I at that new apparition;
I wished to see how the image to the circle
Conformed itself, and how it there finds place;
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-139">But my own wings were not enough for this,
Had it not been that then my mind there smote
A flash of lightning, wherein came its wish.
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-142">Here vigour failed the lofty fantasy:
But now was turning my desire and will,
Even as a wheel that equally is moved,
</p>

<p id="paradiso-xxxiii-145">The Love which moves the sun and the other stars.
</p>
